The Red Page
SONNETS TO THE SPIRIT OF
BEAUTY.
.
Long, long ego, when all the glittering earth
Was Heaven itself, when drunkards in the
street
Were like mazed kings shaking at giving
birth
To acts of war that sickle men like wheat,
When the white clover opened Paradise
And God lived in a cottage up the brook,
Beauty, you lifted up my sleeping eyes
And filled my heart with longing ivitli a
look;
And all the day I searched but could not
find
The beautiful dark-eyed who touched me
there ,
Delight in her made trouble in my mind,
She was ivithin all Nature, everywhere,
The breath I breathed, the brook, the
flower, the grass,
Were her, her word, her beauty, all she
was ....
These myriad days, these many thousand
hours,
A. man’s long life, so choked with dusty
things,
How little perfect poise with perfect powers,
Joy at the heart and Beauty at the springs.
One hour, or two, or three, in long years
scattered,
Sparks from a smithy that have fired a
thatch,
Are all'that life has given and all that
mattered,
The rest, all heaving at a moveless latch.
For these, so many years of useless Toil,
Despair, endeavor, and again despair,
Sweat, that the base machine may have its
oil,
Idle delight to tempt one everywhere.
A life upon the cross. To make amends
Three flaming memories that the deathbed
ends.
There, on the darkened deathbed, dies the
brain
That flared three several times in seventy
years;
It cannot lift the silly hand again,
Nor speak, nor sing, it neither sees nor
hears.
And muffled mourners put it in the ground
And then go home, and in the earth it lies,
Too dark for vision and too deep for sound,
The million cells that made a good man wise.
Yet for a few short years an influence stirs
A sense or wraith or essence of him dead,
Which makes insensate things its ministers
To those beloved, his spirit’s daily bread;
Then that, too, fades; in book or deed a
spark
Lingers, then that, too, fades; then all is
dark ....
It may be so with us, that in the dark,
When tee have done with Time and wander
Space,
Some meeting of the blind may strike a
spark,
And to Death’s empty mansion give a grace.
Jt may be, that the loosened soul may find
Some new delight of living without limbs,
Bodiless joy of flesh-untrammelled mind,
Peace like a sky where starlike spirit swims.
It maybe, that the million cells of sense,
Loosed from their seventy years’ adhesion,
pass
Each to some joy of changed experience,
Weight in the earth or glory in the grass;
It may be that we cedse; we cannot tell.
Even if we cease life is a miracle.
From “Good Friday and Other Poems,” by
John Masefield.
Jt
The value of John Masefield’s Good Fri-
day and Other Poems (Macmillan, New
York, jis. 6d. out here), lies in the other
poems, for the dramatic poem, Good Friday,
a brilliant stage presentation of the trial
and crucifixion of Christ, appeared in print
some years ago and was adequately appre-
ciated by this page. The series of 60 son-
nets that follow contain, as will be seen
from those quoted, sonnets that deserve a
permanent place in English literature; and
the argument is logically developed from the
first exquisite sonnet to the culminating one,
to be quoted later. By this sonnet series
John Masefield ranges himself in the van
of contemporary poets, for here he has
proved himself in a medium that would at
first sight seem strange to the author of The
Everlasting Mercy and The Widow in the
Bye Street. He has taken the Shakespear-
ean form of sonnet, and shows himself some- x
what careless in his control of metre, allow-
ing in more than one line six feet, and
throughout making dissyllables of words like
“fire” and “hour”; but these minor blem-
ishes do not mar the splendid sweep of his
imagery and the sonorousness of his rhythm.
. •
Lays and Tales of the Mines, by Arthur
Wilson (“Dairy”), published by George
Robinson, Melb., deals with “the heroic deeds
and noble sacrifices, the sore trials and long
sufferings of the workers of the mines,”
These stories are forcibly written, though
without any pretence of style. Many of
the sketches have appeared in the press.
Naturally, the author knows his subject,
and his sympathy for his mining _ mates is
everywhere apparent. The collection is an
object-lesson in fhe improvement of under-
ground conditions, due chiefly to the lesson
of the appalling'mining catastrophes that
have stained the record of mining in every
period and every country. And, in these
new days of heroism, it is well for Arthur
Wilson to remind us that in times of danger
the miner has always been a hero.
A Calendar for 1917, with pen-pictures
by Ethel Georgina Heiy (Gordon and
Gotch, Q.), is neatly got up and delight-
fully Australian in atmosphere.
America, and the Future
Roland G. Usher, who professes history in
a U.S. University and is the author of
Pan-Germanism, now, in a remarkable work,
The Challenge of the Future (Constable, per
Sydney Book Club), takes liis country on his
professorial knee and spanks all the non-
sense out of her. She has to get out or get
under. She has to abandon the Atlantic to
Great Britain, the Pacific to Japan, confine
her famous Monroe Doctrine to the countries
between her and the Panama Canal, and ally
herself to Great Britain if she is to survive
at all. Usher insists that it does not matter
a cent what ideas the U.S. has of Europe;
the U.S. must sit up and take notice of what
Europe thinks of her; for she is “the most
completely vulnerable country to-day of any
large Power in the world.” And the desire
of America to remain isolated from the world
is quite impossible of attainment. The per-
fectly-natural aim of America that she
should be left alone to develop herself at the
maximum speed, with the cheerful hope that
the collapse of Europe will enable her to
enrich herself with even greater profits than
in the past, is also shown to be utterly
impracticable. First, even if she could go
on progressing as in the past, she would,
ipso facto, make herself a menace to other
Powers, and that menace would be met by
force. And, second, owing to her economic
inferiority to the European Powers, her
trade is going to suffer so much in the
future that she will certainly become a
poorer country, compelled to put' up with an
inevitably lessening rate of profit. There
are two kinds of trade profit, that of trading
in world markets at world prices, and that of
economic control of markets in undeveloped
countries. The former allows only a small
profit; the latter results in such abnormal
profits that “the present war is being con-
fessedly fought to retain or to obtain con-
trol” of the.se markets. “Undoubtedly, the
result will be to limit Americans to those
markets where the competition is greatest
and the profits least, to exclude them from
those markets in which competition is less
and the profits greatest.... The U.S. can
grow indefinitely, but not at the past rate
nor with the present degree of prosperity.”
As soon as Great Britain (or Germany)
re-enter the trade with South America, the
trade of the U.S. disappears, purely owing to
economic forces.
The economic reason is that the U.S. has
not yet acquired equality with England and
France, nor with the industrial development
of Germany. “There are two classes of
nations, creditor and debtor countries. On
the one hand stand Great Britain, France,
Belgium and Holland, the creditor countries,
elaborately developed from the proceeds of
their own industry. On the other we see
Germany, Austria, Italy, Russia, the U.S.
and Japan, in which the same conditions
have been produced by means of borrowed
capital apd skill. (To these we must, of
course, acid Australia.) These are the debtor
countries, which still owe the capital invest-
ments to which their great growth is really
due Those only are creditor countries
whose total assets are- greater than their
liabilities, who are owed by other nations,
and their citizens, more than they must pay
to other nations and their citizens. The
U.S. is still a debtor country.” For Usher
shows that the recent big buying back of her
securities has not by any means cleared her
from debt. Though her exports exceed her
imports the U.S. is merely paying her debts
in extra exports. And the U.S. is industrially
inferior to the best-organised European
States. “Where the price is identical the
quality is not; where the quality is the same
the American price is usually higher... .The
finer and more elaborate products of Euro-
pean industries, which constitute a large pro-
portion of the commodities exchanged
between nations, we are not yet able to make
at all in competition with Europe....We
have not yet learned to compete in quality,
promptitude, accuracy and efficiency with the
great European nations.” And the U.S. is
not able to undertake direct trade with many
countries, because she is not able to utilise
sufficient quantities of the raw products
which those countries export, nor to ex-
change sufficient quantities of her own manu-
factured goods of the desired quality.
Americans will have to think internation-
ally, to think in terms of Europe, not. of
America. “The assumption that we are iso-
lated from Europe in any sense, that our
safety and prosperity rest primarily upon
factors existent in America and are pro-
tected by our territorial integrity, is a
fallacy of the most dangerous type.” It
depends solely upon the other (European)
fellow whether America is going to get it in
the neck—that is, if she persists in being
too proud to fight. “We shall retain only
what the other nations cannot take away
from us, what they do not value, what they
will voluntarily concede.... The real danger
lies in the determination of European
nations to create by the use of armed force
artificial conditions to their own advantage
in practically all the undeveloped countries
on the globe.” And if the other nations do
more business and receive more profit, the
U.S. will do less and receive less. And it is
well to remember that the abnormal growth
of the U.S. has been due to the protection of
the sea-Power, Great Britain. It .was Britain
that stood between Europe aiid America
when America could not protect herself.
“In reality America is economically de-
pendent upon Europe, can remain isolated
only if Europe allows hex*. European ambi-
tions and not American interests govern the
international situation, and so long as their
economic fabric is so much stronger and
better organised than ours, they may coerce
us by economic methods at will.” And if the
U..g. attempts by armament to become inde-
pendent of Europe, Europe can be depended
upon to deal with the U.S. as the Allies are
now dealing with Germany. But, as Usher
shows, American independence is inexpedi-
ent. The victors in this war are not in the
least likely to share their winnings—the
control of the most profitable markets —with
any outsider, unless that Power is strong-
enough to demand them. Thus Usher is led
to the conviction that it is absolutely neces-
sary for the U.S. to make a European
alliance. And the U.S. can come into the
European ‘Concert only on the European
terms. For, judged by international status,
the U.S. ranks below Belgium. And_ there
can be no “preposterous and impertinent”
notion of the U.S. joining the European
Powers in order to lead them. Europe is
more than able to look after herself, and the
world. So America’s only chance is an
alliance with Great Britain, in which the
benefits each party received would be
approximately balanced.
But the U.S. must withdraw from the
Pacific. Usher makes out a strong case.
America must sacrifice the ‘Philippines,
Hawaii, and her coaling stations to Japan,
not only because she is unable to hold
these places, but also because it is highly
inexpedient that she should try to hold them.
Japan does not want to colonise the Pacific
Coast of the U.S., since she could not hope
to stay there. “The Japanese would be lack-
ing in sanity to establish colonies 8000 miles
away when admirable opportunities already
exist on the Asiatic shores which are already
in her control. In Korea and Manchuria
will be the New Japan, and, if additional
territory is eventually needed....”
Among other inevitable sacrifices are an
American mercantile marine and most of the
Monroe Doctrine. The only sphere of influence
that belongs to the U.S. is the Gulf and the
country as far south as the Canal —though
Usher notes that so far the U.S. has held the
canal solely by permission of the sea-Power,
England. It will be the job of the
U.S. to police Mexico and Central America,
and that will need something more tha'n a
toy army. “Certainly Great Britain, France
and Germany will not much longer tolerate
our attempts to produce order in other ways.”
Usher even suggests that when the European
war ends, the Mexican * clean-up might
furnish useful employment for the left-over
armies. In conclusion, while Usher almost
despondently recognises the difficulties in the
way of any adequate preparedness in a
nation that is not yet a nation, he logically
pleads for a beginning on that tremendous
task of national defence. But he insists
that the very life of the U-S. depends upon
an immediate consummation of “the - indis-
pensable foreign alliance”—and all his argu-
ment shows that that ally must be Great
Britain.
A. H. A.
With approximately the speed of the new
British armored “Willies,” Fredk. Watson is
deliberately and ponderously traversing our
early history in his series of Historical
Records of Australia, of which the latest is
vol. VII. of the first series, published by the
Library Committee of the Commonwealth
Parliament. This heavily-armored volume of
900 pages consists of governor’s despatches
to and from England during the period Jan.,
1809-June, 1813. The despatches are chiefly
those of William Paterson. Paterson did
some good service in exploration; but other-
wise left little mark on the settlement. He
had his full share in the troubles caused by
the sale of rum; and he will perhaps be re-
membered chiefly because of his duel with
Macarthur. His administration of the
settlement at Port Dahymple, in Tasmania,
gave no evidences of efficiency. After the
disposition of Bligh, Paterson, solely by
virtue of his military seniority,_assumed the
government of the colony. “His influence,”
says Fredk. Watson, “was purely adminis-
trative He was purely a passive figure-
head, and the little mark that he has left
was made by his encouragement of explora-
tion and his enlargement of the knowledge
of natural history in the two colonies.” Since,
in pursuance of Fredk. Watson’s elaborate
plan, he finds it necessary to devote 900
pages of correspondence to this nonentity, it
must be evident that these volumes are not
history but merely the dull bones that some
historian of our future may possibly gal-
vanise into life.
“Henry Janies James” : An English edition
of my Double-Bed Dialogues has been pub-
lished by Nash, at 3s. Gd. For the English
reader, however, it bears a different title
and a different signature. In neither case
am I to blame. The London publisher pre-
ferred the less shocking title. Honeymoon
Dialogues, for his less sophisticated readers.
And, owing to the death of Henry James, he
asked me to dispense with my first name, in
case purchasers of the Dialogues might won-
der what high explosive had strafed that
great novelist’s famous style. Hence, for the
English public, lam James James. And
now my agents have sold the Swedish pub-
lishing* rights— So Babette will have to
learn Swedish.
Billzac
Tall or short, lie is always lean, slim,
tough as whipcord, bearing himself with
perfect assurance and a certain degree of
athletic grace. He has a quick glancing eye,
a sun-tinted skin, and a thin mouth, cigar-
ette-adorned. That mouth is the label of his
wit and readiness to laugh at the comedy he
finds or invents in all things. He prefers Ms
humor served either well-flavored or with a
whimsical twist; the too-obvious horse-play
style of wit does not generally appeal. Con-
ventional reverence is not his strong point,
evidenced by the fact of his huge enjoyment
of any joke concerning the Almighty and the
After-Life.
In temperament he approximates more
closely to the Celt than to the Anglo-Saxon.
He is impulsive, quick-witted, generous, and
beneath that careless and no-sentiment-about-
me exterior lies an innate love of music,
color, life and movement. His blood, as one
might expect from the mothering of the
warm South, is red and hot, and his sins
are those of commission. Quick and strong
are his affections, too, deep-rooted but well-
hidden —so well-hidden that many doubt their
existence. His generous use of slang and
“langwidge” is really due to this same dis-
like of showing his true feelings. “Die before
you appear sentimental or pedantic” is cer-
tainly one rule that governs the general
behavior of Billzac.
Friendship is Billzac’s forte. Of mates he
is the most true, loyal and generous. But he
is a man’s man —-in men’s society he finds
true relaxation and enjoyment. Lay the
unflattering unction to thy soul, O Woman
of the Southland! Sport, in which thou hast
no place, is in reality the darling idol of his
heart. He dearly loves woman’s companion-
ship—occasionally. The quality of his love
for his mate usually transcends in strength,
purity and loyalty his love for the woman he
admires. .
He is a born rebel, with a deep dislike to
any rigidity Of discipline and order. Still,
the rebel is deeply tinged with a fierce sense
of possession. Attack his own particular in-
stitution —be it •‘push,” profession, State or
country—and he will fight like a very devil
to vindicate its. honor, forgetful of his quon-
dam resistance to its authority.
Perhaps, however, the most outstanding
characteristic appertaining to Billzac is liis
utter democracy—his lack of reverence for
the powers that be. His superior officer in
any rank of life is only superior in so far as
his age, experience or greater advantage have
piloted him to a higher position. Billzac per-
sists in seeing the man very much like him-
self —behind the veneer of office, and, seeing,
refuses to be overawed or impressed by his
authority.
Lastly, let me complete my miniature im-
pression of Billzac by painting in a super-
vivid tone of sheer personal courage and
contempt of danger and physical suffering.
—Helen Burke.
Jt
He is tall and wiry and bronzed, and he
swears in between his slang. There is
laughter in his eyes—they suggest the sun-
light of his native land. He will cheekily
compare London with Yackandandah to
the advantage of Yackandandah. He pro-
claims his independence in his walk, and hav-
ing something to skite about he does so.
He tells his occasional enemy to go to Hades,
but his mortal foe —well, he sends him
there. There is no gush about Billzac. “Not
Bad” is his superlative. Religion lie takes
casually (he has a nodding acquaintance
with God, so to speak), but deep in his
heart is a reverence for things that are good
and true. That is why he is a Billzac. And
he will back a team or a horse or a dog
with his last coin because that is in him too.
J. D. C.
Philadelphia N. Robertson issues An Ansae
Budget and O.V. (Australasian Authors
Agency, Melb., Is.)- But shouldn’t the use
of “Anzac” in a title be the privilege only
of those who are themselves Anzacs? The
verses that the author enclosed in a Christ-
mas billy are rather silly, but doubtless would
be welcomed by the recipient. Among the
other verses, which are on the whole unpre-
tentious but sincere, there is only one that
is worth quoting :
I am so placid as I sit
In train or tram, and knit and knit;
I walk serenely down the street
And smile on all the friends I meet;
Within the house I give due heed
To every duty, each one's need,
And when it’s dark and lamps are hit,
I take my sock again, and knit.
Sometimes the newsboys hurry by
And then my needles seem to fly
Through purl and plain, rote after row ,
They flash, until the fire burns low—
I am so tranquil as 1 sit
Till bedtime conies, and knit and knit.
And when the house has grown quite still,
I lean out on my window-sill —
Lean out to the velvet night,
Gemmed with ail its points .of light,
And pray to God to see to it
That I keep sane enough to knit.
It seems curious that a poet who can reach
this poignant sincerity should nowhere else
in this little booklet equal this high emotion.

IMPORTANT
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such a§ cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rememberings.
When thou art old
And sit beside the fire,
Past dreams and thoughts will come to
thee
To stir long dead desire. ■
Then shall be fought again
Of battles long, long gone.
But thou shalt sharpen sorrow
On the good things left undone.
When thou shalt see
The candle flicker low,
Passing, afar in shadow,
Will a dim procession go—•
Friends who were friends;
Friends who were none.
But thought will sharpen sorrow
On the good things left undone.
When on the roof
Shalt hear th' insolent rain
(Clamorous as thy youth
That scattered chaff for grain),
Thou slnilt half-smile
Remembering dreams won.
But sorrow will be sharpened
For the good things left undone.
N.S.W.
MARY GILMORE.
Confessional.
The flowing waters sluice the shingle,
The white beach, shaped like a crescent
moon.
Stretches for westering miles to mingle
Scarping headland and crouching dune,
In the soft distance yellowed, mellowed
By the lifting vapors of afternoon.
Oh. wist o’ me, O woman of yearning.
Beckoning there from the outland bar.
The lore you taught I was slow in learning,
Though Love, perhaps, is. as seabirds are,
Touched with wildness- —the more my debt,
then,
I who was fretful that small things jar.
I as a worm have revelled in mire,
Grappled ’ the sun and laughed that it
burned;
Have made debauch with draggled Desire,
And still to the thing that I scorned
returned;
Held Life a vassal, a whimsy plaything.
This you accepted, this you discerned.
Nor made rebuke, full understanding
That what has been had the need to be,
Since froward man to his own demanding
Brooks no withholding of ample fee,
Minting coin of his own red mintage,
Heedless of aught than his will’s decree.
Yet knew, beneath the brave veneering.
What wistful dreams of you there were,
Iu spite of my vagabond wayfaring,
Knew of the ferment working there,
And knew, at the last, they would lead me
safely
Home to your heart and the end of care.
Here to the music of waters lipping
The shingly beach in a soft sea rune.
With the seagulls planing, wheeling, dipping
Through the vapory splendors of afternoon,
Mellowing yet to a richer glory
Scarping headland and crouching dune,
Love, anhumbled and truly meting
How small the triumphs that father Pride,
I proffer my holiest dreams in greeting,
So I, through them, may be deified—
By your acceptance, or by your spurning,
Be outcast wholly, as you decide!
MAORILAND.
BERNARD INGLEEBY.
Woodhaugh.
Now hold me close a little trembling space,
While water-lilies bloom beneath the moon
And fragrant is the earth of dew-drenched
grace.
Steeped in a drowsy white and golden swoon,
Shaken of love —there is no faintest breeze
Upon the breast of the swift-lipped lagoon.
Ah, hold me close! The quick buds on the
trees
Tremble to life, their blooming time is near,
The surging creek runs singing to the seas.
The night a censer is, swung high and clear,
Flinging to Heav’n the incense of the earth,
The wedded fragrance of the fruitful year.
Ah, hold me close! Love clamors to have
birth.
Great golden stars lean from the skies
a-croon,
And water-lilies faint beneath the moon.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
The Seeker.
Truth lias a well; its water feeds
A shelving edge of tangled weeds;
And, on the brink, full many sit
Whose questing feet have muddied it.
There came a youth with face aflame;
It matters not his rank or name;
He viewed with scorn the thronging crowd ;
“Oh, sophists vain!” he cried aloud,
“The Truth -is not the thing you think—
This muddied water on the brink ;
“It lies far out from banks arrayed
With tangled reeds and lying shade.
“In shallow pools you weaklings creep;
But in its w r ell the Truth lies deep!
“Too deep for you to care about;
But I —yes, I —will seek it out!”
He climbed upon a shelving height,
Then, plunging, disappeared from sight.
He came not back. They say the youth
Was drowned. Perchance, he found the
Truth!
N.S.W.
HAMER.

kept apace. The demand for a lamp that
would stay alight in the strongest gale,
resulted in the “hurricane” lamp, the “Rayo”
representing the highest standard to which
these lamps have attained.

can be used than

responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 2, 1916.
IS WHITE AUSTRALIA WORTH
DOING ANYTHING FOR EXCEPT
TALK?
One of the yells raised by the opponents of
reinforcing the army was this: —
Are you prepared to break down the barriers that
keep Australia white? Are you prepared to see our
beloved country submerged by an inrush of that
“colored ocean” by which Mr. Hughes himself has
warned us that we are surrounded ? That is the
question you are called on to decide. Conscript all
our white men....and either we must behold our
industries perish and the remnants of our popula-
tion starve, or we must import colored labor,
jeopardise our racial integrity, and give up the
cherished ideal of a great white nation enthroned
in the Pacific.
It was not an ineffective yell either. Many
people heard it. In a sense The Bulletin
can find no reason to regret that the colored-
labor question should exercise a strong influ-
ence on the minds of Australian citizens.
To them the question is vital. Yet this
has been sorry to see the very just
‘apprehensions of the people played upon by
a string of words which in the circumstances
of the hour were maddeningly irrelevant to
the problem they helped to confuse. The
nation’s army is locked in deadly struggle
with an enemy; and the argument was that
this army must be allowed to waste away in
order to maintain a ludicrously ineffective
defence against danger which would arrive
with a bang if the enemy pulled out a
winner. It looks more like a shriek for a
straight waistcoat than a sane contribution
to controversy. But of course the argument
was not seriously put forward. It was
merely howled into the atmosphere much
in the same way as thousands of demented
individuals burst into braggart song when
the war started and bitterly reviled every-
one who ventured to remark that the pro-
ceedings might last more than two or three
weeks. Yet, though there is no arguing
with a yell, there are a few things that
require saying about colored labor, and a
few which the Labor party and the other
party and conscriptionists and antis ought
to take into consideration. And the first of the
things that need saying is this : the coolie can
be a product of peace just as readily as of
war, and in this paper’s judgment Borrow
and Bloat and Shirk and Loaf have always
been formidable menaces to a White Aus-
tralia. The success of the policy of Borrow
and Bloat is not to be judged by the super-
ficial events of a year or two, but by the
foundation on which it rests. If there is
anyone who thinks that the white-ants have
not been at work on this foundation, then
such an individual carries his eyes under his
hat.
* # * #
The case for a White Australia is partly
racial and partly economic. It is easy to
prove that the mixture of white and colored
races is undesirable. Colored peoples know
this as well as the whites, but the dangers
of such mixtures have often enough in the
world’s history been accepted because of a
real or assumed economic necessity, and
repented of in misery and degradation after-
wards. It is easy to show also that the
exploitation of the colored man’s labor is
as indefensible morally as exploitation of the
white man ; that cheap labor is a bad invest-
ment in comparison with labor that has
reached a higher standard of living; that
white labor is more effective, productive and
reliable than the labor of the colored helot.
But there is a qualification to this, and it is
that white labor must perform its job—-that
it shall be economically productive. And
this paper p,uts it without equivocation to
the citizens of Australia that unless the
industries of the Commonwealth are carried
on by white men after the war with an
honest intention of fulfilling the obligations
involved in the White Australia policy, they
will find themselves driven by the stern
consequences of their long shirk and criminal
reliance on loans and imports to get others
to do what they had not the wit to see they
should do for their own salvation. White
Australia is not a mere yell, though a lot
if people think it is. There can be as much
."dither about it as about anything else, and
Heaven alone knows what the possibilities
of blither on any given subject really are.
Thousands of men are ready to talk in a
loose way about a White Australia being
worth fighting for. How many are there
who realise that a White Australia is worth
working for—worth being made the corner
stone of a public policy that will hand on to
the -Australian kid something better than a
record of wasted time and a staggering load
of debt, contracted so that cash might be
bartered for votes? This paper is regret-
fully compelled to say that it is convinced
there have been more politicians toiling in
Australia to create conditions favorable to
the birth of a new demand for coolie labor
than there have been statesmen working to
create conditions that would make coolie
labor.an impossible contingency.
Talk is not one of the barriers that will
keep Australia white. Wind will never keep
the coolie away. It is only by populating
Australia, living on the produce of the
country and getting rid of the twin curses
of foreign debt and dependence on foreigners
for goods, that the ambition to keep white
will have a dog’s chance of survival in the
generations to come. Borrow and Bloat
and Loaf mean the perversion of the healthy
ideals of the past: if they have not already
destroyed the sense of obligation to work
for Australia’s future, they have at least
seriously impaired it. How many of the
men who have been using “White Australia”
as an excuse for making the Commonwealth
look contemptible in the eyes of her allies
at a moment when the nation’s very life is
trembling in the scales have ever raised
their voice" in protest against the policies
of squander and log-rolling evasion that have
disgraced our democracy of recent years?
None, so far as this paper knows; and the
tragedy of the thing is that their opponents
have been as somnolently indifferent as they.
But if any. of them imagine that “White
Australia” is a mere shibboleth they are
mad.. The day it degenerates into that cate-
gory the next stage will be easily reached.
There is a constructive side to the White
Australia policy. It has been, and is now.
being systematically neglected. Indeed, it
has been worse than neglected. There is a
poison for that policy; and loanmongers and
bloaters and land jobbers and loafers of
every sort have been mixing it in tons. To
say that compulsory service involves aban-
doning the ideal of a white nation is silly.
But a White Australia living on imported
goods acquired by foreign loans and leaning
up against Cohen for a job is having a
gamble with its very soul.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior, D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
LEARNING FROM THE BARBARIAN.
When the Greek met the Roman for the
first time in Southern Italy the contempt of
the cultivated person from the /Egean for
his enemy was supreme. He was the bar-
barian. the person who knew nothing of
Hellenic art or idealism, whose low mind
ran in the direction of law, military fitness
and orderly routine. He could not make
poetry or enjoy the drama or appreciate
sculpture or even speak the Greek language
with accuracy. Therefore he was of no
account; and being of no account the
superior race could learn nothing from b_im
even in a military way. For lack of the
latter lesson the Greeks, even with the aid
of the best general of the times and picked
troops from Epirus, got walloped to a stand-
still. There is always a danger of that sort
of thing happening, and an under-estimate
of the enemy based on some phase of his
character is as bad an error as any nation
can fall into. The German brute, ravishing,
destroying, murdering, sating his foul lust in
sweet old-world villages and historic minster
towns, tills us with a loathing and horror
which demands satisfaction in his defeat and'
ruin; but this mood of decent and righteous
indignation should not blind us to the patent
facts upon which a lot of his strength is
based. Making all allowances for his secret
purpose, his years of preparation, his defi-
ance of international laws and conventions
and certain elements of luck which at times
have helped him out, the German does show
a marvellous grip and tenacity and a mar-
vellous faith in himself. So far, too, he has
kept his end up astonishingly well ; and
while we hope that his smash is not far off,
what he has already achieved is sufficiently
remarkable to make us look for the points
he can show us in nation-building, while
repudiating the purpose behind them and the
character of the man himself.
* * * *
Already, on the military side, most that
he had to teach has been pretty thoroughly
learned by his three big opponents, England,
France and Russia. But mere military
arrangements are not the only factors that
enable a nation to stand the bitter test of
war. Underlying these are other things that
count for a lot even in the actual carnage.
First may be placed the notion of German
•civilisation as held by the common soldier
in the ranks. We sum up all things Teuton
in the scornful word “Ivultur.” But the
contrast of the rigid German lines of
development with the better and sweeter
ideals of the allied nations does not dispose
of the plain facts that Germany was better
in her home deeds than in her theory of
things, and that two at least of the
Allies were lame enough in their efforts
to reach after any sort of ideal. The
common Teuton soldier knows that before
the war the Fatherland stood to him for a
lot of comfort. While the slums of England
howled aloud to Heaven and those of Sydney
shouted plague curses at every Fat thing they
saw, Germany had cleaned up most of her
cities and looked after her people with
thoroughness and sympathy. This may or
may not have been done with the idea of
getting capable soldiers for the coming war;
but whether Fritz has now paid too high
a price for the conditions under which he
once lived or not, the memory of those con-
ditions remains with him in the muddiest
and bloodiest trench, to easily convince him
that German aggression means gain -to the
world.
Again, in the matter of popular education,
the German system of trade and technical
schools gave the people a big lift. It may be
that these institutions were merely adjuncts
tp the military hopes of the national leaders.
If so, the success attained was out of all
proportion to the incentive. The Teuton did,
as a plain fact, score enormously in the ways
of trade and industry through superiority of
knowledge. His natural advantages were not
great, yet he built up a huge commerce, and
seized business advantages in a manner only
possible where practical knowledge is
wedded to patience and skill. The German
peace organisation was far-reaching ; and if
peace was regarded in some quarters as
merely a foundation for war, such a know-
ledge must have been pretty well confined to
military circles. To the average German
the thing looked good ; and to a very high
point it was good, if certain antiquated
rorces with a modern edge on them had not
come down from the Middle Ages to spoil
things. But the score in favor of Germany
went farther than municipalism, education
and organisation. In some respects the Ger
man Empire was about the freest country in
Europe. Of political freedom the people had
little enough, though those in power contrived
to make them believe that they had a lot;
but within very wide limits personal liberty
had a good run. Though the Prussian home
might be as rigid as a Scottish Auld Lichts
manse, over the greater part of Germany
there was a fat, heavyweight toleration of
others’ views and practices which made the
citizen feel that he was his own man. The
wowser didn’t exist; beer was regarded as
desirable; the band played very good music.
The military heels clicked uneasily, but
llans was fully persuaded that he had a
very good time. He talked Socialism over
his pipe, but the subject was pretty much an
academic one. and he didn’t feel in the least
like fighting for principles whose acceptance
would leave only a minor effect on the daily
sausage. In a word, all the time that the
Essen factories were hammering out big guns
the prevailing note in Teuton domestic life
was one of content.
* * * *
Of course, against all this there is a lot to
put in the debit column. The condition of
Germany was at no point ideal; but she had
something to teach us, and something that
we are fools if we are unwilling to learn.
The very simple and obvious fact that a
decent city can produce better fighting men
than a rookery of slums is worth noting as
much for defence purposes as for aggression.
The plain truth that trade education makes
for commercial advantage may be generally
accepted ; but Australia at least is not losing
much perspiration in her endeavors to put
it into practice. National organisation, too,
has its advantages in making the nation
richer, more comfortable and every way more
efficient; but, so far. our statesmen appear
to think that it is merely a meaningless
phrase to use occasionally in public speeches.
And the wide, individual liberty that makes
for a kindly outlook and a good conscience is
also a thing worth thinking about. With all
his crimes thick about him, the German has
still quite a reasonable amount to teach us.
We can be debtor to the barbarian as well as
to the Greek, and only a blockish vanity
stands in the way of our learning. Because
of some things that really matter, the Ger-
man has held, his own until now with aston-
ishing fortitude and resource. Because of
those other things that the world would be
glad to forget, he is going to get the great
licking of the ages.
POLLUTING THE SACRED THING.
Judging by the recent Referendum cam-
paign, the biggest of all dangers to demo-
cracy is organised party. Neither king nor
emperor nor the uncrowned despot who
JTom time to time rises by the popular will
and causes the theoretical democrat to
gnash his teeth, is so subversive of ordered
human liberty as the machine which modern
representative government has evolved, and
which is seen in all its strength in Aus-
tralian politics. Anything which makes any
citizen or any representative of the citizens
act in any possible case against his own
conscience and judgment is destructive of
democracy. The whole theory of democratic
government is based on the idea that the
citizen will act honestly if given his full
electoral freedom. He may act foolishly or
hastily, but that is not dangerous to the
community in any acute sepse; for the great
bulk of popular opinion, if clean and out-
spoken, will make for wisdom, and the deep
instincts of men in the mass are generally
right. The danger is that .citizens for some
imaginary advantage over other citizens
may join hands to defeat what they really
believe to be their own best interests; and
the moment this point is reached democracy
becomes a mockery, and despotism takes its
place. The despotism may for the moment
be the despotism of the machine, but it soon
becomes the despotism of the men behind
the machine; and the natural transition
from this to the rule of the autocrat
clothed with arbitrary power is easy. Once
men realise that their democratic freedom is
gone they look for a strong man to save
them, with the inevitable result that a
C/ESAi;. a Napoleon or a Diaz comes along
and really gives the people a better show
than they had under the distorted forms of
democracy.
* * * *
Australia is in grave danger in this
respect, for her democracy is seriously tram-
melled, and individual freedom of opinion is
no longer the factor that it might be in
national affairs. Parties there must be, for
the grouping together of men of like opinion
is naturalbut the machines called party
organisations in Australia group together
men of unlike opinions, and force con-
sciences to bend to what is thought to be
party expediency. Australia has witnessed
the spectacle of two parties in Federal poli-
tics each with a majority in its own ranks
in favor of a -certain reform and each
humbly bowing to the will of its own
minority and sinking a question which was
of first importance to the Commonwealth.
Had the parties been real and the cleavage
along natural lines, the reform would at
once have taken its place in the forefront
of the political battle; but the two machines,
which from their very nature can have no
real concern for any reform, betrayed the
people of a continent to its continual shame
and loss. As against such sham party
organisation the great principle of the Refer-
endum has offered to the democracy a means
of escape from its servitude by a return to
that primal government in which the free
elector’s voice must be supreme above all
mechanical combines and give real expres-
sion to the nation’s will; but the _ party
engineers, alive to the danger of their own
extinction, have stepped in and made a deter-
mined attempt to nobble the Referendum
itself, warp it from its true intent and make
it serve their own plan of keeping demo-
cracy under the wdieels of the machine.
When the recent Referendum was autho-
rised it was understood that a free expres-
sion of Australian opinion was the one
thing to be desired. That the matter should
be fully discussed and debated was in every
way desirable. All possible light was
required on the question. Thought, senti-
ment, emotion and prejudice had a clear
title to find fullest expression. Only in such
a way could the will of the nation be abso-
lutely known. The matter was referred to
the people and was at once by that refer-
ence beyond the scope of party and the
party machine. But the engineers were not
willing to leave it at that. Such a Refer-
endum, once successfully conducted, must
be the death-knell of their own unholy sys-
tem. Instantly the machine was set in motion
to persecute and injure those who held a
particular view, to discredit them with the
people who had looked upon them as com-
rades and leaders, and to coerce them, if
possible, into silence lest the weight of their
arguments should convince the public. All
this was a plain crime against democracy,
and the persons guilty of it by their act
ranged themselves definitely on the side of
the tyrants and bigots of all ages who
imagined that the torch of freedom could
be blown out if the forced draught were
sufficiently strong. Side by side with such
practices citizen liberties could not exist.
The engineers and wire-pullers had no desire
that they should exist.
* * * *
The direct appeal to the people by Refer-
endum must be regarded as the most sacred
of all our political institutions, and to
tamper with the free opinion behind it is the
democratic shame of shames. In the case of
the vote recently taken all this was intensi-
fied by the question on which the people
were asked to give their voice. It con-
cerned the whole future of the Australian
race —perhaps, in as far as it was a lead to
other democracies, the whole destiny of
Man. The political leaders and the Parlia-
ment of the nation deliberately took from
their own shoulders the burden of so great
a responsibility and left it to the people
intimately concerned to decide for them-
selves. Under such circumstances, every
feeling of honor, honesty and good citizen-
ship suggested that the party organisations
should also stand aside and leave the field
of thought and conscience absolutely free.
The voting at such a Referendum partook
of the nature of a religious act. But to the
party machiAist nothing is sacred except his
job; and from the moment that the great
decision began to be clearly considered by
the Australian citizen, the machine’s full
force was employed to warp and twist the
national thought and lash the weak-minded
or uncertain into line with an alleged pro-
gramme of the party. If such practices and
the spirit behind them could prevail, demo-
cracy is dead. The Referendum is its last
great defence —a defence that cannot be
captured by frontal attack, but may be
sapped and mined while its defenders are
unaware of the danger. The party machinist
has made representative government a
mockery. It is up to the people of Aus-
tralia to cry a loud “Hands off!” when the
same unclean person attempts to lay a
destroying fist on the great, simple principle
of the appeal to the nation.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior. D. M. Wrioiit and
P. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]


THE NEW AUSTRALIA THAT WASN’T
SO GOOD AS THE OLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twenty-three years ago William Lane
and his band of enthusiasts shook the social
sins of Australia off their feet and sailed
from Sydney in the Royal Tar with the idea
of establishing a kind of New Jerusalem in
Paraguay, the reputed Land of the Eternal
Siesta. The pilgrims soon discovered that
life and labor in the S. American montes was
not conducive to that brotherly love and con-
cord which is essential if the New Jeru-
salem is to develop into a going concern.
The history of the outside troubles and
internal dissensions \yhich led to the aban-
donment of the communistic ideal has been
given world-wide publicity, and much politi-
cal capital has been manufactured by anti-
Socialist writers out of the hardships,
struggles and dissensions of the pioneers.
One “pioneer,” who had spent a few
months iu New Australia, compiled a book
about 50 per cent, of which consisted of
arguments of people directly opposed to the
principles which New Australia and Cosine
represented. Possibly the hard graft and
harder tack which usually accompany pio-
neering struck a chill to the marrow of this
gently-nurtured youth fresh from the flesh-
pots of Public School and Old World civilisa-
tion, and in his modest little compilation he
made the most of the harrowing details. He
was particularly entertaining when he started
to dissect Cosine, particularly as he was
never on Cosme. He was so eager to boost
his side of the argument that he endeavored
to make it appear that the people of Cosme
were an aggregation of gaunt, starved
wretches who, in frenzied devotion to their
communistic creed, forgetting God and their
country, prowled through swamp and monte
foaming imprecations against capital and
civilisation, living on the monkeys, monte
cats and armadillos, which they killed with
their bare hands and devoured raw. The
contagion of this stinking tripe spread so
that yon meet people who firmly believe that
the inhabitants of New Australia and Cosme
have degenerated into a community of white
niggers living in hollow logs and gibbering
Socialistic platitudes at the passing stranger.
Let us get to the solid truth about these
settlements.
Those who remained on New Australia
when William Lane and his followers
seceded and founded Cosme soon abandoned
their communist principles, and each man
vas allotted a portion of the communal
lands and certain grazing rights. Under
the conditions obtaining in this part of
1 araguay the only paying spec, is cattle-
raising. The New Australians wisely
devoted their energies to this business,
doing little or nothing in the way of
agriculture or working the valuable timber
in their montes The men who took stock-
taking on are to-day, almost without ex-
ception, comfortably off, some of them own-
ing as many as 400 or 500 head of cattle.
On the other hand, the people of Cosme
depended on the cultivation of sugar, maize,
mandioca, tobacco, coffee and bananas and
\i*i development of their timber resources.
Although they worked harder than the New
Australians they have not done so well in
a financial sense, as, owing to bad markets
and worse transport facilities, all their pro-
duce except timber, sugar, maize and tobacco
was practically valueless except for home
use. But they are far from being in as bad
case as they are represented to be. Poor
they are, but as hardy, independent and
self-respecting a community as can be met
with between the Poles. They have not “for-
gotten God or their country”; neither have
they forgotten themselves. Although but a
remnant of the stalwarts who sailed from
Australia in the Royal Tar in pursuit of All
Desire, only to be disillusioned within a few
weeks of their arrival in the Promised Land,
these people have remained steadfast to their
color-principles, though hemmed in on every
side by an Indian people with the morals of
monkeys and the progressive ideas of a sloth.
They have jealously preserved their national-
ity, language, system of education, manners
and customs, and when I went among them I
felt that I w r as at home in the Australian
bush of my boyhood.
But there is no future for these people in
Paraguay—nothing except a life of hard,
unremunerative toil and an exile’s grave.
They have seen their hoped-for Utopia fail
and dissolve into nothingness, but they still
face life gamely and resolutely, and, in the
teeth of almost insuperable difficulties, live
in the hope that they may one day be able to
return to their native land. But if this is
dependent on their own slender resources
they will all leave their bones in Paraguay
and their children will be lost to Australia.
The present population of Cosme is 12
men, nine _women, 28 biJys and 1G girls, a
total of 65 souls. There is nothing from
which you can better judge the burning
patriotism of this little community than this:
it has sent 16 of its boys to the Front. This
probably constitutes a record for our race,
and is a glorious refutation of the miserable
and malicious lie that they had “forgotten
their country.”
The Cosme people, with one or two excep-
tions, would give years of their lives to be
back in Australia again. And what better
material could be found for settling the
empty North than these hardy, self-reliant
folk who have been battling with the tropical
Paraguayan wilderness for 20 years? If
Australia really wants the North populated
by men of our own race, where can she find
a more congenial or profitable task than in
repatriating and settling these people and
their families there?
. Australia may find thousands of European
immigrants and war-worn soldiers to plant in
the Territory, but she will never get any-
thing so eminently fitted for the development
of the North as these our own people, who
are being wasted on a morally and finanei-
all.v-bankrupt country like Paraguay.
And the matter is hereby earnestly com-
mended to the attention of those whom it
should concern.
BAD AUSTRALIAN.
MENDOZA, ARGENTINA.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Disunited Australia and the Pound of
Steak.
Tiie High Court has made it all clear at
last. A year or so back it was established by the
N. S. Wales wheat grab that if a State took
possession of any commodity, ownership
being thus vested in the Crown, the State
aforesaid was acting within the law when
it refused to allow any person to push its
property over the border. Now, in the
Bananaland meat case, the Court lays it
down that if the State announces by Act of
Parliament its intention not to allow a com-
modity to be transferred over the line
because the Crown will possess itself of the
goods later on if they are wanted, this also
is good law, since it fails to outrage the
Commonwealth Constitution, which declares
that trade, commerce and intercourse
between the States shall be absolutely free.
How it can be free if.six States have the
power to legislate to prevent it being free
is another matter, and seemingly is of no
consequence whatever, the legal point being,
not whether there has been interference with
ordinary intercourse, but whether the States
still have authority to put their joint and
several feet down. A majority of the Court
found: —•
The power of the Legislature of Queensland ex-
tended to making laws for the peace, order and good
government of that State in all cases whatsoever. A
law having for its object to make stock bred in
Queensland available for food for the Impel ial
Forces was a law conducive to the good government
of that State.
Also, apparently, when the said law pre-
vents Australian forces in Sydney and Mel-
bourne getting Bananaland steak until they
arrive in England, it is conducive to peace
and happiness all round. And as the inci-
dent keeps up the price of the domestic
brisket and sirloin for Sunday at Parra-
matta and Coogee, “good government’’ gets
an additional testimonial, or thistle or some-
thing. But what a farcical inversion of the
clear intention of the Constitution it all is!
As the law now stands the provision in the
Constitution protecting inter-State trade from
any barriers that might be constructed by
State Parliaments is about as dead as last
year’s mutton. All that it would be neces-
sary for Victoria to do if Peacock wanted
to prevent cauliflowers or barracouta being
dragged over the Murray would' be to pass
an Act to retain them lest Collingwood
should be invaded by Montenegro. In fact,
it seems quite possible that a single, all-
embracing measure could be framed that
would tie inter-State trade up into more
knots than if each State was running a
tariff.
W. Higgs and the Let ’er Slide Policy.
Federal Treasurer Higgs last week made
what was alleged to be “a reply” to charges
ot extravagance against the Government, in
course of which he said they had been pre-
fened by people who overlooked the increased
cost of goods and services. He went on to
remark:—■
Study of the interim financial statement of Sep-
tember 27 would show that the total expenditure in
the pre-war year 1913-14 was £25,000,000 in
round numbers. In the first year of the war it
£40,000,000, in the second year to
£70,000,000, and the estimated expenditure for the
third year was over £120,000,000. It seemed to be
forgotten that war costs money, and the longer the
war lasts the greater the yearly cost.
Brother Higgs might just as well have
declared that the day after to-morrow
will not be the night before last and
have challenged the universe to contradict
him. The charge of extravagance is neither
sustained nor disproved by flinging down a
large hunk of figures about what the war
costs. But it is given considerable force by
the knowledge common to every citizen of the
Commonwealth that—
(l) The Australian war expenditure is conducted
upon principles which would bring any commercial
undertaking to ruin.
(2) Parallel with the drain of war on the country’s
resources, the ordinary departmental expenditure of
the Commonwealth goes leaping upwards.
(3) Every department into which an independent
investigator has pushed an inquiring nose has been
pronounced to be run on wasteful, costly lines.
If lliggs is prepared to deny this, he ought
to do it. The charge of extravagance
applies not only to the Commonwealth but
to every one of the Seven Ministries infest-
ing this happy land. They are all struggling
tor pre-eminence as spenders and taxers, and
that the sum total of their operations is
shockingly dangerous is known by no man
better than by W. G. lliggs.

The Chance of Low Prices and High
Wages.
A cable last week told us that Ford, advo-
cate of peace and maker of stuffed doves and
motor-cars, intends to pay his expert women
workers a minimum of £ 1 per day—the same
as the. men get. It sounds like,good wages
and might have been expected to depend on
some huge increase in the cost of motor-cars
in U.S.A. But it doesn’t. Cars of all
sorts are cheaper than ever. One can be
bought for £80; and the average price of
all U.S.A. cars is said to be only £l2O. Add
to this fact that strength and reliability
have improved with the. reduction of cost
and the increase of wages, and it is plain
that in some departments at least the people
across the Pacific have a lot to teach us.
The lesson lies in organisation under satis-
factory tariffs. Every new industry brought
into being by a tariff makes it easier to
establish another. We say lately how the
establishment of the coke-making industry
in Australia had made it possible to estab-
lish the steel industry. The steel industry
will make it possible to establish a dozen
others ; and the dozen others will make the
steel industry so much more profitable that
it will be able to pay higher wages than
are possible to-day. This is the process
that is going on in the United States, which
were. not afraid to make a start with a high
tariff. The women workers at Ford’s fac-
tories are now lifting a pound a day as the
reward of that policy. It is more than the
Australian workers could get even if they
had a wages board apiece.
Conscience and Cold Feet.
The person with “conscientious objections”
begins to loom largely in the Exemption
Courts. Usually his conscience didn’t start
putting in any serious work until about the
week before last; and when this is the case
the extent to which his feet affect his moral
outlook is difficult to determine. Still, there
ls such a thing as conscientious object.on.
and it requires sympathetic handling. It
may, no doubt, be argued that no man has
a right to a private conscience which con-
flicts with that, of the State; but this is a
dangerous doctrine,' and has caused more
trouble than most other things. To get over
the difficulty it is necessary to set aside
certain specific national work, the doing of
which by conscripted labor may enable others
with no conscientious scruples to take their
places in the fighting ranks. Such work
should not be regarded as in any way dis-
honorable ; but it should be hard enough,
distasteful enough, and (if necessary.)
dangerous enough to fairly test the good
faith of the conscientious' objector. Your
real conscientious objector is of no use as a
recruit. If true to his convictions he would
lay down his arms in face of the foe and go
to his death with a smile. Yet there is no
reason why on that account he should not
bear a special portion of the burden of the
community in return for its protection. If
this alternative were properly known and
the person called to serve his country had to
face one of two evils, the military camp
would probably miss very few recruits. But
whether that be so or not a cheap insult is
not the way to remove a serious obstacle.
Compulsory military service must be admin-
istered with discretion, and not by the crude
methods which answer well enough when
drafting ewes and wethers.

Much though The Bulletin regrets that
lliggs, Gardiner and Russeix were not of
different mind about compulsory service, it
has no word of hostile criticism to offer
over their resignation from the Ministry.
No reasonably decent men could have
remained after having evidence of
Hughes’s scheme to interfere with the voting
at Saturday’s poll. That the Prime Minister
should have seen fit to heavily discount the
efforts of every speaker and writer in Aus-
tralia supporting compulsory service by
committing himself to continual contradic-
tions, evasions and wild verbal eruptions
was perhaps his affair. But that he should
have tried to associate them with a device
for fouling the ballot-box is something
which The Bulletin at any rate repudiates
any connection with or toleration of. By
their resignation and exposure of the con-
templated trickery lliggs and the others
saved the political life of Australia from an
outrage that would have made mockery of
our institutions and defiled the very altar of
liberty.
From a man who speaks with some
authority :
Far at sea I can still hear Cockatoo Dock scream-
ing for reform. This is the third transport I have
had experience of. The engineer of one of them esti-
mated that the refitting of his boat cost double
what it should have done. Apart from straightout
loafing on the job, silly sectional rows between
unions as to who should do little scraps of repair
work here and there were responsible for numerous
delays. Stories of quiet poker parties of workmen in
out-of-the-way parts of the ship are vouched for.
But it is not merely the loafer who is wasting
money. A steriliser was erected in such a position
that one of the boats could not have been launched.
A hospital was built in such a position that the
first sea shipped would hit it. This happened, and
the hospital was stove in. Not that it mattered
much, as the place had been untenable even in
moderate weather owing to constant leaks through
the roof and floor. The wireless room was in like
condition, in spite of the valuable apparatus it
contained. During wet weather the operators spent
their time plugging up leaks. This though the
safety of the ship might have depended on the wire-
less working efficiently. I could give many more
instances, including the construction of “ports” that
couldn’t be closed. This, however, should be enough
to at least suggest the disgraceful character of the
dock work which is being done under Government
employ in the port of Sydney. It is a commonplace
observation that the Government won’t interfere —
and “How can you expect ’em to?” you are asked. I
don’t know whether it’s a reasonable thing to expect
them to. But in that case, so that the responsibility
might be divided, and that the punishment for get-
ting a fair thing done should not fall on the Labor
party only, I do think it would be proper in this
crisis to have a National Government.
“Ex-Banker” : When Treasurers are east-
ing round for revenue it is opportune to call
attention to an unused fund of money which
properly belongs to the Governments, either
as trustee or as legal owner in lieu of the
original owner. There are in the hands' of
banks, public companies, liquidators,
assignees and trustee companies large and
numerous sums of money unclaimed by
their owners, which do not and never can
belong to the persons holding them. The
Statute of Limitations with its six-year
period applies to debtors, not creditors.
When, 20 years ago, I first wrote to The
Bulletin on this subject I gave an in-
stance from my own banking experience of
how easy it is for depositors’ deposits, either
fixed or on current account, to be lost by
the owner. A careless ledgerkeeper opening
a new ledger had to transfer over 500
unclaimed balances. He was a bad writer
and a worse teetotaller, and when he had
finished copying in the names of the
customers, mostly dead and all forgetful,
not even he could read them. They were
never checked or called over, and "if by-
chance “Thomas James” applied for an
old balance left by him when he went shear-
ing in Queensland 10 years previously, he
was told there was no money at his credit
and of course; Thomas having no proof, the
matter ended. The new name.in the new ledger
was Thomas Jones,” and the money
belonged to nobody henceforth. I suspect
when that bank closed its door all its
unclaimed balances were transferred to' a
suspense account and disappeared for ever.

The Commoner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lords, through summers a hundred score I
have flung to the sod below
Shovel and flail for the sword and mail, the
gun or the singing bow—
How have you guerdoned, how shall you
guerdon, me who have served you so?
Long in the shade of your buttressed walls
you cherished me and mine,
A sturdy, shock-head breed, to fight and to
follow the herding swine ;
Passive in heart-deep ignorance, ankle-deep
in the mast,
We bowed at the beat of your jennet’s feet,
while you and your ladies passed,
Dragging in peace your firing logs, storing
the yellow yield,
Whose every grain we had wrung in pain
from the balk-edged Virgate field ;
And for the toil we rendered you, the fruits
we gave to your hand.
Your favor cast us the common right of the
outer meadow T -land,
The boon of shelter beneath your sword, of
your wisdom’s chance redress,
And to fight in quarrels we recked not of,
and understood the less.
Ever when lords had wounds to salve or a
petty'pride to save,
We strung the ash and we swung the axe
and pushed with the pike and glaive,
Spitted and slew, for a poke of loot and a
parcel of buttered words,
Vassals who foraged and fought in turn at
the hest of other lords;
True as a dog to lord and law, lusty and
witless we,
Who looted and laughed and loosed the
shaft in Spain or Picardy.
And some by the Maid and the blade won
through, and some by the wayside
dropped,
And others sailed for the Downs again with
the thumb of the bow-hand lopped ;
Landless men we mattocked and threshed
for the tale of our niggard pence,
All-but-dead we buried the dead in the years
of the Pestilence,
Grinned in the brank and the galling stocks*
and stank in the county jail,
Counted our measure of happiness by the
hoops on a pot of ale.
You struck our hands for a partridge-snare
and a bended bow in the brake.
Our scooped-out eyes have showed the price
we paid for a leveret’s sake.
Yet ever we groped toward the goal of our
souls’ imaginings;
Stupid and faithful we served you, lords!
God knows we have loved you, Kings!
You filohed our fallows to graze your sheep.
and clipped our common land,
And we wrote the lesson you taught in
blood, that our sons should understand.
Stubborn, seeking the meagre hope the hid-
den years might give,
V e labored and lived and throve amain, for
only the strong might live !
And when, perchance, for a grinding tax or
a stolen field in fee,
We rose and struck, you rode and slew with
your swording yeomanry,
Hunted the bitter remnant out to harbor in
ditch and hedge,
Ilested thereafter, and lifted thanks to the
God of your privilege.
Later, your mine-shafts stabbed the land, and
we followed the winding seams
In the gutted earth, while you built us
towns and slagged our running streams.
From Time’s grey dawn we have learned to
sweat that your limbs might softly lie,
For we knew the law—that the man should
serve, and the master rule thereby.
Ever and ever you rendered thanks that the
people’s hearts were true
To fight in a myriad wars for the land they
could not own—and you !
Lords, for a hundred score of years we have
fought on. sea and land
The wars of your brewing we recked not of,
nor cared to understand ;
We have counted virtue in wounds and dirt,
in death have we holden fast,
And you filled our bellies with empty praise
and a broken faith at the last.
You have lauded and armed and thrust us
forth, and you whom our bodies guard—
What have you paid of the pledge you made,
in the calm of the Afterward?
You have drilled our kind in the ordered
ways, in the fear of the traitor’s Hell,
Taught us the masterless servants’ shame
—God, but you schooled us well!
There is never a thread of the garb you
wear, nor a grain of your sorry gold.
But was won by the favor of these our hands
and our fathers’ hands of old.
We have owned the cheer of the fireless
hearth and the fungus-blotted wall,
For that which had been must always be,
and the law was over all;
You figured and posed, and vapored schemes
r for the poor man’s deep distress,
Till our pockets brimmed with your minted
words, and our souls with your promises.
Shall it count for nothing that, biding still
by your master-written creed,
We have bled for twice a thousand years,
and a thousand still may bleed?
(For this is the way of the common man,
though his sad heart dare the steel
In a wageless quarrel he never sought, he
cannot be aught but leal !)
Yet a seed may sprout where a hand has
sown, and the minds of simple men
Being turned to brooding, grow thereto, nor
lightly turn again!
We have ringed your banners and borne your
scars the blood-bought ages through";
We are sick to death of your promises—is
there nothing, lords, ye can do?
To-night, from the heart of sullied France
to the dunes and the Flemish sea, '
W e light by the law of years that were, for
the sake of the years that be.
Tie are maimed and die for freedom, lords!
Will you never make ns free?
N.S.W.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

The case as it has been set out from day to
day lately :
Allied claims that the enemy is being walloped
on the Somme.
German claims that the position thereabouts is
sreure and that the attack must fail in its objective.
Roumanians said to be holding their own. Ger-
mans assert they are advancing.
Russians fighting hard and stated to he hurrying
mere troops to Roumania.
Enemy submarines increasingly active among
British and neutral shipping; and large numbers of
new “subs” said be building.
French hit out suddenly at Verdun and wrest back
various fortified positions.
The usual conflicting yarns about the internal
condition of the Fatherland.
And apparently it will be that way when
General Rain and the Heavy Frosts arrive in
a day or two and the wearied armies settle
down to wait until the mud dries up and
they can get into their stride again. Perhaps
by that time the chaos in the vicinity of
Greece may be tidied lip ; but there is nothing
to indicate that anything more decisive will
have occurred, though, unless enough Rus-
sians leap into Bulgaria directly, the hap-
penings thereabouts may provoke the Kaiser
to give the kids another holiday. Just at
present the individual with prophetic utter-
ances about Germany being on the verge of
being wiped off the map is keeping very quiet.
That at least is something to thank the good
Lord for. It gives a chance to the man who
wants to shout into the public ear that the
way to victory is long and hard and rough,
and that there is nothing to look forward to
but many months of battle. And if something
else is looked forward to and prepared for,
the wash-up may be a less joyful business
than a lot of people were thinking 12 months
ago.
At the time of writing the trench vote and
the absentee’s scrap of paper may or may
not turn the balance in favor of a Referen-
dum “Yes.” But that is of little material
consequence. The roar of “No” has been
far too emphatic to be disregarded by the
Federal Parliament as it is now constituted.
We shall not have conscription. But our
men must have reinforcements. Our whole
job now is to get them by the only means
that offer—by a vigorous recruiting cam-
paign. And upon those who declared that
compulsion was not necessary—upon those
who affirmed that we could fully reinforce
our men without “putting the shackles of
conscription” upon the people—rests the
heavy end of the responsibility of making
that campaign a success.
Before State politicians and the press enter
upon the new recruiting campaign one thing
ought to be made quite clear to the Defence
Department and the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment. There must be no more monkey-
ing either with news about the war or
with criticism of the Defence Department;
and a definite undertaking to that effect
must be extracted from the Commonwealth
Government. In the past anything and almost
everything has been suppressed “in the in-
terests of voluntary enlistment.” There was
nothing that could not be held to be in some
remote way “prejudicial to recruiting.” Those
who had to fight the recent campaign know
the result. They know that past lying and
past. suppression of the truth were among
the worst difficulties they had to wrestle
with. And while so much was to be lost
nothing was to be gained by it; therefore in
England, after the first few weeks of the
war, there has been no censorship compar-
able with the insufferable tyranny which has
been imposed and maintained in Australia.
Before State politicians enter upon the new
campaign they should, then, at least insist
that this tyranny, which ended only with
the opening of the conscription campaign,
shall not be resumed under the old pretext
that anything and almost everything is
“prejudicial to recruiting.”

The Gonsalez singers gave two new per-
formances of exceptional merit last week.
Both in “Ernani,” spangled with Verdi's
flowers of orchestration, and in the light
music of Rossini’s “Barber of Seville” the
male chorus and the orchestra were in better
form than on any previous occasion. Pre-
sumably they have been merely rehearsing
and trying it on the Sydney dog for the
last 10 weeks. And the dog has, in moments
of agony, bitten savagely. The female chorus
is where it was—a slipshod conglomeration
which sings askew and hooks up its dresses
in the same fashion. In “Ernani” the enthu-
siasm began early—with a vociferous encore
to the opening song of the gentleman supers.
Tenor Dagradi and soprano de Revers were
more satisfactory than in many former
operas. Basso Cacialli and baritone
Scamuzzi gave, as usual, admirable perform-
ances. Scamuzzi, as Carlo V., with a flam-
ing red wig, amethyst leather boots nearly
to his waist, and brown kid gloves, carried
off the vocal honors of the evening.
“The Barber of Seville” gave Scamuzzi a
great opportunity. As the merry rascal
Figaro he was in charge of the stage most
of the evening. Miss Gonsalez, though not
at her best as Rosina, was still a charming
minx who led her jealous old guardian
Bartolo the very devil of a life. Fooling
does not fit the stately Balboni, and he
wasn’t drunk enough to suggest anything
but a ginger-beer party. Cacialli fortified
the nonsense of the evening in deep notes
like the ultimate chord the organ lost in
the long ago. Petrucci gave a three-star
performance as the ancient dodderer, Bar-
tolo. This week the Gonsalez people are
warbling good-bye to Sydney in five revivals.
The gloomy fact must be faced that to-
morrow night “Romance” flees from Sydney
Criterion —probably for ever—carrying
beauteous Madge Fabian with it. On Satur-
day evening syphilis will be the sole attrac-
tion. The Firm, after much prodding by
good citizens, will produce Brieux’s famous
“Damaged Goods” for a brief season.
“To-night’s the Night” froths and bubbles
inside Sydney Majesty’s as though it were

The Garden Theatre (Sydney), where the
Red Dandies bloom, got its covering umbrella
fitted just in time. As this week’s issue of
Tiie Bulletin swims to press Noah’s
ancient flood yarn begins to take on a gleam
of possibility. Manager Branscombe de-
clares that his show is watertight and lies
on ball bearings, so that, if the waters cover
the face of the earth, the theatre will float
and. ablaze with lights and resounding
with songandance, will go rolling on to
Giory. No extra charge will be made.

Melbourne remembers many revivals of “A
Royal Divorce,” each of them, all things
considered, weaker than the one that went
before. The original picturesque array
of Napoleonic courtiers has dwindled down
to a small and shabby gathering; the fSte in
the Garden of the Tuilleries is a diminished
festivity; the horrors of war in the incidental
tableaux are not as appalling as they were
of yore. Moreover, familiarity continues to
breed contempt for the simple fustian dia-
logue of this artlessly theatrical drama,
wherein no character save the drunken inn-
keeper conveys any sense of realism to one’s
critical faculty. The characters do not live.
The performers merely act, and mostly
succeed in doing their bit fairly well in a
mechanical manner. All of which being
duly stated, the fact remains that “A Royal
Divorce,” on the strength of its subject
matter and its audacious perversions of the
truth about Josephine, has been a sure
money-getter for exactly 19 years. Money
talks. It testifies, as a rule, to the public’s
poor intelligence. But, anyhow, it Talks.
Napoleon Knight, the great military person-
age whose methods are now as obsolete as
those of Julius C;esar, and J. B. Atholwood,
the innkeeper who was licensed to be always
drunk on (lie premises, are the two reminders
of the original cast. Time has mellowed
them in their parts. Knight is all the more
Napoleonic for being rather thicker than he
used to be, and his testimony to the wondrous
courage of the English is a more popular
histrionic effort than ever it was. Miss Jean
Robertson is a tall and elegant Josephine
with a sweet, expressive voice. She looks
too young for Nap.’s first wife, and neglects
to dress her hair in accordance with histori-
cal pictures of that giddy lady ; but she has
stage dignity for half a dozen ill-used
empresses, and promises to be a more con-
vincing actress a year or two hence. Miss
Sparks is a tall and tolerably efficient Marie
Louise. Miss Anderson, ns Stephanie, is
bright and lively without being sparkling.
For the rest of the principal speaking parts
tl#re are Herbert Leigh as an utterly nn-
courtly Talleyrand, Boyd Irwin, Sweeney,
Stuart and Lloyd in becoming uniforms, and
Emma Temple as the nurse to a conven-
tional knickered stage girlie described as
Napoleon’s baby-boy.

At Melbourne Princess’s the wearisome
Follies of Pleasure have ceased to drag an
inch of puerile jest over a yard of space, and
the house is just now wrapped in silence.
On Saturday (fourth) Allan Wilkie and co.
return with their Sliakspearian repertoire.
The season opens with “Taming of the
Shrew,” in which Miss Hunter-Watts im-
personates the chastened Pankhurst of the
period.
At Melbourne King's until Friday night
parson Pliinmer will continue to vex his con-
gregation by introducing billiard-tables,
divorced ladies and other godless things into
the mushy ’Murkan parish of Lovers’ Lane.
IMi miner, by the way, is piling on adipose
tissue, and begins to look like a reverend
Socialistic endeavorer who gets toffee for
breakfast, likewise for tea. On Saturday the
Carroll and Beaumont Smith co. comes along
with “While the Billy Boils,” and the lamp
of Australian farcical drama will be re-
lighted.

“The Cinema Star” keeps on shining at
Melbourne Majesty’s, thanks to Florence
Young, Clyde Cook and Minnie Love in
particular, and a number of other considera-
tions in general. There have been no changes
in relative values since the first night, nor
is there any noticeable difference in the
plot. A lot of the entertainment has nothing
whatever to do with the plot, so a few altera-
tions in the career of Josiah Clutterbuck
would make no real difference, anyhow.
