Wirths’ Circus, whereof this paper has
already remarked that it is more exciting
than usual, will stay another two weeks in
Melbourne. Next week, being the Feast of
Horse, will be full of money for the circus,
and an extra matinee may be confidently
expected.
The one thing wanting in Melbourne
Tivoli bill is a special feature. Half of it
consists of The Strollers. Now, Sydney
James, Madeline Rossiter and their accom-
plices (including the dummy) are just a
pleasing smile rather than a feature. The
first part also lacks a protruding attraction.
The clown gymnasts, the marionettes, con-
jurer Merlin and the laconic strong man and
his balancing pal are good turns; the skaters
are satisfactory; the ’cellist and the dancing
fiddler bring up the rear. Cardo and Noll
and two or three other people will return on
Saturday.
Up to time of writing the Pink Dandies,
at St. Kilda (Vic.), have had no assistance
from the weather. Given a rise in the pre-
vailing temperature the tuneful little co.
could always get a warm reception. The
last change of programme was distinctly the
best up to date.
Albert Chevalier, the notable English
comedian, is appearing in a film called “The
Bottle” at Hoyt’s Melbourne house this
week. “The Bottle,” it may be said, is a
sermon on Derrink, and Albert has to gibber
fearsomely as the Dreadful Example. Bertha
Kalich, the beautifully-coiffured Roumanian
actress, is a welcome reappearer in a five-
part Fox item entitled “Ambition.” Bertha
is a luscious bribe in a matter affecting an
official appointment, but the gent, to whom
the lady is offered declines the gift. In the
third Hoyt feature, “The Flower of No
Man’s Land,” a winsome little actress, Viola
Dana, figures as a small prairie violet that
is doomed to fade in a bad man’s keeping.
A Sioux champion of betrayed virtue plays
a big part in the film and gives a thrilling
exhibition of stealthy Injun r-revenge.
Adelaide shows are quiet. Maggie Moore,
always herself and always welcome, at the
Tivoli; a strong variety co., with the Man-
churian athletes at the top, at the Majestic;
the pleasant Dandies at Austral Gardens.-
The rest pictures: the Fox variety at the
Royal, Tom Brown and his Schooldays at
Wondergraph, views of New York at West’s.
Some day a man who might find a worse
job will go round Australia and have a look
at the kinds of Public Enterprise various poli-
ticians have been playing with these last few
years. One of the “experiments” calling for
prompt examination is the State shipbuild-
ing yard in Vic. If this proceeds at the
present gait the whole population will soon
be toiling to keep the blessed thing going.
There seems to be a violent hurry-up with
everything except the jobs on hand. A tug
ordered in December, ’l3, on the understand-
ing that it would be delivered in nine months,
was completed in two years and two month's.
A launch to have been finished in six months
was a year and six months overdue. The
Melbourne Harbor Trust ordered a suction
dredge; but as the job had not been started
nine months after the contract was let, the
Trust wearily set about getting a machine at
another address.

“Kaniva”: I can go part of the way with
'"Fax” (B. 19/10/TG) regarding the Wil-
liamson chorus; hut I call a halt when he
says: “Australia isn’t producing the wealth
of voices that she did when Melba was a
girl.” Certainly, J.C.W. principals are not
so good; but as 90 per cent, of them are
imported, what bearing has that on Aus-
tralian voices? There are better voices in
the chorus at the present time than the last
half-dozen imported soloists iiave owned.
The G. and S. revivals of two years ago
were marked by atrocious vocalisation; it
was below the level of a sixpenny suburban
church concert. No wonder the ambitious
Australian girl, seeing no chance in alleged
comic opera, takes the first opportunity of
distinction in the seaside shows. When
Rosina Buckman appeared with the J.C.W.
management about six years back she easily
proved that no imported priraa donna of the
last 15 years was near her in talent. I
heard most of the native-born vocal products
of “Fax’s” golden age, and I don’t believe
the supply has run out or is in danger of
doing so. But not until Australia shows that
it wants its own sweet singers in preference
to the imported mediocrities will the locals
get the show that Violet Varley and the rest
of them secured.
Of course there has been a hubbub in
Adelaide over Melba’s comment on the way
the local Conservatorium has handled the
winner of the first Melba scholarship: “It
will take months to undo the results of pre-
vious training.” . Doris Leech, of whom this
is said to have been said, has risked the
consequences and declared she has had “the
very best training,” softening that, however,
with the observation that “no doubt my
voice has altered with cultivation”—this
apropos the verdict that she has been
trained as a contralto though she is natu-
rally a high soprano.
“Bob D.”: What about making the censor-
ship of plays and pictures a Federal affair?
At present it is in the hands of the different
States, and occasionally the Customs Depart-
ment butts in. This is the sort of thing
that results. J.C.W., Ltd., has received
permission to play “Damaged Goods,” but
when that piece was offered to a local film
concern for the screen 12 months ago the
Customs Department would not guarantee
its entry into the Commonwealth. The
Firm will be able to reach only a few thou-
sand Australians at most, whereas the cino.
would have preached the much-needed ser-
mon to several hundred thousand, and 12
months sooner.
Florence Rockwell, who is on her way to
Australia for J. C. Williamson, Ltd., has
been here half a dozen times already—on the
films. The six pictures in which she has
been seen have won her so much fiapperly
admiration that the Firm is dreaming of a
packed gallery every night.
Stella Cazalla, in private life Mrs. Ernest
Lashbrooke, died recently. Coming from
England she made her first visit to Maoriland
with the Wykeham-Noble company in 1!)12.
Subsequently she played in “The Blue Bird”
and “Humpty Dumpty.”
Betti Vanini, a noted Vienna opera singer
of other days, died recently in that city.
She was 102 years old or even less, and had
not appeared since the burning of the Ring
Theatre in Vienna in 1881. That tragedy,
one of the worst theatre fires in the world’s
history, in which 550 persons were killed, so
shattered Vanini's nerves that her career
was finished. The opera in progress was
Offenbach’s “Tales of Hoffmann,” and it was
not staged again for years.
John Glendinning, who came from America
with Nance O’Neil as Earl of Essex and
Ingomar 11 years ago, has just died in Eng-
land. He had an Australian connection
inasmuch as he was nephew of the late John
Thompson, for many years editor of the
Maitland Mercury. Born at Whitehaven,
Cumberland, in early life he was leading man
for Walter Bentley, F. R. Benson and the
Kendalls. He married the English actress
Jessie Milward, and was touring England
in “The Rosary,” in which he played Father
Kelly, until a few days before his death at
Cheltenham.
“Rosna” (London) : Essie Jenyns of
beloved memory is dwelling at Kingston
Hill these days and has rented a billiard-
room and garden next to her home, for the
use of Anzacs. She keeps open house for
the boys day in and day out, and pnmdes
them with games and tea and other diver-
sions.
The new South Aus. tax on amusements
came into force this week: a farthing on
threepenny tickets; halfpenny on every six-
pence of the higher-priced. Patriotic, reli-
gious and charitable affairs exempt, subject
to proper guarantees ; dog, flower and agri-
cultural shows exempt also.
English provincial music-hall managers
Avere in a fair way to make a pile of money
recently by exhibiting a sailor billed as “The
luckiest man in the world.” He was
announced as the sole survivor of the Bul-
Avark when site was bloAvn up at Sheerness;
as part of the salvage when the battleship
King Edward Avas mined; and as one of six
seamen picked up from the Invincible at
Jutland. Unfortunately chance turned dog on
him and his tour Avas interrupted by arrest
as an impostor. Even the old understanding
that there is luck in odd numbers went sour
on him when a cynical judge handed him
five years’ hard graft.

Billy Boils,” is quite satisfied with Ms share

have to practise war economy. The fire

Beaumont Smith Avill fan the ashes with his

the clothes were for the youngsters. Henry
doesn’t trouble much about neAV clothes for

Peter”: The threatened eminence of
Rosa Alba is a reminder of the late Alf
Vincent’s discernment. , Alf discovered the
soprano in Melbourne two or three years
ago, and in his quiet way proclaimed the
voice good. Moreover, he got a few disin-
terested pressmen to listen to the novice, and
soon the most guarded of them were declar-
ing that the artist had some excuse for his
enthusiasm. Yet, strangely enough, profes-
sional Melbourne critics never detected the
possibilities of Miss Alba. Musical pedan-
try is less kindly to a natural warble than
to a modest pipe finished in a music factory.
So the young artist was allowed to escape
from Melbourne without any newspaper
prophecies of future brilliant behavior.
•Tbsenshaw”: The aristocracy will not
bulk so largely in British-made plays after
the war. Pinero hardly ever wrote a piece
serious or comic, that did not contain a
lord, a duke, or at the very least a “right
honorable.” Henry Arthur Jones, who
would sooner die than be out of the fashion,
crowded his comedies with peers and sassiety
people and bishops’ aprons. R. C. Carton,
Sutro, Maugham, Barrie, Vachell—all sought
inspiration in the atmosphere of the “best
people.” The lives of commoners, full though
they be of the stuff of drama, were seldom
exploited. Not the gallery but the half-
guinea stall was played to. But —this war
again ! Two plays are to be seen in London
at the present time, both simple chronicles
of middle-class Lancashire families. One is
“Hobson’s Choice,” the other “The Rotters.”
Neither is by a fashionable author, yet they
are the successes of the season.
“C. Ockney”: General Smith-Dorrien has
an idea that the theatres might really help,
too, and not merely pretend to help by put-
ting- up a fat girl in a few clothes to sing
softgs composed of jingo mush. So he has
addressed a timely letter to certain theatri-
cal managers begging them to raise the tone
of their performances. Says this old hard
case: “I am convinced that our soldiers and
sailors would be the first to admit that, if
they were given the choice, they would pre-
fer performances which, while cheerful and
inspiriting, appealed to the best side of their
patriotic natures, and not exhibitions of
scantily-dressed girls and songs of doubtful
character.” Of course the trade journals
mostly regret that the General was not more
‘ specific,” and the spotless London mana-
gers, especially those staging revues, are
asking one another, “Does he mean you :”
“J.F.H.”: Our latest Referee publishes a
letter from Sydney written by Cecil Brooking
of the “Peg o’ My Heart” Co. He proclaims
its 100th performance in Sydney on July .10,
and adds: “This is a record run for Aus-
tralasia.” I don’t believe it. Asked to name
the Australian longest-distance performer, I
would, on the spur of the moment, reply
“Struck. Oil.” But whether the official
statistician on the spot would confirm that
guess is another matter.
There is always something to be thankful
for. Even if Australia will not fight it out,
the inflexible resolution to talk the German
to death remains unimpaired.
“Adelaide”: No use kicking, I suppose ;
but can anyone tell me why, living in South
Aus., and drawing a trifling dividend from
a Westralian mine, I should have to pay
five shillings in the pound English income
tax on it? Presumably the Great Boulder
head office is in London. Then why is it
there? Money earned and paid and spent
in Australia ought tq pay no English tax,
and it is up to somebody to find a way of
avoiding it.
Conscription has some unsuspected virtues.
For instance, one or two of the Melbourne
banks applied for the wholesale exemption
of young clerks who were alleged to be “in-
dispensable.” “Ha,” said Dwyer, P.M., pre-
siding at the Exemption Tribunal. “Let’s
see what wages you’re paying these indis-
pensable young men.” They told him, with
a cloud of explauations. “Needn’t go any
further,” said Dwyer in effect. “You’re fair
men, I’ll presume; and I’ll judge of the
indispensableness of these officers of yours
by the salaries you’re paying them—mostly
about the same as an unskilled laborer’s.”

November 4.

It looks as though Victoria will struggle
along presently without a statistician. Pub-
lic attention is being violently drawn to the
foolishness of keeping a State tallying staff
to do nothing that doesn’t come within the
sphere of Commonwealth Statist Knibbs.
The office has lost its original excuse for
existence, and its officers might be employed
in teaching the clerks of other departments
how to put in their time.
Herbert Hughes, buried last week at Glad-
stone (S.A.), where he had lived, was the
eldest son of another of the same name who
left a lot of sons prominent as squatters.
This one stuck to the old Booyoolie run in
S.A., and the brothers are scattered about the
Darling and in Queensland. Herbert White
Hughes was known to the public chiefly for
his immensely massive build, and to his inti-
mates for his unsuspected knowledge of art.
He had been on the land ever since he left
Oxford. Losing first his wife, and then his
only son (by an accident at school), he lived
more quietly than ever till the end came at
61.
Once more the conscientious objector—a
Sydney exhibit this time: —
He was a strict vegetarian. He did not believe in
taking any man’s life, and he objected to the killing
of harmless animals.
Quite right. But there are always the
onions to peel and the washing-up to do and
the latrines to look after. A country which
wants cooks and scavengers and then turns
away somebody simply because he isn’t a
butcher, is only half-organised.
“Catkin” from the Mud: —
Here's another one for whoever is collecting war
yarns. We were in the middle of the biggest rough-
up we’ve had, and the numerous Headquarters Staffs
were working at highest pressure. The Adjutant of the
—th Brigade, which was in the thickest part of it,
was up to his ears in papers. Suddenly a motorist
tore up at full speed, dropped his machine on the
road, bolted into the office and threw down an
urgent message on the table. “Adjy” dropped his
other papers and ripped open the envelope. The
urgent message read: “Please note that the —nd
Battalion sent in an incorrect form 213. One mule
is not accounted for.”
And here’s one more. Just up the road is a “dud”
shell—in other words, a shell that didn’t explode.
Such spots are marked with a notice “Blind
Shells.” A cheerful assassin has nailed a bully-
beef tin with a slit in the top on the board, and
added “Please assist.”
“0.A.5.”: War grim? Not when such
joyous items as this are part of it. It came
in reply to our C.O.’s request to the
A.D.M.S. for an increase in our daily
“manger” (French for grub) :—•
The Field Service ration is built upon a liberal
scale, and provides a large margin to replace the
wear and tear of men employed under the trying
circumstances of war conditions.
The high proportion of protein in the dietary
enables the ration to meet the needs of tissue repair,
and in the present place of warfare the men should
have no difficulty in supplementing their rations
locally should they require to do so.
I would suggest the officer commanding taking
steps to weigh the men weekly (without clothes)
and furnishing a report at the end of the month.
Would you believe it, not one of us had
thought of bringing a set of scales or even
a weighbridge? Kindly forward one so that
we can ascertain if we really are hungry.
A send-off to one we can’t afford to lose: —
An acetylene-gas explosion on Sunday at Litligow
robbed Australia of one of its brightest young men,
Guildford Hoskins, eldest son of Charles, the plucky
and persistent ironmaster of Lithgow. Those who
knew something of the running of the Lithgow
works realise that “Gill” had got past the stage of
being the son of his father, and promised to be of
incalculable benefit to Australia in its struggle for
industrial life. The industrial birth of Australia
really occurred when Charles Hoskins and his three
sons took hold of Sandford’s works. Then began a
technical struggle which slowly and surely is earning
for the Charles Hoskins family the admiration of
every patriot who knows what is happening. The
task has been herculean, but bit by bit the old
scrap heap has been transformed into a systematised
plant, and before Australia had realised what some
of her sons were doing the Hoskinses were turning
out steel rails. To those who knew, the most
picturesque sight at the much-advertised opening of
the B.H.P. Co. Steel Works, at Newcastle, was
Charles Hoskins and Ids late son gazing down upon
that well-fed assemblage while it was being told
what a fine thing the B.H.P. had done in starting
the industry of making our own rails. Possibly the
Hoskinses went back from that gathering «nd asked
their statistician what their total outpui of rails
had been to that date; but if they did they never
mentioned it. The Hoskinses are not talkers, and at
this stage in our history, when the chief output of
the country appears to be sloppy rhetoric, a high-
minded doer like Guildford Hoskins can ill be spared.
THUMBNAIL SKETCHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Blue skies, ablaze with joy, that gleam and sing;
Glad winds with saltish bite a little gritty ;
Brave ardors free that love and have their fling;
Fair hope that glamor sheds on everything;
Hot youth, exuberant, hand in hand with pity ;
Strong faith that sucks the honey from death’s sting—
And this is Sydney city.
A something strangely bilious in the air
That grips the host of houses grey and brown;
A sort of smartness peeping from its lair,
A haunting laughter shrill that mocks despair ;
Columbine weeping on the knees of Clown,
While Pantaloon bids all the loves beware —
And this is Melbourne town.
A golden" heat that stings in all one’s bones
And grills one’s skin till it were fit to eat
By some strayed eager cannibal named Jones ;
A heat that screams and has no undertones;
And then a blaze of color, and a sheet
Or" incandescent sunbeams hard as stones—
And this is Brisbane street.
An air of velvet, slothful and demure,
That like a blanket lies upon the staid
Still swelter of the streets ; white sunlight pure
That beats upon the brain; depth of black shade
That dreams of purple; buzz of sober trade,
Mid conscientious scruples grown mature
And quite incurable; fair dreams that fade;
Morality the popular manure—
And this is Adelaide.
A brazen blinking river strangely flat;
A roystering town too broad about the girth;
Fierce thirsts, long begrs, a shilling .in the hat;
A howling bard who terrifies the cat ;
Ten pious Christian souls who salt the earth
And prate of Hell, and persons doomed to that—
And this is Perth.
VICTORIA.
DOROTHY BRUNTON.
THE NEW HOMAGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A popular Buda-Pesth actress, Hansi Niese, in
acknowledging' applause after a performance at a
Vienna theatre one night last week, expressed a pre-
ference for provisions rather than bouquets as tri-
butes from the audience. Her wish is being regarded,
as since her announcement she has been nightly
presented with flour, rice and eggs.— Cable.
“Once,” said the mime, “if on the stage
You’d thrown a cabbage green,
Or eggs, 1 would have cried with rage,
As at an insult keen ;
But artists proud, when feeling hollow,
Will gladly such an insult swallow.
“So, if you’d do me homage, friends,
Then bring to me, I beg,
Not roses red, but food. Who sends
Some frankfurts or a leg
Of lamb respects my inclination,
And fills me thus—with admiration!”
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
“A.G.” introduces his pal '“Bluey,” a
Gallipoli identity, now of Flanders: —-
lie hopped, skipped and leaped into our shell-
hole while Fritz strafed us with good Broken Hill
lead.
“Strike me pink!” says Bluey. “Me luck’s stiff,
Here was me coming out of me dugout when Ted
Dougan pushes past me, then drops. ‘Bluey,’ he
chortles, ‘l’ve got a wound, and I’m going ter
Blighty.’ If the cow hadn’t duckshoved me I would
have had a trip to Blighty meself.”
Last time Bluey went to Blighty he did not alto-
gether approve of the rules and regulations in the
convalescent camp. It was the parade after pay-day,
and we did a light march to a handy villlage, where
the mob had a quencher. Bluey had a few extra
and then disappeared. We nearly spring-cleaned the
village looking for him, and were just giving him up
JUDGED BY LOOKS.
SHE: “Does he sell many pictures?"
HE: “Oh, he says he only paints for Iris own
amusement.”
SHE: “ Indeed! Tie must paint badly.”
when the oldest inhabitant suggested looking round
the churchyard. Sure enough Bluey was there, beer-
fully reposing in the shade beneath an old time-pay-
ment tombstone. As a punishment he was put on
guard that night on No. 3 post. “Visiting rounds”
came and asked the usual questions ; also, what would
Bluey do in the event of Zeppelins coming along?
Bluey hiccuped twice, then announced that he would
“ ’and ’em over to the Quartermaster.” No. 3
post was suddenly relieved.
Bluey looked like getting a stripe after the Push
until this morning, when he was up before the O.C.
charged with looting a duck. The indictment was
read. What did he have to sav on the matter ?
“It was this way, sir,” says Bluey. “I sees a
duck, and asks her to quack for Australia. She
wouldn’t quack, so I took her prisoner.” “Case dis-
'missed,” says the O.C. “About turn, quick march.”
Bluey did a lively double.
“Billjim’s Brother”: There are tricks in
every trade. When an unsophisticated re-
cruity was put on sentry duty at our camp,
and conscientiously stuffed the guardroom
with every man who arrived after the
correct hour, he found that he was rapidly
getting unpopular. It was then that aii ex-
perienced non-com. tipped him the wink. “If
they’re sober,” he said, “there’s no sense in
putting ’em in the guardroom. It only fills
if up and gives trouble. Take ’em round
behind the guardroom, and see if they have
any fish an’ chips on ’em. They nearly
always have, and then you can punish ’em
yourself.” There’s no end of “Manuals for
Soldiers,” but never a one that tells ’em
half the things that every gnarled old ser-
geant-major knows.
* * * *
“Kuscobin”: However our cannibal bro-
ther’s soul may be progressing under the
philanthropic coaching of the London Mis-
sionary Society, his body could apparently
do with a little extra attention. The Rev.
Obeda, of Vaipae, Cook Islands, died there
the other day, aged 95, and the report of his
death states: “Maggots were actually
crawling over his body before death, and
there was no one to nurse him.” Obeda
was a retired native pastor, and had spent
the best part of his life at the job in Samoa
and other islands of the Western Pacific.
Yet, when the Resident Agent approached
the representatives of the L.M.S. for an
allowance for the destitute Obeda, he was
told that the Society had no fund for the
pensioning of native pastors. The old man’s
end, coupled with this disclaimer of any
responsibility with regard to worn-out
native helpers, is grima facie such an abom-
inable scandal that the L.M.S. must make
its defence. Silence on the subject will
brand it as a body far too dangerous to- the
white man’s prestige to be permitted in our
islands.
“Aneb” : Is the Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals still dead? And do
the police continue to go about with one eye
shut? Attend any sale of stock in a dairy-
ing centre, and you'll see newly-born calves
under various forms of torture to keep them
from following Nature’s great call—jam-tins,
with serrated edges, tied over nose and
mouth; liay-band tied tightly round nose;
filthy old socks drawn over mouth and nos-
trils ; and, lately, fine galvanised tying wire
is becoming the fashion. Cows with abnor-
mally distended udders, milk oozing out of
bursting teats in constant drip, or, when
moved, in a stream, low in pain. Experi-
ence tells me that those calves haven’t been
fed, or those cows eased, for at least 24
hours. Even if cow is the curse of Aus-
tralia. in the name of our common human-
ity, don’t let us treat her with Hunuish
brutality.
“Try Fluke”: Following the example of
the Ngati-Tuwharetoa tribe, of the Taupo
Country, who gave the M.L. Government
25,000 acres of the Owlmoko block free of
all conditions for soldiers of the native race,
the Taihape section of the tribes interested
in the block is considering what it can do in
the same way. There was an Oriental
touch of courtly self-depreciation in the
Taupo people’s description of the present as
“our insignificant gift.” On the East Coast
of the North Island 2000 acres have been
given for the soldiers; the land has been
stocked with sheep, and a returned soldier
has been put in charge, to carry on until the
boys come home. . Meanwhile sundry pale-
face owners and syndicates are offering
backblocks areas to the State at full market
rates.
“The Old Soldier”: I’ve been in a few
scraps when we were short-handed, and I
wish some of our no-compulsion angels had
been handy. They’d have been cured of their
freak. Before Sebastopol, in the winter of
1854-5, the French kept their conscript army
up to 70,000 men, but England’s voluntary
service lot was at one time holding the same
length of trenches with only 12,000 fighters
fit for duty. They held it, but was it right
to put such a strain on the gallant few?
Then, in the Indian. Mutiny two years later.
20,000 British soldiers were face to face
with 200,000 native troops, as well armed,
disciplined and trained as themselves. Again
the weak force pulled through, but if any-
body thinks that that sort of thing is a fair
go I’m sorry for him. His grandmother
must have lived in a ‘tree.
Plain lack of imagination makes seeming
cowards of a lot of men. The Victorian who
told an exemption court that he would
merely “call for help” if a man tried to
ravish his mother probably libelled himself
cruelly. The most likely explanation is
that it simply isn’t in him to visualise a
domestic or national horror; therefore, when
presented with the theoretical case, he makes
himself look contemptible, whereas faced
with the actual circumstances he would no
doubt behave as creditably as the average
decent man.

From a Bulletin Billjim in Egypt:—
J»
The Light Horse, after some strenuous arguments
with Abdul, are waiting for that misguided heathen
to come again. At first the son of the Prophet didn’t
realise that he wasn’t up against Yeomanry ; when he
did he suddenly recalled an urgent appointment else-
where. I heard a German n.c.o. —one of six who
had served machine-guns magnificently on August 5
until the Turks had all scooted —ask our fellows if
they were English. Quite a pleasant expression flitted
over the Huns’ faces when they found they had not
surrendered to Englishmen. 1 saw these same Ger-
mans turn their machine-guns on surrendering Tftrks
three times on August 5. In conversation subse-
quently the Teutons said that the rapid movements
of the Light Horse took them by surprise and gave
them no chance to get their guns back. These Ger-
mans with the Turks are picked men, many of them
having won the Iron Cross in Europe.
HADN’T COME HER WAY.
HE: “Of course we'll keep our engagement
dark for the present."
SHE: “Yes. But where's the present?"
Exit finally the home in which Adam
Lindsay Gordon lived at Glenelg (S.A.),
while better known as steeplechase-rider and
eccentric M.L.A. than as poet. It was a
cottage in a large garden. The former dis-
appeared years ago, and five new houses
have now finally disguised the identity of
the place. So no more is likely to be heard
of the occasional suggestion—never really
actively entertained —to “preserve” it.
The Law may be an ass, but it knows how
to pursue its oats. A while back William
Howell, herdsman of Shepparton (Vic.)
Common, proceeded against Roger Bullen,
and succeeded in having him fined £5 55.,
with £5 7s. 6d. costs. Bullen appealed and
the Supreme Court decided that the little
men were wrong, remitted the fine and
ordered Howell to pay all the costs, some
£26. Commonsense would seem to demand
that the magistrates who made the bloomer,
or the “Crown” which appointed them,
should pay the costs thereof; but the Law
doesn’t view matters from that standpoint.
Howell, either because of financial strin-
gency—few herdsmen are millionaires —or
because he thought his employers, the
owners of the public common, should part up,
omitted to pay the £26; so Bullen, through
his lawyer, asked Justice Hood to issue an
order for the attachment of the person of
Howell. His Honor hesitated a while, re-
marking that Howell “had not committed
any wrong nor been guilty of any crime, yet
it was asked that he be sent to gaol. Still
he would -not depart from established prac-
tice,” and ordered William to be “attached”
as asked. If Howell is well advised he will
pay the cash in the time specified, because
this is obviously a serious matter. He
might owe his butcher £26 for meat which he
himself had actually consumed, or even £260,
and there would be no thought of sending
him to gaol. But Costs is an obstreperous
beast, with tremendous force behind it. and
summary ways of getting what it considers
its due ; and even a Judge who sees the in-
justice and unfairness of its methods isn’t
game to defy it.
■* * * *
Who says the Hun hasn’t got a sense of
humor? Luderitzbucht, the town the Ger-
mans of South-West Africa retreated from
across 600 miles of sand and forest, had a
population of 3000, and 400 of them were
Flossies. When the British were at hand
the Germans abandoned these women, after
telling them horrible lies about what would
happen to them when the place was taken.
Subsequently a Hun aviator flew across the
captured town and released a makeshift
bomb consisting of a 4.7 shell and a small
parachute to keep it right end up. When
the 8000 troops in camp saw the parachute
inflate and steady the falling shell, a great
howl of joy went up. The parachute con-
sisted of a pair of something looted from a
lady’s wardrobe, and the blue ribbons with
which they were threaded fluttered coquet-
tishly in the breeze. As it happened, .the shell
didn’t explode, and some representatives of
the Witwatersrand Rifles won the race to
the spot and secured the dainty trifle for
the regiment’s messroom.
SUMMER SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oil, piper of the breathing world,
Blow warm, blow warm on me!
A little crescent cloud is curled
Above a dimpled sea.
A little mouth is lightly laid
Against my mouth to kiss —•
The Brown Girl, laughing unafraid,
Is queen of hours like this.
Spring with a rout of urchins ran
Past aged Winter hoar —
I saw a chill wind lig-htly fan
Her tattered pinafore.
But Summer is a woman grown
For red-haired suns to woo ;
Her days and nights have madly known
All tunes the piper blew.
Oh, piper of the hidden ways,
Blow warm, blow warm on me!
The rose lights haunt the happy days,
The hushed, sweet nights are free.
The Brown Girl lays a dimpled cheek
Against my ruffled hair,
And wise, brave joys play hide-and-seek
With truth and cash and prayer !
N.S.W.

HOW TO GET THICK, GLOSSY HAIR.
No matter how careful you may be of
your hair —how often or how faithfully you
may shampoo or brush it—the hair cannot
attain the thick glossy brilliance nature
intended for all men, women and children
so long as the dandruff germ is sapping the
strength and vitality of the hair roots. Fre-
quent brushing of the hair and a shampoo
once every two or three weeks are both
valuable aids to gaining hair beauty, but
they cannot destroy the dandruff germ. Prac-
tically the only way to destroy this germ
is to rub into the scalp twice a day a mix-
ture prepared by mixing 3oz. Bay Rum with
7} -dram Menthol Crystals and loz. Lavona
de Composee. As this preparation not only
destroys the dandruff germ, but is also a
truly marvellous hair grower, it should be
applied only where hair is desired. Any
chemist can prepare the above formula, or
you can procure the ingredients and mix
them at home; but you must use it regu-
larly, and be sure to brush your hair fre-
quently and give it an occasional shampoo.
Avoid the frequent use of hot curling-tongs,
as they tend to make the hair dull and life-
less. — Advt.

“F.A.Q.”: I can’t understand this Conser-
vative objection to the collection of soldiers’
fingerprints. It isn’t only a criminal’s test.
Some Yankee banks require thumbprint as
well as signature on large cheques ; and in
India fingerprints are used by the civil ser-
vice examining boards. When Uncle Wood-
row’s navy enlists crews, the prints are
taken, not merely to catch deserters, but to
prevent undesirables enlisting or re-enlisting.
Two Anzaes may gaily swap identification
discs, but they can’t swap thumb signatures.
Ilair and eyes may change color, features
alter; but from childhood until death, a fid
after death, the thumb-stamp is unalterable
except by a surgeon.

“Copy Boy”: October 25 was the tercen-
tenary of the discovery—or re discovery,
perhaps of the west coast of Australia by
I)irk Ilartog. The Dutch mariner was on
his way to Batavia, and, having run further
east than he should have done, spotted a
strange coastline on the horizon and
promptly marked it on his chart, naming it
Eendracht’s Land, after his own ship. Later.
Dutch navigators ran against other parts of
our coastline and presented them with
Dutch names—Landt van Pieter Nu’yts, de
Wit’s Landt and Edel’s Landt—and the first
name used to embrace the whole was New
Holland. Ten years earlier than Dirk
Hartog’s discovery, in 1606, a Dutch yacht
had poked round the Gulf of Carpentaria,
and the little bit of this continent that came
into view was set down on the maps of the
period as part of the Indies.

“Rag”: A tip for Billjim. Although
natives of the South Sea Islands are continu-
ally exposed to bad weather, rheumatism
rarely attacks them. European medical men
have the backing of Brown Brother when
they say that cocoanut-oil well rubbed in
does the trick. What about the girls of the
Pacific starting a cocoanut-oil fund for the
boys at war?

“Ilassan”: Nine years ago I left Sydney
tor my own good. Three months ago I
returned, for Sydney’s good. In the in-
terim, I am shocked to observe, Sydney has
become _an American city. Half of its
population talks through its nose, and the
other half through its neck. It is infested
with American pictures, American soft-
drinks and American pugilists and vaude-
ville performers. Once upon a time it was
Australian, but now it is surely qualified to
vote in the Presidential elections. It is all
strange and painful to me. I do not like
Americans. I hate their Notes and the way
they cut the hair on their necks. Some of
my friends have taken advantage of my
absence to go for short trips to America.
Now they talk about “cutting no ice,” and
stunts, and say ‘Sure!” at least once in
every sentence. Ohidley may or may not be
mad, but at least he isn’t American and I
love him for it. The Americans are the
prime vulgarians of the earth, as anybody
who has heard their music and read their
newspapers must recognise. Why should
Sydney want to imitate them when it could

imagine. Sydney’s harbor is all right, but
even _it has been Americanised by electric

until I see how deeply the American blight
has bitten.
“F.C.”: Among other achievements the
war has stressed the need for equality of
sacrifice in peace times, as well as when the
call is for men and money. To-day we are
putting the sons of all Australian parents
into camp to help defend their country, but
we exempt the only sons. Now, what Aus-
tralia wants is large families of sons and
daughters too; but unless she encourages
them in a practical manner she is likely to
get more only sons and daughters than is
at all good for her. One way to discourage
these often pampered individuals or rather
to encourage the parents with the quiverful,
would be by an adjustment of the income
tax so that the father of three children should
pay considerably less than the father of one,
while the parents of six or more jewels
should be exempt from the tax altogether.
The father of many, whether they be boys
or girls, is not only more patriotic than the
progenitor of one, but he unavoidably pays a
much larger sum to the State in' indirect
taxation. It isn't the only son’s fault that
he is one, but it is very often the result of
design on his parents’ part, and it would do
these latter good to be touched up a bit.
Ihe parent of the only son receives a lot
more consideration than he deserves.
“0.K.” remembers a hanging :
Marshall Lyle’s long talk (B. 28/9/’l6) about the
late Albert Deeming and the suppression by the
police of certain evidence of the man’s mental in-
firmity set me thinking of the days when 1 heard
him sentenced to death and subsequently saw him
hanged and hanging. Friend Lyle states that the
evidence which might have saved Deeming from the
gallows was supplied in “cables from the lunatic
asylum he had escaped from seven years before, the
reports of his treatment as a child at a home for
mentally-afflicted children, and numerous police
reports confirming his previous insanity.’’ But would
this evidence have saved Deeming? Nobody sup-
posed that the flash, boastful bounder who had
developed a mania for wife murder, and had also
murdered his children in England, could have been
sane all the time. And nobody who saw him done
to death in Melbourne Gaol seemed to think that
the question as to the exact measure of his mental
infirmity mattered twopence. We all thought that
thing to do with Deeming was to put him
out of the world, and we all felt that the last sen-
tence of the law is a humiliating business for every-
body present except the man who dies. lie is the
helpless sufferer for his offence against society-an
offence for which he is np more responsible, maybe
tine'ss a ” Chlld responsible fol ' its instinctive “naugh-
After the execution I was “privileged” to stay
awhile in the gaol and hear some facts concerning
the deceased. I was present when the late Dr Pat
Maloney called attention to the different markings
on the two sides of the corpse’s countenance, sug-
gesting that Deeming was mild and harmless on one
side and wicked as a monkey on the other. The
man had told the . doctor, it appeared, that when he
felt impelled to murder his wife he had the strength
ot a lion, and that when his fit of ferocity was

the warders in sentencing this cunning and occasion-
y murdeious animal to imprisonment for life or
would it have been fair to womankind to let him
work out his release some 20 years later?
This present war-time seems quite the wrong time
for attaching any value to the lives of men who are
murderous lunatics occasionally. A bishoD h*i«
stated that “the duty of civilisation is to kill Ger

anyhow. Being married and anxious to
escape 41.:> creditors, Hughes’s announcement
that all men able to serve would be wanted
sounded to me like news of a win in Tatt.’s.
l gave three cheers to go on with when some-

TROOPSHIP A[?].
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Jliin mists—those grey rememberings of night—
Crept from the burnished brow of golden dawn,
And from the harbor came a quivering call.
The fairy music of an unseen band
Broke through the wall of dreams, and people turned
And wondered, and the light
Peered, glimmering, round the blinds, and sleepy
eyes
Opened and closed again, and to the Heads
And to the strange blue mystery beyond
Troopship A4O took the dancing tides.
And “So-long, Letty!” played the merry band,
\\ hile the young voices caught up the refrain,
And khaki figures lined the crowded decks,
And waving women on the long brown quays
Stood stiff, as though saluting, keeping back
Their tears, and in the gardens of North Shore
Red roofs gleamed wetly, and the purple hills
And little bays of tender lavenders
Sent back the joyous echoes of the song.
I woke. I slept again. I did not know
That you were on ftiat boat that took the tide.
N.S.W.
M. FORREST.
LABOR-SAVING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Good-night, my own, I’m thinking of you yet—
That was my day of days. I can’t forget
How green the trees, how turquoise blue the sea ;
It was an earth new-born for you and me.
Ah, how we wandered simply hand in hand,
As Eve and Adam might have, through the land
Fragrant with bloom, a-swoon with heavenly bliss —
Something of that we tasted in our kiss.”
The second copy’s done, a goodly scheme;
I’ll to my bed, there’s morning’s first pale beam.

camp....

stamp!

NOT HIS JOB.
MOTHER: “Well, my boy, be sure and keep

Now that it’s all over, we might as well
hiive a handy work of reference so that we

in Australia”?
Dead in his prime, Jim McCrorey, ex-
trucker, navvy, miner and company-promoter,
blessed with a grand frame and as lithe as
a panther, he fought 'for the pure love of
nght through every railway camp in the
States.. Then he took to alluvial work, and
soon picked up the fact that early news and
a quick turn in the market meant pocketing
more profits in a day than you could get by
shoving a rake of trucks for a year. So
ivicCrorey punched his way into the Mel-
bourne market and thence into the Board-
room. Here his Irish wit and nimble brain
Sot him into the front rank. There wasn’t a
Y ictorian alluvial mine he didn’t have a go
at, but the Maryborough claims attracted
him most —Main Leads, Main Leads North.
Duke Extended, Chalks No. 3, and lastly his
big float, Duke and Main Leads Extended.
On the Cathcart boom he floated Cathcart
Central, and scored his biggest win. Langi
South may be another trump card, but the
Grim Reaper got in with a better hand.
Norfolk Island is a little object-lesson in
economy waiting on the blue Pacific shelf
until a sobered Australia has time to con-
sider it. Administrator M. V. Murphy hails
from Sydney; and he runs the
speck so well that the cable which
leans on it for a rest never mur-
murs a complaint. Perhaps it’s an
advantage to be a surveyor—you
know just where to draw the line.
A genial, appi'oachable Australian,
Murphy has a hatred of “side” and
silk hats. Apropos, a whaleboat of
reverent belltoppers went out one
stormy day to a visiting cruiser to
pay a courtesy call on a Governor,
and found his Ex. waiting to re-
ceive in sensible tweeds. Murphy
was the only one of the boatload
similarly dressed. He had tipped
that there would be no cocked hat that day
and had taken the risk like a good sport.
t Retired from the N. S. Wales District
Court Bench, Charles Murray, Judge, at the
age of 74. He had been handing out law
for 3S years, and did it with microscopic
attention to detail. “You say you had thrup-
pence,” said Murray once to a witness.
“Yes.” “Well, was it in one coin or three
separate coppers?” And it didn’t matter id.
one way or the other. But he was very
sound in his law, and to the frowsy, badly-
environed prisoner in the dock usually merci-
ful. As a youngster Murray did brilliant
things at Sydney University. He was there
a contemporary of Sam Griffith’s, and with
him was bracketed for a medal after doing
Aveird things with elliptical triangles and
quadrilateral equations. When he started
in pursuit of Costs on circuit he developed
a passion for walking and generally arrived
at the next place well ahead of the other
Gent, who waited for the coach. On those
occasions the early bird captured the brief,
and the other bird felt very much annoyed.
After getting on the Bench he contracted a
dislike to the court orderly administering
the oath in the raucous tones of a wil’-rabbi’
specialist, and insisted upon doing the swear-
ing himself with great decorum. He is very
likeable, and equally thin, and addicted to
colds under the wig.
Docker—of “the little brown jug”—the
Judge who has sustained more attacks than
all the rest of the N. S. Wales Bench put
together, is four months older than Judge
Murray, who at 74 has just decided that he
is old enough to retire. But, then. Murray
always was a better Judge than Docker.
Three Sydney barristers who are half-
Avay up the ladder are showing what sort of
Judges they would make —Pickburn, Boyce
and Coyle. Pickburn is the favorite for the
first vacancy—caused by Judge Murray’s
retirement—but at least one other seat is
likely to be empty soon. All three are
home-grown, though Coyle has some super-
added English training.
The estate of the late Lord Clifford
of Chudleigh has been sworn at
£69,104, and the heavy English death
duties will make it a good deal less
before the mature-whiskered heir from
the Flyspeek has had a look round the old
place. Still Clifford is the sort of baron
who wouldn’t mind taking a job to make up
his proud old butler’s stipend.
An ancient named Dermody made a re-
appearance at Ballarat City Court the other
day charged at his own request with having
insufficient means of support. “With the
walls of a prison around me I would be
happy,” he said. He was not always so
content to be “inside.” Forty years or more
ago Dermody was one of the party of half
a dozen who broke out of Ballarat gaol
under the direction of “Captain Moonlight.”
As he had recently objected forcibly to the
restraint of the Benevolent Asylum, the
Bench obliged the veteran with three months
at his old home.
Dead in Adelaide, at 65, Adam Adamson,
accountant, liquidator of many companies,
and best known in private life as having got
together one of that literary city’s best
private libraries.
Yon can’t kill a sailor —you can only
drown him. Gunner (“Boxer”) Markham,
a late Imperial Navy man, was reported ill
at liabaul, missing in the Gallipoli Landing
and killed at Lone Pine. Libels, two at
least of them! He enlisted in the A.I.F. the
other day, the livest and most present dead-
and-missing Billjim that any Fritz could
hope not to meet.
In the advt. of an anti-conscription pro-
paganda meeting held at the Melbourne
Trades Hall on a recent Saturday for “revo-
lutionary unionists and P.L.G. members,”
the convener’s address was frankly given as
Coward-street, Footscray.
PAPA’S ADVICE.
“Papa, Dr. Brown-Smith told me I ought to
go away for a holiday. Where do you
think I should go?”
“To another doctor.”
Retired from the Vie. Railway service last
week. Peter Hughes, the North Melbourne
stationmaster, perhaps the only man left in
the service who had worked for the old
Hobson Bay Co. which the Government
bought out. And it was a mighty good co.
to work for, says veteran Hughes, though it
served the public pretty badly. His recol-
lections of Melbourne are interesting re-
minders of what has happened to the city
within only 56 years. He was once lost for
a day in the bush between South Melbourne
and Prahran, and was found by aborigines who
were taking opossums and native bears to
town. The Albert Park district was the
last camping ground for the blacks within
what is now the Melbourne radius. There
was virgin forest all around the lake where
naval scouts now learn the rudiments of
navigation, and black folk held corroboree
on the site now desecrated by a statue to
the late caterer Skinner.
An old bold mate of the sea passed out
when Captain W. R. Barwood died. His
halcyon days wefe with the clipper ships,
running between Australia and port o’
London town. For 83 years he was on this
run under the house flag of T. B. Walker.
The “old man’’ never had an accident until
he came to the Rock of Ages last week.
“Chudah”: Baronet Jacob Elias Sassoon,
whose death was reported a few days ago,
came of a unique family, next in wonder to
the Rothsehildren. Jacob Elias was grand-
son of David, who migrated from Bagdad
to Bombay, inspired by the example of Parsi
Zorastrian fire-worshippers, and did better
than any of them. His son Albert, another
alert gentleman, founded the London branch
of the business. He became a baronet in
IS9O, and rivalled Hirsch and Cassel in
entertaining Edward Prince of Wales, both
in London and Brighton. But Rube, Albert’s
younger brother, was even a more expansive
host. Jacob Elias was the boss at Bombay,
which was always regarded as headquarters.
He obtained an independent baronetcy in
1909. The family stretches over Bagdad,
Shanghai, Japan and United States.
The loyal Australians of old German name
in S. Aus. are paying a heavy toll. George
Reinecke (lately Chief Clerk in the Tram-
ways Trust) left Adelaide as a sergeant, and
earned his commission in the field a month
or two before he was killed. He was the
only son of C. A. Reinecke, himself also Aus-
tralian born, auditor for the Broken Hill
Prop. Co.
Sam See, a Huntingdonshire man, died
in Sydney last week after a long Jllness.
His family came to Australia in 1853 and
settled on the,Hunter, where, they farmed.
There were four brothers, one of whom was
John, a former N.S.W. Premier. The Sees
later on settled on the Clarence, where they
commanded the trade of the river. John
engaged in shipping business in Sydney,
while the brothers looked after the interests
of the firm of Nipper and See on the North-
ern Rivers. With Sam gone, but one of the
four, it is understood, remains.
“Kai-Pia”: An early victim of the anti-
“shouting” regulation in Maoriland was
Mita Taupopoki, the boss man of the Tuhou-
rangi tribd, in the village of the geysers,
Whakarewarewa. Old Mita is a picturesque
figure of Rotorua —a tall, white-bearded
patriarch, with a wild eye and a tremendous
flow of poetic oratory, lie has a beautiful
carved house at Whakarewarewa; he can
reel off his pedigree for five-and-twenty
generations; he carried carbine and toma-
hawk for Queen Wikitoria in Te Kooti’s
rebellious days, and he has wagged an in-
credibly agile tongue in the cause of Maori
recruiting for the present war. Now this
patriot of patriots has to fork out £2O for a
breach of the Jimmy Woodser law. It’s a
great country. May its fads increase and
multiply so that the neighbors be not down-
hearted and the ribald continue to go about
their business!
“Loco”: A word in favor of the just-dead
C. Alison Smith, the Maorilander who
helped to make Speight an expensive rail-
way commissioner for Victoria. Perhaps
Smith did scrap too much rolling stock. But
he could build as well as destroy. His “Y,”
“RY” and “AA” locomotives are to-day
doing good work, though nearly 30 years
old. Many a driver has blessed the name of
Alison Smith, as he struggled up Oliver’s
bank, on the North-east, or Sunbury, on the
Main line. When he had an “RY” under
him he knew “she” could do it if anything
could. Ask any driver to-day to take his
choice between a “Y” and a “HD” for a. load
of goods to Woodend. and the “DD” will be
left in the shed every time. Some future
Alison Smith will scrap the “DD” and
“A 2” locomotives before they have dione
nearly the work of the old “Y,” “RY” and
“AA” classes. And the relative cost of
repairs? Well, ask any running shed
mechanic about that!
Just-retired N.S.W. District Court
Judge Murray started his law career as
associate to Justice Wise, father of the
just-deceased Bernhard.

never once told her she teas too good
for him.”
THE OGRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
Once every clay an elf of three
Comes stealing up to spy
In at the open door at- me,
And timorously cry:
“’Ole ’Oppy ’Opkins! ’Oppy ’Op!”
Then turn about and fly.
Her face is like a guarded rose,
Her hair all Autumn dyes,
Her brown eye with the ardor glows
Of awesome enterprise,
And in her face sweet impishness
With mortal terror vies.
“’Ole ’Oppy ’Op!” She chatters much
As raffish boys have done,
For moving- round upon a crutch
I dot and carry one.
I dimly in my corner sit,
While she is in the sun.
I sew the kip, and see the glow
Of sun and swelling tree,
And love the wind, the water’s flow,
The spaces wild and free,
Yet in the open I am as
A swimmer to the sea.
“ ’Ole ’Oppy ’Opkins! ’Oppy ’Op!”
She stares in at the door,
But scampers from the bogey shop
In fearful joy before
She rouses that be-whiskered shape
That wields the arm of Thor.
And so ’tis for the goblin grim
I feel a lasting woe,
Not for the Princess seized bv him
Because he loves her so,
With love that all ye shapely things
Well-loved can never know.
She is to me all sweetness born,
Tlie sun g’od’s radiant spark,
The scent and color of the morn,
The music of the lark;
And to the wonder-child I am
The ogre in the dark. !
BOB THE COBBLER.
AN ODE TO MYSELF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Hide-aiiil-Seelc I’ve endured with you,
And I fancy I know you through and through ;
You have tempted me in the vacant night
That we would be kings, which, of course, was skite.
The castles you built for my gladdening eyes
Were as solid, my fraud, as Magenta's sighs.
I once knew an optimist, keen and sweet,
Who walked on his hands instead of his feet;
But time moves on, much as time will do,
And you couldn’t cheat me, my Little Boy Blue.
So, knowing ourselves, I think you’ll agree
That the properest ending for you and for me
Is to fill ourselves with a Springtime breath
And laugh and laugh ourselves to death.
N.S.W.

Banks, like other people, are getting the
temporary benefit of the splashing around of
borrowed money. We are doing the thing
properly, and in order to make ourselves
believe we can afford it. we are pulling our
own legs mercilessly. There is, apparently,
no serious attempt to realise that the present
“prosperity” is bogus—that we are going
gay on our own lOtJ’s. But while the banks
will suffer, more or less, when the lOU’s
have to be cashed, they probably will not
suffer heavy losses Of principal; and if they
escape that they escape the worst that can
happen to a bank. For in spite of the bogus
prosperity there has been no boom at all in
real estate. On the contrary there has been
a gradual shrinkage, as of course there
should have been, ever since the great
trouble came upon us. True, advances have
been made also upon merchandise, directly
and indirectly, at inflated prices. But a
pretty wide margin is generally required
when such securities are on the- table ; and,
anyway, there is not going to be an instant
readjustment of prices when the war ends.
For one thing, the world’s reduced shipping
will be pretty busy for a while on another
job.
Chiefly as the result, then, of prudent
management, this paper doesn’t look for any
heavy banking losses, though it looks for
reduced banking profits. The latter feature
will, however, be largely governed by the
behavior of the markets for our products;
and previous experience on that point isn’t
worth tuppence. There is one writer, though,
who will be surprised if things remain in a
bad way for very long. Commercially a uni-
versal war wouldn’t be as bad as a universal
drought. In a drought, not only is there a
stoppage of production, but the machinery
of production —the live stock —is destroyed.
There is comparatively little of that in war.
On the contrary, in some directions the
machinery of production is vastly improved.
We shall find it so with regard to several of
our manufactures; and Germany will cer-
tainly find it so. That indeed is one strong
argument in favor of the levying of indemni-
ties from an apparently-smashed enemy.
.Germany may, after all, have done a bad
thing for herself when she proceeded to bleed
France after the last great war; for the re-
sult showed how much an apparently outed
country can be bled.
The Royal’s ’-figures have undergone con-
siderable change during the year:—
Sept., ’l5. Sept., ’l6.
Notes in circulation £953 £BBO Dec. £73
Billsincirculation, etc. 276,452 161,519 Dec. 114,933
Deposits at interest 1,451,085 1,544,041 Inc. 92,956
Free deposits . . 970.257 972,482 Inc. 2,225
Liquid assets ..1,340,429 943,661 Dec. 396,768
Advances . . . . 1,840,142 2,231,438 Inc. 391,296
Premises . . . . 83,090 83,815 Inc. 725
On the whole, those changes don’t look too
goad. Liquid assets have been reduced to
£043,661; and as the bank owes the public
£2,678,923, its liquid resources are reduced
to 35 p.c. of the deposits, etc. But, while
that isn’t a shockingly low proportion in
any case, the reduction is not so serious as
it seems because the bank has only about
£lOO,OOO less actual coin, bullion and Com-
monwealth notes; the rest of the reduction
is apparently due to the disposal of public
stocks —other than Commonwealth loans —
in 'order to make the money available for
clients ; and for such a policy there is a great
deal to be said just sow. Hundreds of
people have simply dumped their money into
banks to wait till the clouds roll by.. They
will do nothing with it themselves; and it
isn’t safe for banks, holding it on such a
tenure, to do much with it either. It would
suit the banks and the country better if
these people relieved the banks of some of
their State and municipal holdings, and thus
put the money at the disposal of somebody
who is prepared to do something with it.

There lias been a much sturdier growth of
business "than of immediate profits, because
expenses have been abnormal, partly on
account of extra taxation, partly because this
co. is doing the fair thing by the one-fourth
of its staff who are in khaki. This little com-
parison shows the rapid changes that have
been taking place:—-
*■ Gross Salaries, Fees Taxes and
Earning's. and Bonuses. Office Charges.
1910 ..£11,743 £4167 £763
1914 .. 13,041 5551 827
1915 .. 14.515 6102 1501
1916 . . 16,988 7548 2716
The increase in the interest earnings of
money has no doubt been a factor in im-
proving the gross earnings; but the most
important element has been the growth of the
business. In 1910 there was £3,029,970 at
credit of estates under administration ; there
is now £4,083,196 administered by the co. as
trustee or executor and £467,920 as agent.
And it looks as though the figures will con-
tinue to move along briskly. The State office
has been in operation a year or two now,
and a third trustee co. has come into the N.
S. Wales field. Nevertheless, instead of show-
ing’scars, the Permanent reports that “the
new trust and agency business for the 12
months amounts to upwards of £600,000.”
There is a very large amount of Fat man’s
business that never will go to the State
office. All the same, the Fat man won’t mind
quoting the State office when he is bargain-
ing as to whether he ought to be charged' an
or less. That’s probably where
tin# Existing trustee cos. will feel State com-
petition most; for the State office is not even
permitted to make money—it must reduce its
charges to cost price.
The co.’s own funds are either on deposit
with the Government or in the very fine new
premises of which the co. occupies a part.
The co. has £23,103 on mortgage—most of it
perhaps temporary advances to estates, but it
.apparently arranges an overdraft to provide
for that. Here are the figures:—
1905. 1910. 1915. 1916.
Capital.. ..£25,000 £25,000 £50,000 £50,000
Liabilities . . 23 42 16,679 17,111
Deposit with
Government. . 20.000 20,000 20,000 20,000
Mortgages . . 20,289 34,204 21,558 23,103
Premises . . Nil Nil 67,902 67,902
Sundry debtors 98 98 98 759
Cash . . . . 959 3,041 30 30
In addition to the security offered to clients
by the co.’s solidly-invested capital, share-
holders are liable to be called on for another
£950,000. But, though those figures are very
comforting, the man who proposes to let this
co. handle his affairs when he is too dead to
do it himself, has a better protection still—
(1) in the fact that the co.’s prosperity
depends entirely upon the way it does its job ;
(2) in the other fact that the men who do
the work are trained as specialists. And you
can’t say either of those things about the
casual or friend-of-the-familv trustee.

“Trade improved,” the directors say, with a
note of astonishment, “notwithstanding the
great difficulty in procuring supplies and
further heavy increases in the cost of goods,
freights, etc,” They really mean : “We did
a bigger business, though wo had to pop on
the prices to make up for increased costs,
with a hit over to cover our risks and for
the good of our health.” And the risks, on
paper at any rate, are not small. A lot of
the co.’s business is done on time-payment
terms; and that means very— large book
debts. As the co. has gone in extensively tor
shop property and carries a heavy stock as
well, this has meant leaning on creditors as
follows ■ ■ ' •

Not that the Creditors mind. They sit
comfortably behind the following assets :

The increase of book debts in the present
uncertain state of the world is courageous ;
and so is the very big holding of stock.
Admittedly, nevertheless, under all previously
known conditions the losses on time-payment
business have been astonishingly small; and,
further, there isn’t likely to be any very
sudden collapse of the bubble of prosperity
which is being blown to a great size by the
expenditure of war and other loans. That
is all to the good of this co. and the time-
payment business on its books;
When sub-normal conditions of trade
presently arrive this co. will be in a better
condition than most to continue its present
dividends. Its margin of undivided profit
has always been wide enough to fill this
paper with admiration. In 10 years it has
put by £116,000 —an average of about 5} p.c.
p.a. on the present ordinary capital of
£200,000. Besides that there is £150,000 of
pref. capital; but that is limited to 6 p.c.

The directors are still proud that they pay
the dividend on the original capital, and they
say so in big black letters, no doubt because
it would cost more to do it in red. It is
something worth being proud of, too; for
the Langridge. being nearly 40 years old,
went through the land boom. Now the direc-
tors don’t propose to risk their reputation,
wherefore they work on most conservative
lines. The trouble which struck Australian
building societies in the ’nineties arose from
the fact that they had borrowed heavily and
couldn’t afford, to nurse their securities. So
this office fights shy of deposits, and, being
thus under no obligation to pay out, is
under no obligation to sell when the pro-
perty market is unfavorable. The following
little table shows its total liabilities alongside
its assets :—■
Deposits, Fore- Other
etc. Advances, closures. Assets.
1905 .. £12,229 £59,172 £54,245 £3678
1910 . . 21,351 96,143 24,997 Nil
1915 .. 21,402 103,022. 3,962 9139
1916 . . 20,229 110,235 3,929 1012
So it owes only £20,229, including unclaimed
dividends, and has more than seven times
that much in assets. Further, very few of
the advances are new; and it is the new
advance that is apt to be thrown in when
adversity strikes a community. The really
astonishing thing is that the society should
be able to make so good a profit out of build-
ing-society business in these days of State
Savings Bank loans without taking more
risks. It wouldn’t be at all possible but
for the Langridge’s excellent reputation and
its economical management.

There isn’t enough evidence there to convict
war of causing depression. But to assume
that a country can become really prosperous
by keeping one-fourth of its men out of pro-
ductive jobs, and paying them for not earn-
ing a bean in the commercial sense, and
spending millions of pounds on things for
them to waste (in the economic sense)— to
imagine you can do that and be prosperous
on account of it is quite insane, it is no
more possible for a country to become really
prosperous that way than it is for a business
to become prosperous by sending off one-
fourth of its hands on holiday on full wages
and paying their train fares and boat tickets,
and providing for their keep, and supplying
them with a motor-car and 10,000 rounds
of ammunition each to shoot at something
that is no good' to eat. lienee if anybody
thinks that the- present figures of trading
cos. are normal, and are likely to be the
figures of a couple of years hence, he is
booked, this paper fears, for a horrible dis-
appointment.
This co. will not have much to complain
about even if profits slump 25 p.c. or so.
It would "still show well over 15 p.c. on its
present capital of £180,000; and of that
capital £30,000 has been transferred from
reserves. Further, the results over a long
range of years entitle the shareholders to
expect something very good in normal times
later on, even if profits are rather badly hit
in the depression that is likely to arrive soon.
Look at this evidence of solid progress :
1906. 1915. 1916.

into account that the retail drug trade is not
one in which fortunes are easily picked up.
No doubt even in this case it would be
found that the side-lines have most to be
thanked for.

“The increased dairy and agricultural out-
put as anticipated in last report,” say the
directors, “did not result owing to the dis-
trict experiencing very dry conditions during
the greater part of the half-year.” So the
disclosed net profit has dropped slightly
below* the figures of the previous half-year.
But whether it expresses the true net profit
earned depends entirely on what has been
allowed for depreciation, and this co. pro-
vides no evidence on that point. Maybe the
directors have allowed for more than ample
depreciation and the net profit is unduly
shrunken in consequence. But the co. is not
likely to disclose anything sensational in its
ballast. From an earning point of view it
is apparently little better off than it was 10
years ago. Look at these figures :

So in the matter of net liquid assets the co. .
is about £27,000 worse off than it was 10
years ago, and it shows hardly any better
net profits. Or put in another way, in order
to maintain its profits at somewhere about
the old level it has had to draw on its liquid
assets to the extent of about £27,000, which
amount has been mostly put into vessels.
It isn’t at all a brilliant record.
* * * *
“The Palmer interest” has controlled the
Broken Hill Junction North for very many
years', and has succeeded in producing a
hundred promises but no dividends for the
last five years and only six during this cen-
tury. A majority of the directors have now
got tired of the promises and have rebelled,
so a movement is on foot to get rid of them.
If the shareholders like promises and no
dividends they will sack the rebellious direc-
tors. If they don’t, they will do the other
thing.
In effect the fight is to settle, not so much
who are to be directors, but who is to be
general manager. The position is now and
has for many years been held by one of the
Palmers. Some or all of the directors who
think that dividends are a fair thing are
known to favor a change —-probably in the
direction of putting the whole concern under
the control of Bewick, Moreing and Co.,
who manage the Zinc Corporation and have
made a success of the South Blocks, which
had previously been almost as great a disap-
pointment as the Junction North. In reply
it is contended that there is no occasion to
make a change—that (for the 495th time or-
thereabouts) the mine is just on the verge of,
paying dividends. The Bulletin is unable
to find any proof of it. The whole position
is so interesting, and incidentally throws a
light on the terms on which silver-lead smelt-
ing is done, that it is worth going into.
The Junction North has two sources of
supply—dumps of slimes which it has
bought from other cos. for about £BO,OOO
and a small quantity of ore still left in its
own mine. The latter has not been drawn
upon for some time, and the specially-erected
treatment works have been employed on the
slimes. From these slimes, which are worked
by the Bradford process, a lead concentrate
is produced and sold to the Associated
Smelters. Now, it is admitted that on pay-
ments actually made no profit is shown ; but
it is alleged that profits are being banked
up. It therefore becomes necessary to look
into the contract between the Associated
Smelters and the Junction North. This
agreement is for the purchase by the former,
for a nominal term of 50 years, of “mean
leady concentrates produced solely by -jig-
ging, tabling or vanning direct from crude
ore, and shall not include concentrates pro-
duced from slime, or by any other system
of concentration than the aforesaid.” The
acceptance of concentrates derived from
slime by a flotation process is therefore a
matter of special arrangement; and the
smelters are entitled to penalties—as, for
instance, for excess zinc, which is a detri-
ment to silver lead ore. (“The purchaser
may make a sliding scale of lines and allow-
ances as it may think fit to cover the varia-
tion in grade, constituents and character of
concentrates.”) The charges under the
agreement amount to about 1445. per ton,
including railage to smelters. The procedure
is as follows. The co. delivers the concen-
trates at Port Pirie. The smelters estimate
their gross value. Pro forma recoveries of
90 p.c. of the lead and 98 p.c. of the silver
are assumed ; and 90 p.c. of the gross value,
less charges, is paid at once. Thus 10 p.c.
is held back for final settlement. Payment
has been made at the rate of £25 for lead and
2s. for silver; that is to say, £25 per ton
and 2s. per oz. less 10 p.c. It is claimed
that, as the markets have been higher than
those figures, more than 10 p.c. of the total
amount already received will be available
presently : and this constitutes the “profit
which is being allegedly banked up.
Nobody knows whether there will be a
penny more to be drawn or not. There pro-
bably'will be something—perhaps £2 per ton
of lead and Gd. per oz. of silver; but nobody
can be sure of even that. For these points
have to be taken into account: —
(1) Final payments are made, not on prices at time
of delivery, but on “the average price obtained by the.
purchaser (the smelter co.) over the whole of its
sales for the half-year.”
(2) If any of the smelter charges have been under-
estimated, the purchaser will deduct the amount from
the final payment.
(3) Ditto as to fines and penalties —as, for in-
stance, for zinc contents.
(4) Ditto in the event of the smelter failing to
recover 90 p.c. of the lead and 98 p.c, of the silver.
(5) Ditto in the event of the smelter co. not aver-
aging £25 for lead and 2s. for silver; and there is
no obligation on the co. to sell the metals in any one
period. The co. may not have disposed of the whole
of the product yet.
On the first four months’ output after the
works were restarted ,it would appear that
there was a loss, on the manager’s estimate,
of £2904, without allowing anything for
depreciation. There seems, however, to have
been an over-valuation of the lead contents,
which would bring the total loss to about
£3lOO, exclusive of depreciation. If the
extra £2 per ton and 6d. per oz. before men-
tioned are realised, the co. will get about
£3900. The £2O0 —the difference between
the two figures—would not nearly make a
fair provision for depreciation of plant. On
these figures there will be no net profit .what-
ever, but a substantial loss.
* -X-. * *
The law is a wonderful institution. . The
Silver Peak Mining Co.’s N. S. Wales leases
were cancelled because of a breach of condi-
tions, and the land became open for leasing
on a certain date. At 10 o’clock on that day
the contending parties pegged the blocks.
The warden then sat to decide who was to
get them. Experts in horology went from
Sydney to give evidence about time ; experts
on chronometers gave evidence about clocks ;
eminent counsel argued for three long days.
After they were j all finished the Mining
Warden calmly dismissed all the applica-
tions because not one of them had asked for a
“mining lease” as required by the. Act.
When all hands recovered their senses from
this shock there was a stampede from the
court and a wild rush to peg out the leases
again by the light of a feeble moon.
