From Wqstonia : —-
Edna May is not looking as well as it did, and,
taking everything into consideration, shares seem
dear at anywhere near £4.
Central is being knocked into shape by the new
manager, and if there is really anything worth
becoming enthusiastic about in the show it will be
unearthed during the next few months. Prospects
look more promising than they did a little while
ago.
Deeps still yields a little gold and much bluster.
Tom Duff has now advanced over £5OOO to keep
things, going, and by the time this and other debts
are liquidated there won't be much left .of the
£lO,OOO ,to be derived from the disposal of under-
written debentures.
Consolidatedi, which up till recently couldn’t be
regarded as a mine, now looks much better. The
reef has a good appearance, and promises to respond
favorably to further developments.
* * * *
Option hunters are having a look around
Mt. Monger (W.A.), but at present the field
offers no attractions to the public. A
crowd of Bulong- dryblowers got some shallow
alluvial and a few reefs there about 20 years
ago, but the scarcity of water and greater
scarcity of gold ultimately drove them away.
Recently gold was found in some small veins,
but signs of permanency are still wanting.
“Oof Bird” : I was among the quick and
the dead of Kalgoorlie the other day.
Golden Horseshoe looks the picture of health
in places, but in other parts is but a shadow
of its former self. Still, on the whole, it
shows the best promise of life the field.
Great Boulder is gradually wasting away,
with no hope of recovery. Ivanhoe still
hazards on getting out of the porphyry in-
trusion and meeting with good ore again. It
recently put in a drill at the 8620 ft. level,
but beyond striking one or two good-grade
stringers, results were disappointing. Lake
View and Star is in poor condition. The
management talks of things righting them-
selves, but there is not much to justify this
optimism. Perseverance is on its last legs,
unless a miracle happens. Associated’s mill
is kept going by burrowing here and
there. Kalgurli expects to pass out towards
the end of next year, and there isn’t likely
to be much left of the Oroya Links by that
time. South Kalgurli still makes profit out
of poor stuff and is all right. It, however,
has nothing to look forward to at depth and
is gradually using up its resources. Except
a few hand-to-mouth ventures at the extreme
north and south ends of the belt everything
else on the field is down and apparently out.
* * * *
T. 11. Williams, once of Marvel Loch fame,
is now running Leonora Blocks, Leonora
(W.A.). Broker Marriott is his special
admirer, so when he picked on Leonora
Blocks as a promising proposition. Marriott
wrote the foreword that clinched the option
and brought in the ,public. To the outsider
Leonora looks to be- a patchy show even on
Williams’s painting. Still he counts up to
14,000 tons as proved on three sides at the
400 ft. level east. This ore gives values at
150 ft., and in the underlie shaft of 50s. and
along the 400 ft. level of 80s. To the west
4000 tons are standing and the shoot seems
to average 4ft. in width. So the battery
should be able to make a pretty splash for
the next 12 months provided the stone mills
to Williams’s sampling. With things run-
ning smoothly Marriott will have lost his
dash if Leonora Blocks isn’t doing a smart
two-stepper long before the market closes
for the Christmas vacation.
* * * *
Kampong Kamunting has taken fright—
and flight. Willing enough to pay one lot of
taxes it wasn’t prepared to pay two or
three, so .it is winding itself up here and
registering with exactly the same number of
shares, etc., in the Malay States (British),
where the mine is.
■ Briseis (Tas.) handed out a sixpenny
(£15,000) last June, and there is talk of
another before the end of the year. Still ils
net profit for nine months to September 30
was only £20,000. For the last quarter the
net profit was £7OOO and the output .80 tons
of metallic tin, with gold from the Wallace
dredging property (Vic.) The
revenue for the. whole of .this' .year isn’t
likely to exceed £BO,OOO, of which £58,500
will represent tin values; and practically all
the anticipated profit of £27,'000 ■ for. the 12
months must come from this source. During
the last quarter the Wallace gold dredges
turned over 314,900 cub. yds. and recovered
gold worth £5044. But the average yield
has fallen below 4d. per yd., so profits are
cut fine.
* * * *
A sale of Austral Bolivia ss. shares at
40s. indicates that Sydney speculators have
been impressed by the enthusiastic reports
on this S. American tin property. But while
the outlook is excellent the conditions men-
tioned in reports are exactly those calcu-
lated to deo«ive the most careful prospector.
An average from the surface of 71b. of black
tin per cub. yd. is very rich, and half the
tin scratchers in Australia would rush to
Bolivia if they were quite sure of it.
“Gadgett” on mine ventilation and other
problems:—■
“Paddymelon” asks (B. 12/10/T6) “what is wrong
with applying the principle of the vacuum-cleaner
for ridding mines of dust, etc. ?” There is nothing
at all wrong with the idea, though it is not neces-
sary to use it with rock drills. The water jet is
just as efficient, and is much cheaper and handier.
But for ventilating rises and “dead ends”' there is
nothing to beat.it. The smoke, fumes,, etc., are
drawn away instead of being sallowed to drift through
the mine, and the miner is spared the annoyance of
a head buzzing like a swarm of bees. Various ex-
haust fans have been put on the market, all of them
more or less efficient, but only in rare instances are
they used. The whole reason is that they cost some-
thing. True, in some cases, it may be trifling and
the compensating advantages are out of all propor-
tion thereto ; nevertheless mine owners and managers
are incredibly reluctant to do anything that would
add another penny to their costs. Only the man who
has worked in a “dead end” aftpr a heavy firing-out
knows how bad the fumes of the exploded nitro-
glycerine compounds are, particularly if the firing is
in sulphide ores. For hours after the rock drills
start work the air reeks; but as long as the miner
will put up with it (force of circumstances gener-
ally compels him to), the manager will not dream of
adding to his costs the expense of a decent ventilat-
ing system.
This devotion to the god of Costs is carried to
absurd extremes. Unbelievable though it may seem,
it is nevertheless a fact that one of the principal divi-
dend-paying mines in Tasmania begrudges the neces-
sary drill steel for the rock drills, and it is nothing
uncommon for machines to be hung up for hours at a
time because of insufficient supplies. The energies
of the whole staff, from g.m. to pannikin boss, are
directed to reducing costs, and they all become so
absorbed in the occupation that many other import-
ant and advantageous things are completely over-
looked. For instance, suppose a mine shaft to he
500 ft. deep, with pump chamber or cistern on the
bottom level and the whole mine draining thereto.
There are five levels all connected and many dead-
ends where the air currents do not circulate. Suppose
on (say) the 200 ft. level there is a steady flow of
water from a bore-hole, or country break or vugh
this water flows to the bottom level before it reaches
the pump. If a small tank were built where the water
comes out of the rock and the water were piped to
the bottom level the pressure there would be about
1301 b. to the square inch. Then a I-inch nozzle
and a 2ft. Pelton wheel would be all that would be
required -to drive a fair-size exhaust fan, which
could he used for ventilating the rises and “dead-
ends” to the manifest benefit of the men working
there. I have seen many places where this scheme
could be worked, but have never yet seen it tried.
We badly want a clause in our Mining Acts similar
to one in the South African code. “No shift boss shall
enter or cause any other person to enter any rise,
winze, stope or other working place, while smoke
or fumes are perceptible to sight, smell or any other
of the senses. Penalty £50.” With such a regula-
tion, combined with rigid inspection, mine owners
would ventilate the “little hells” that are unfortu-
nately so common.
* * * *
Owing mainly to increased costs, which
in this industry can’t be passed on, Aus-
tralia’s gold yield for the first three quarters
of 1916 was over 216,,0000z. short of that
for the previous year. Victoria produced
55,5940 z. less; Westralia 108,3550 z,, N. S.
Wales 33,1930 z. and Bananaland 17,4140 z.
less. The Speck and S. Aus. don’t matter.
* * * *
S’African gold and diamond mine-owners
are bucking at the way their share of the
cost of the war is mounting up. Gold-
mining profits are now taxed at the rate of
16J p.c., and for the first time on record
diamonds have also to contribute when pro-
fits exceed 35 p.c. De Beer’s profit in the
past lias ranged from 60 p.c. to 100 p.c.
No dividend has, however, been paid during
the past two years. But if, as alleged, the
co. controls the market in the matter of
demand, supply and prices, it could easily
pay nothing one year and a thumping div.
the next. Abe Bailey remarks sadly that
South Africa is no longer the sanctuary of
the capitalist.

HOW TO RUB OFF FAT.
CIRCULAR MOTION NECESSARY.
An excess of fatty tissues on hips or abdo-
men not only prevents one from looking well
or feeling comfortable, but it is likely to
keep increasing until all the youthful beauty
of the figure is gone. A very simple yet
wonderfully effective means for quickly and
permanently reducing this superfluous flesh
consists in applying night and morning, rub-
bing with a circular movement, a harmless
herb mixture composed of one dram of quas-
sia chips and three ounces of cirola bark
extract, which you can get at small cost
from any good chemist. Put the quassia
chips in a pan and pour over them a teacup-
ful of boiling water; in about half a minute
strain through a cloth and add the cirola
bark extract, when it is ready for use. This
recipe is also very valuable for reducing
double chin, puffy neck, arms, hands, etc.
The fat shrinks _ away rapidly, leaving no
wrinkles or flabbiness.
CAUTION. —Be sure and apply on both
hips or both arms, for a difference in one of
them would soon he noticeable if the lotion
were only used on one side. — Apvt.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, October 30, 1916.
My Dear Akim. —
The G.-G., like the Indian Viceroy, has
the special perquisite of handing the Com-
panionship of Michael and George and simi-
lar baubles to the new owners. 1 fancy
his Ex. can even in an emergency slap his
walking-stick on the shoulder of a brand-new
knight and tell him to x’ise up Sir Mama-
duke. Sydney’s veteran champion of the
Kiddies’ cause, Dr. Charles Mackellar, forti-
fied by the mineral waters of Moree, took
his Iv.C.M.G. from the G.-G. at Admiralty
House. On the same afternoon the lathy
Peter Board came up for his C.M.G.—a sort
of good-conduct prize in the infant class.
Director Maiden, of the Botanic Gardens, got
away with an 1.5.0. on this gift afternoon.
The Red Cross dames had a new force to
shake them into smiles (even the dismal
Jennies looked more pleasant) when Lady
Galway came to Sydney. She tosses off a
graphic picture of Red Cross work while the
ordinary feminine orator is feeling for sta-
tistics. At the big Town Hall meeting the
other afternoon the lady from S’Australia
held the crowd with her picturesque prose.
For instance: “Before long may morning
break and the women of Australia catch the
joyous footfall of the victors’ homeward
march!” I understood the lady to also
say that she still believed the Grape State
made the best shirts for the Red Cross, with
immovable buttons.
There’s no connection, I believe, between
the Miss Macaulay who shadows Lady Gal-
way as friend, secretary and so on, and
the family of famous Tom. She’s an indus-
trious letter-writer, though, like the great
namesake who wrote to sister Hannah every
day—l suppose because he couldn’t ’phone
her.
Lady Helen, in the chair at this huge
gathering, summed up'the year’s socks and
comforts. Chaplain Stewart gave the story
of the heroic Australian stand when the
Southland was torpedoed—and more than
women wiped their eyes. J. O. Fairfax, J.
J. Virgo (world’s rouseabout for the
Y.M.C.A.) and Dr. Edith Barrett (Mel-
bourne) added to the speech-making. The
Baldwin Spencer ladj r represented the lone
Northern Territory—l suppose because her
explorer knows more about the innards of
this continent than most men. Miss Mary
Strickland tools her delicate mother’s place
at this Hip Hooray for ourselves.
The Lads of the Village, with secretary
Budge, YV. G. Layton, Consett Stephen and
a few more, gave A.D.C. Boulton a good-bye
party. Dinner at Paris House, then to see
the Follies at the Tiv., and supper in the
Phillip-street snuggery to wind up with.
Next evening they saw the boy off for Mel-
bourne by the express. And just as the
engine was making its final noises the boy
found he’d left everything he wanted most —
ticket, passport, money, etc. —at Government
House. The stationmaster let him go on —
with a caution—and the Lads dashed back
to get the indispensables and send them on
by the later train. Youth has its long, long
thoughts, I suppose. And they don’t include
passports.
Lady Edeline Strickland is head of the
Red Cross in this State, and in spite of
illness contrives to keep in touch with all
the big centres. The party at Govern-
ment House the other afternoon was a
gentle reminder to all and sundry that there
is a final voice in executive matters. Some
of the top ladies are said to have needed the
prod rather badly.
Three hundred or more Red Crossers over-
flowed the drawing-room and drifted into
the ballroom. No, it wasn’t dancing! Mrs.
Blaxland was the lady-marslial in charge of
damsels at a long line of spinning-wheels.
His Ex. has put still another of these
machines together for the Eldest Daughter.
The latest has a treadle like a sewing-
machine, and I forget how many miles of
wool it’s supposed to turn out to the square
half-hour.
Soldier Finn, with daughter Kathleen—a
voluntary who passed round the cake —helped
the party. The Creswell lady, Mrs. Bage
(also from the southern city), the tall
Onslow family, Miss Yeo (Yass) and Mrs.
Douglas Gilfillan (Wentworth Falls) came
to Sydney for the event. Prof. Andy
Stuart’s grey top-haf was in the hall. At
the Town Hall meeting, by the .way, the
dominant Scot was on the platform. But
the peri at the stairway hadn’t any sign or
token to let Mrs. Andy sit amongst the
mighty; so she turned aside and knitted
with the herd.
Getting back to the Strickland party, the
man who could have bumped the chandelier
if he’d been careless with his head was the
big McCall McGowen. He looks like a Gren-
adier Guardsman after he’s gone grey, and
marched up the drawing-room like a whole
regiment of dragoons. Mrs. Kendall looked
over the edge of some strawberries in the new
pepper-and-salt check. The Cullen lady
swelled the number of knights’ wives. The
latter are getting numerous enough to have a
suburb to themselves.
Mother Cecelia, of the Hospice for the
Dying, begs that you will set a sky-blue mark
opposite next Friday night, and spend the
evening at Paddington Town Hall, where for
one shilling you will get 10 shillings’ worth of
song and dance and elocution. Proceeds, of
course, in aid of the unsectarian hospice.
Joynton Smith, Eslick of the Palladium,
and Aid. Farrell are the kindly trio who
have been secured for the honorary offices of
chairman, treasurer and secretary.
Here is Ella
Richards, the busy
hon. sec. and smil-
ing youngwoman in
charge of the Old
Linen Depot. If
you want to lend
a hand, it’s in the
basement under
the Town H all.
Through all the
long hot weeks of
last summer, with-
out a break, Ella
stuck to her old
rags. They are no
violets, either,
though some of the
stuff arrives with
an honest smell of
soap and sunshine.
The rest? Goodness nows what it was like
when it left home. But it arrives just
ready for the dustbin, or so dirty that the
washing of it is a mighty unpleasant busi-
ness.
Will the patriots who send rags—decent
old rags to begin with —be careful to pack
the goods in lined bags? A long train jour-
ney in the open-mesh of a sugee-sack, with
—say —a sack of potatoes on top, give un-
desirable results. The snow-white swabs
which the Old Linen girls turn out by the
thousand are from a design by an Australian
sister now at the Front. Pine sawdust pads
—healing and disinfecting—are made at the
rate of 23§0 a month, along with more or
less of everything else the surgeon wants
for dressings. In the matter of these pine
sawdust pads the matron and nurses at the
Walker Convalescent Home are big contri-
butors. I suppose they go down on their
hands and knees to a local timber man. In
Sydney Langdon and Langdon give continu-
ous supplies of the aromatic dust. Ella
Richards is, besides, hon. sec. to Red Cross
ambulance classes, and commandant of the
Double Bay V.A.D.’s, so she’s doing her bit
for the wounded Kangaroos. Now, what about
that old linen tablecloth you don’t really
want? And that lingerie can’t mend
any more?
So the large and affable gentleman, Consul
Ililmann von Goes, representative of Sweden
in these parts, is to have a few months’ fur-
lough. It’s a stormy time for a pleasure trip
to Stockholm. But perhaps it isn’t Stock-
holm.
The Dufault concert for the French, organ-
ised by little Mrs. Harry Austin, closed its
accounts last week. It was chiefly a private
subscription affair, and the French-Aus-
tralian League has £BOO 10s. 6d. as a
souvenir.
A new order to the A.I.F. overseas com-
mands that Billjim’s jaunty hat shall be let
down all round, and the sign of the rising
sun be put in front with a crown of felt for
its background. At a distance Billjim has
a chance of being mistaken for a Canuck
(which is slang for Canadian), and what
he thinks of the new regulation wouldn’t
look nice in this polite screed.
Know Mrs. Todd Helmuth, of N’York, who
is pictured amongst i other freak record-
breakers in a Yankee’ paper as “the woman
who belongs to more clubs than any other
woman in the world”? At least 100 of them
have bestowed medals on her —presumably
for being the champion dodger of the domes-
tic gas-stove. The print adds that the robust-
looking matron with the Give-me-liberty-or-
give-me-death expression is on the roll of
The Daughters of the Early Settlers of Aus-
tralia, who have broken the sequence of
medals by giving her a key. But perhaps
you don’t now The Daughters of the Early
Settlers of Australia either?
This year’s. Arts and Crafts show is just in
time for the Christmas-presents outbreak.
The Crafters have turned out leather the
half-mile. In this section M. Bamberger,
Mira Mullens, Mildred Creed, and E. Sansom
score. Ada Newman, Elza Josephson and
Ethel Atkinson have a big exhibit. Edith
Bell Brown in pottery shows what you can
do with the purple iris. Millicent Elliot is
strong in pyrography. The National Gallery
trustees think so, too, and bought her panel
with a majestic peacock spreading his tan to
the public eye. Constance Evans in wood-
carving uses the beech, the walnut and the
oak. The show, which is in the Education
Mansion, in Loftus-street, will be open till
the end of this week. One cosy corner is a
tea-room. So you can wash down the ex-
hibition with your favorite brew.
Gladys Owen, the young daughter of
Langer, K.C., is one of the fine products ot
the war. Here is a girl who, naturally shy
and sensitive, is prepared to do and dore
almost anything for the great cause, lhis
week she is entitled to complete, silence aftei
her conscription efforts in Martin Place. In
ringing tones she has been telling of those
horrible gaps in the ranks and what
should be done to fill them. There wasn’t any
craven suggestion that Indians should do it,
either. And then the electric call for the
men to show themselves —and the forest of
hands that sprang up at the call! Three
years ago if Mother had heard of her girl
doing stump-oratory at street corners, there
would have been soft lectures to celebrate, the
event. But we’ve all learnt a lot since
August, 1914.
A gentlemanly goat, the mascot of one
unit in the naval review, kept well to the
fore while his Ex. Munro Ferguson addressed
the men. The inner Domain, where the final
rally was, looked green as emerald velvet
after the recent rains. Under the flickering-
shade of newly-laundered trees, the many-
colored summer parasols —some of them are
tulips—made a gorgeous garden of silk
blooms. Commodore Henderson, R.N., who
bosses the port now that admirals must be
elsewhere, stood near his Ex. while the blue-
jackets’ band gave “A Life on the Ocean
Wave” to keep things moving. Boys fresh
from the fighting zone on the Cape station
were the heroes of this part of the parade.
Tall George Fuller handed over his niece,
Florence Christmas, of Neutral Bay, when
Cecil Scott Waine produced the plain gold
ring. The wedding was in St. James’s
Church, abloom with arum-lilies for the
event. A billowy white frock had the square-
cut neck edged with seed pearls. Lilies of the
valley, the fashionable flower of the moment,
were bunched together for the bridal posy.
Two bridesmaids, Dot Waine and N. Mit-
chell, were in foam white caught with prim-
rose ribbons. Mrs. Christmas hostessed the
party at the Carlton.
At Hazelbrook Mrs. John Sulman, presi-
dent of a Blue Mountain Red Cross League,
headed a recent successful raid for cash. The
excuse was a garden fete in the grounds of
the Hall Sanatorium. Matron Joan and the
staff assisted.
John McCormack
was heard of not
long ago from
the U.S.A.. with
Donald Mcßeath
sharing his tri-
umphs. Donald
started from be-
hind scratch. On
his first appear-
ance in Sydney he
didn’t have any
dress clothes, and
the stupid “music
critic” of a daily,
instead of giving
the shy, shivering
lad a pat of en-
couragement, merely
remarked upon his
unconventional gar-
ments. That nearly drove the sensitive
youngster to suicide. I don’t know whether
The Bulletin man heard of it or not,
but next week Donald was stood on
his feet and told of the future that
was ahead of him. It helped him on
wonderfully. Then came a bout with appen-
dicitis, which turned out another blessing.
He was taken to Lewisham Hospital, and
Mrs. John McCormack happened to be in
the same haven. The Mother Rectress con-
trived to interest her in the youth. She
passed the word on to John. John liked
him; and since then Donald (whose picture
I send you) has had a steady engagement, in
a most congenial atmosphere, with intervals
of study.
Dashing Dorothy Gordon, designer of
daring vaudeville ideas for various local
charities, has cabled her arrival in the
U.S.A., where she is about to test her brains
and endurance in film acting.
Topsy Beveridge, of the Dollar Vale clan,
is about to join up in matrimony with Cecil
Granville, who sports lieut,.-colonel in advance
of his novelettish name and D.S'.O. in the
rear. If the powers and passports permit,
the wedding will take place in Egypt, where
the ’groom (an Anglo-Australian, by the
way) awaits the coming of his bride per the
Medina. If not, then the scene will be shifted
to the shores of one-time (though I don’t
know when) Merrie England.
Also on the Medina, bound for Egypt,
travels Florence Harnett, who follows in the
footsteps of two sisters, and goes to do secre-
tarial work for a Comforts Fund with its
headquarters in the shade of Cheops and
Chephron.
Polling-day was a surprise to the timid,
who expected to see the greatest row that
ever was. In the early morning it was calm
as a procession to a prayer-meeting. Stroll-
ing up William-street the early bird saw the
early thirst with its nose gummed to the
locked doors of the corner beer-bar. And on
nearly every door was the inscription 4 \ ote
‘No’ and Keep Your Country White.” How a
nation that stands armed with a peace pam-
phlet is going to keep its country at all is
one of the mysteries the feminine brain can’t
pretend to unravel. Still, woman can’t take
much credit. It was the vote of the startled
housemaid, terrified by wicked tales of what
was coming from Asia, that gave the Noes a
tremendous lift.
Miss Scobie, whose brother fell in France
not long ago, was one of the brilliant women-
speakers of last week. She raked her. audi-
ences with the fire of a seething, patriotism
that even penetrated the tough hide of the
persistent interjector. But the fringe of the
crowd was adamant.
The State Ex. hasn’t complained audibly
of housemaid’s knee or stiff elbow since the
review on Friday. But he stood for quite
one hour with his hand to his hat while
12,000 of Australia’s best came swinging
down the street. The G.-G., with soldier
Firth at Ids heels, was at the saluting post.
The State Ex. and his young man, the nice
boy Boulton (now on his way back to per-
suade a medical board to send him again to
France), were adjacent. All sorts and sizes
in brigadier-generals —we’re getting a fine
assortment for home consumption!—the
wiry-loolcing Colonel Miller, the newly-wed
Lieut. Baldry (he married Annie Mac Kay
a few weeks ago), Skipper Cumberlege.
R.N., of the dandy ways in peace-time, and
Heaven knows who beside, ought to have
said “Yes” as the 12,000 tramped past.
A big house chuckled at the antics of the
“Barber of Seville” in the Opera House. The
boxes were full, and as usual those of the co.
who were not on the stage were watching
their friends (and otherwise) from the front.
Soprano Yisoni, Filippini and Lois sat back
to see the rogue Scamuzzi flourish a knife like
a Moorish scimitar round the lathered chin
of old Bartolo. Miss Gonsalez, in buttercup-
yellow, covered with black crossbars like a
big fly-net, had to skip about the stage in a
frock that was much too tight. Still, she
frisked considerably as the lively Rosina.
with Balboni, disguised as a Spanish black
beetle, making love under a hat the size of
a dinner-waggon. The big tenor, by the
way, was a six-foot edition of Little Lord
Fauntleroy when he warbled his serenade in
black velvet, with wide cream lace frills at
the edge of his knickers and a ditto collar.
This collar, or its mate, has appeared in so
many operas that it’s a hard-worked first-
nighter. Hilda Caro, in rose-pink, was in a
box, and the voice of the prompter was all
over the theatre.
Contralto Kate Rooney (Mrs. Billy Kirk-
ham) writes to mention her near arrival in
Sydney—probably the end of this month.
She’s been singing as well as holidaying in
Woodrow Wilson’s big kingdom. Kate brings
that much-travelled baby, Kirkham, junior,
who braved the early submarines in his
swaddling clothes, to .show grandma how he
cuts his back teeth.
Mrs. Arthur Duncan, once Alice John-
ston, of Wellington (M.L.), has been seeing
Sydney for a week or two. Her husband is
Maoriland’s pride in the amateur golfing line,
and owns handsome looks, too.
Bird Day moved on a week to avoid bump-
ing into the vote for Tommy in the trenches.
So this Friday evening the Tech. College
Hall will resound with the kiddies’ efforts
to imitate the throstle calls of Bushland.
Even the undertakery crow squawks “Yes”
when his mate calls for help, so the Gould
League will tell you.
The 3rd Battalion Comforts Fund (Mrs.
Philip Street in command) sends out S.O.S.
signals. It wants more workers to get
through a mountain of sewing to keep our
boys warm in the blizzards to come.
North and South were linked up in Lon-
don a few weeks ago, when Hilda, of the
Monaro Harnetts, was matrimonially at-
tached to Lieut. Abbott, of New England
and the Light Horse. The first meeting took
place in Cairo, where Mrs. Abbott-that-now-
is was doing secretarial work for our Red
Cross.
Paul Dufault finishes in the middle of
this week, after filling the Town Hall with
prolonged applause. Then back to Maori-
land for another farewell before boarding the
boat for North America.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, October 30, 1916.
Mv Dear Yarrie, —
Mooroopna, that strip of irrigated orchards
that runs for several miles along the Goul-
burn River, had plenty of excitement during
the flood. Visitors at the local hotel thanked
Heaven from the balcony that their rooms
were upstairs. They woke in the morning
to find the woodheap knocking softly at the
back door, and every kind of queer flood-
creature dodging about in the surrounding
lake. All hands and the cook had been
driven upstairs by the flood waters, which had
submerged* even the water-tanks. If you
wanted such a thing as water the muddy
flood had to be dipped up, boiled and
then strained to rid it of the dead cat from
next door and the drowned rabbits of two
miles up. The hotel family got out the
field-glasses and saw cattle standing knee-
deep in wafer on top of a distant knoll. They
made out the family cow among the group
and tried to milk her by wireless, but failed.
Soon Strawberry (or perhaps it was Daisy)
separated herself from her friends and began
to swim down the road. The faithful old
soft-drinkefy swam round the back of the
hotel, paddled in at. the back gate and
splashed to her old bail, where she waited
(up to her neck in water) to be milked.
Unfortunately, her owners didn’t possess a
diving-dress and hadn’t the materials to
build a coffer-dam round the unfortunate-
beast. So, when her patience was ex-
hausted, she swam away again to the knoll
and made a speech about it.
The event of the week for cafe-haunting
and broom-dodging dames was the opening of
the new Vienna wing. The Vienna has now
spread into what used to be Glen’s music
shop, and Griffin, the Federal Capital
designer, is supposed to have inspired the
decorative scheme. Tillars of gold bricks
hold up fried-egg frescoes. The lights are
hidden, and that which might have been
diffused amber light resolves itself into
crude mustard-colored ceilings. Huge bronze
round bowls (very new) are perched on
overhanging ledges. The waitresses wear
mustard-colored linen pinnies over white
frocks, and their Dutch caps dance on well-
frizzed hair. At the foot of the gold-brick
pillars are gold tubs of growing ferns with
real fountains playing among them. But
the cafe haunters groaned with one accord,
“What’s the good of the Vienna without the
comfy leather sofas?” All the elegant time-
wasters and nose-painters loved to drift in
for tea gossip on the leather lounges that
commanded the room.
Weedon, accompanied by a gold-mounted
umbrella and a white-and-amber wife, made
a speech at Lucas, and Lucas made a speech
back, while a squadron of Lucas daughters,
magnificently dressed, supported Mrs. Lucas
in neat black and a defiantly homely air.
She seemed to be saying: “You can have
your frill if you like, but please leave me
out of it.” The chief worry about the new
lounge is that the pillars block the view.
You can never see just who comes in with
the other cat. But perhaps the pillars have
been put in for that reason. The entrance
is made of more gold bricks under a granite
archway. It looks like a cross between a
church door and the front of Aladdin’s cave.
All the fashionables walked the Block carry-
ing their invitation cards like shields. The
giant pasteboards were about a foot square,
faced completely with gilt, the lettering in
white.
' At Colac, where the sheep swarm in a
thousand paddocks and fatted farmers
occupy the rest of the country, the local
medical help is mostly of the old-established
kind closely tied down to its job. Yet in
one way and another the physicking brother-
hood is well represented at the Front; and
now Dr. Arthur Nankervis, of a later gene-
ration, has got into a uniform and gone off
to do what he can to keep Fritz out of the
Western District for good. He left with a
kit full of good wishes.
Brigadier-General Sellheim had a welcome-
home of which he is inordinately proud.
When he reached Melbourne there were 15
children at Spencer-street station to meet
him. They rushed him with howls of glee.
The General has no youngsters of his own,
but his playfellows are always children.
One small daughter of a military man here
said despairingly, “It’s no use; he’ll be too
grown up to play with now, because he is a
brigadier-general.” But he wasn’t!
Major Rod Howe has buckled on his Sam
Brown belt again after his recent severe
illness. He is in charge of Broadmeadows,
having been forbidden further active service
abroad at present; so Hilda Greene Howe
has established herself comfortably at her
Toorak house and put the boxes away.
Mrs. Watt, who has all her three sons
and her son-in-law in France, spends her
time cutting sandwiches for Y.M.C.A. cus-
tomers in St. Kilda-road. Last week she
presented the Y.M.O.A. quarters with a
huge Union Jack which she bought at one
of the numerous auction junkets.
Mrs. Stead is giving a party next Friday
at the Repertory Club. Her captured lioness
is Major Richardson, a returned military
nurse with a lovely uniform to show. Other
returned nurses will be there and have
promised to “say • a few words”' from the
platform.
Mrs. Donald Mac-kinnon just ; waited to
vote “Yes” before speeding off to England
with elder daughter Hilary to meet the sol-
dier sons.
The Maurice Gibbs pair had hardly got
back from a health trip to Japan before they
were off to Bananaland to avoid the Vic-
torian frosts, which don’t agree with the
head of the house. Now they are returning
to their Toorak headquarters for the summer.
Mrs. Gibbs, a pretty, fragile girl, is the
daughter of one William Gordon Gumming,
Bart.
On the night of the combined school
sports, Margot Mcßean hostessed a flapper
dance-party at her mother’s South Yarra
place. On the oak-panelled ballroom walls,
which look as if they had been borrowed
from a society novel, the light and dark blue
Grammar flags hung amongst a few thousand
roses, and hostess Margot looked a dream in
early-Victorian draperies of olive silk, with
a hand-posy of little pink roses. The faith-
ful De Gilio gave a friendly smile to 20
ex-Grammarians who came up from camp
for a farewell two-step. It was a great
evening. But handsome Mrs. Norman
Faulkner was severely censured by the
youngsters for wearing a creation of purple
and gold. As soon as the boys saw it they
shouted, “Those are Wesley’s colors!”
The heroic Mrs. Campbell, who defied the
dangers of a flooded river and risked her
life to rescue hubby’s prize sheep, is none
other than the Monty Langdon widow’s
pretty second daughter Dorothy. She has
been staying at South Yarra under the
maternal wing to have her injuries attended
to. One hand won’t be out of bandages for
weeks. The lady bears her honors modestly.
The death of Andrew Loughnan at St.
Ivilda removes a popular personality and a
silvery songster. The Loughnan brothers,
Andrew and Wilfred, were squattocrats in
N. S. Wales until an endless drought
emptied their pockets. The pretty Mrs.
Andrew was Miss Mac Lean, of Edgecliff,
Sydney.
Philip Blashki died last week, aged SO.
He founded the Jewish Aid Society and was
one of the originators of the Victorian
Employers’ Federation.' Early artistic Mel-
bourne remembers son Myer, a beautiful
young man with a thick pompadour of black
hair and a not-too-pronounced. Jewish nose.
He was one of the adorers who was smiled
on by the cold and beautiful Mrs. Maesmore
Morris. Myer had romantic and artistic
yearnings and longed to live the life of a
painter. His father frowned on this idea —-
it was before the big-money days of Streeton,
Longstaff, Fox and co. Nevertheless Myer
dabbled away without dad's encouragement,
until finally he joined up with a fellow-artist
and with him worked a passage to America.
There Blashki’s good looks stood liim in
good stead and married him to a .multi-
millionairess. He soon set up a fashionable
studio in New York and had reams of notices
in the papers; whereupon father gave him
his blessing and spent his later years show-
ing friends laudatory notices of “my son.”
Justice Isaacs, his wife and Associate
daughter Nancy have trundled back to Mel-
bourne from Sydney. Duty kept dad and
Nancy there, and it happened to fit in very
well with the races. They put up at Coogee.
The Brighton Nashes had a tea-party the
other day to entertain the Bananaland Ham-
monds, Miss Conroy, of Sydney, and some
others. Young Hammond will be an aviator
presently: meantime he is engaged to Miss
Conroy, who made the Vienna Cafe haunters
stare. She entered quietly and demurely in
a navy-blue coat with the newest cartridge
pleats ; but that wasn’t what stopped busi-
ness—it was her beauty. Merely pretty
girls dropped back helpless with their
powder puffs in their hands, and ugly ones
rushed off to sue their parents.
Mrs. George Chirnside and daughter
Audrey are returning home pretty soon to
escape the London fog—and other things.
Rumor mutters that crowds of Anglo-Aus-
tralians threaten to steam back to. their
native gum-trees to elude the double income
tax. Pity the poor rich !
Melba has cancelled her projected trip to
India, her doctor having advised her to
avoid the heat. He probably saw by her
eye that she didn’t want to go there, anyhow.
Adelaide’s billet-doux: —
In response to an appeal from the Minister of
Industry, the metropolitan racing clubs organised a
patriotic race-meeting at Morphettville on Saturday,
and raked up a good cheque for the Repatriation
Fund. £72 was added to tlie collection by the
sale of Ivosai, a horse presented by A. D. McDonald
to Lady Galway. Allan Bowman bought him at
the finish. The dearest animal, woman, put on her
plainest coat and skirt, topped with a dust-coat, and
spent most of her time persuading her hat not to
take precipitate flight.
Mrs. George Aldridge and the Frank Fishers have
berths in the Medina. Mrs. Aldridge is bound for
England and the Fishers for Bombay, where their
son is. The Garrison Artillery is his little lot.
The engagement is announced of the Peter M oods’
second daughter, Joyce, to the W. B. Wilkinsons’
elder son, Keith, who has a commission in the
A.I.F. Incidentally the bride-elect’s uncle, Lieut.
W. A. Wood, who has been with the Scottish Horse
since the early days of the war, has cabled that he
is on his way to Egypt.
Died at Medindie on Saturday, Adam Adamson,
who was for years lion, secretary of the Blind
School and the Home for Incurables. He was also
a trustee of the James Brown Estate. Of his two
daughters the elder, Mrs. Barringer, is a well-known
painter of S. Australian scenery. White gums are
her specialty.
Qecil Bray, of Glenelg', is to be one of the Bed
Cross unit which is under orders for Roumania,
with Dr. O’Leary in command. Cecil left Adelaide
last year to volunteer for ambulance work, and has
since put in a useful time in France.
The proceeds of the first concert of the Adelaide
Women’s Choir are to go to the Eugene Alderman
Memorial Scholarship Fund. The date is fixed for
November 4.
A Government sanatorium for consumptive soldiers
is to be established at Bedford Park on property
bought by the Peake Government. There are about
40 such patients in our military hospitals.
Herbert Hughes, who died at Booyoolie station,
near Gladstone, last week, was a link in a family
closely identified with the early history of the State,
especially in its agricultural and pastoral develop-
ment. His wife died some years ago, and his one
daughter and sole surviving child was married about
a year ago to Roy Davidson, one of the Wigg clan.
Amadio was born to the
Pipes of Pan as some are
born to the purple. Papa
Amadio was a flautist genius
who transmitted his wiz-
ardry to his sons, more par-
ticularly John. Now John
has grown no and attained
the rank of Husband, but he
is still looked upon as the
Boy Wonder by adoring matrons and mams.
They catch this Peter Pan sometimes and feed
him with afternoon-tea at fashionable cafes.
One gushing tea-stewed dame (size five feet
high by six feet round) shrilly exclaimed
lately on one such occasion : “If I were
only a girl again, I’d be madly in love with
Amadio.”
Frederick Bevan, of the Elder Gonserva-
torium, Adelaide, naturally makes the best
defence he can against Melba’s slaughterous
criticism of the vocal education of . Doris
Beech, winner of the Melba Scholarship. As
a teacher of 20 years’ experience Bevan feels
that he “may be permitted to advise Melba
at the beginning of her career as a singing
teacher.” This attitude towards the most
gifted exponent of voice-production in the
world may bring a smile, but it won’t carry
conviction. Melba is the most famous pupil
of Marcliesi, to whom the divine fire was
handed down from Garcia the Great. Aus-
tralians are given to regarding the Queen
of Song with delightful familiarity as one
of the family. They do not all realise that
she receives the homage of the world not
only as the Voice but as the intellect behind
the Voice. Having taught Doris for five
years, Bevan thinks he should know better
than this peerless woman who only heard the
girl at the scholarship test. But what has
Bevan been teaching her for? It is not. for
the cook to say that the meal*is delicious
because she took a week to prepare it. The
connoisseur who eats it can decide that in
10 minutes. That is what the cook worked
for.
Bevan stresses the point that several great
singers began their careers on one voice and
sang their way to greatness on another. lie
quotes baritone Santley, who began as a
tenor, and the very uncertain tenor Sims
Reeves, who is asserted to have started as
a bass. But the fact is these voices sur-
vived the early mistakes. llow many others
have been ruined? Besides, Bevan is per-
haps not aware that Santley got his correct
training from Garcia, the teacher of
Marchesi.
Many Australians hope that their repre-
sentative singer will establish a Melba Con-
servatorium here. If that happened Aus-
tralia would be the Mecca of the vocal
world. The voices might come here to be
trained —as for years they went to Marchesi
and Paris.
Iris Connolly, the 10-year-old daughter
of racing-man Eric, galloped past the
winning-post in the R.M.C. examinations
last week. She carried a big weight ot
piano knowledge, and her colors were (and
are) blue eyes and golden curls. The clever
youngster has a tine platform manner. She
explained gravely to the examiner that she
had been handicapped by six weeks absence
from study helping Dad watch the Sydney
races. The win under these circs, showed
all the better form.
Melba has another scheme in hand. A
Conservatorium singing class is to be estab-
lished in Sydney, as well as Melbourne, and
the pupils will be inspected by the diva her-
self. She will make frequent trips between
Melbourne and Sydney, and look after the
girls in each capital. Fritz Hart has visited
Sydney and laid the rails.
From “Rosna,” in London :
The Australian Red Cross panjandrums on this side
have made the Australians in London rage and roar
furiously by advertising for people to “adopt” Aus-
tralian prisoners of war in Germany! There s a
general feeling that, in view of all the money we
’Roo-landers are handing- out to other nations, we
could surely spare enough to care for our own
prisoners, instead of asking strangers to do it for us.
There are 450 of them to date, and the cost is ss. a
head per week —not such an enormous liability that
wa need to hold out the hat to others. The Aus-
tralian community here can’t imagine what the
Australian Red Cross people could have been thinking
of.
Nellie Stewart has been in Melbourne on
a brief visit, daughter Nancye being at the
Royal. Incidentally, a Collins-street photo-
grapher has crowded a showcase with por-
traits of Sweet Nell, who still manages to
wear the sweet, sad look of a beauty in the
twenties. Some wretched man in court once
asked Nell her age while she stood in the
witness-box. “I was 17!” she murmured
naively.
Ruby Hooper, a young Australian, wrote
a play called “The Fair Gipsy” and staged
it at the St. Kilda Town Hall on Thursday
night. She played the leading part, stage-
managed the whole show, coached the rest
of the players, designed dances and taught
children to dance them, and added a song-
and-dance programme to the lot. She is a
popular young bundle of energy with fluffy
fair hair and a very pretty face, and two-
shilling patrons came at her bidding and
filled the Town Hall. Flappers who were
there say gushingly that the show was far
beyond “So Long, Letty” in entertainment.
That is' a criterion, for flappers simply
adored “Letty” and cherish memories of the
nights they were shouted to- the frivol.
Melba .did her bit in the Pageant at the
Playhouse. She chose nine of her singing-
girls from the Conservatorium and polished
them up for Cherry Ripe. She even stage-
managed the group and spent the afternoon
“making up” the nine young faces. A lot
of Melba’s genius consists of taking pains.
When Marlow cut his connection with the
Italian Opera Company he arranged the
details with one Carpi, an opera manager
who followed the Italians out here. Carpi
speaks English perfectly with his mouth, and
all languages with his flashing black eyes.
He is taking a company out India way w T hen
this venture pulls through.
L. K. S. Mackinnon has found a fine place
for his racehorses. He recently bought
Chatswood Park, on the Goulburn, near
Tabilk. The lovely place used to belong to
squatter Patrick Power Hickey; but after
his death the daughters sold it to trainer
Redfern, who has just completed a deal with
Mackinnon.

ROUND DARLINGHURST WAY.
Some local fashions. Hurriedly sketched by a Bulletin artist who had lost his
way in a back street of Sydney while making' for the Young Men’s Christian Association.
“Reka”: The trawlers working outside
Auckland Harbor (M.L.) bring up some strange
residents from the depths, but the queerest creature
that has yet come to light is the porcupine fish.
When properly inflated he takes the shape of a
prickly white football, with a face on one side that
is uncannily human —a wide, scarlet mouth, two
large, mournful eyes, and even the suggestion of a
nose. Collapsed, he is merely a pulpy white bag.
Nobody offers to eat him. It would seem like
cannibalism.
“Rag” : Once saw 4S mokes hauling on a
vehicle. A jinker laden with five tons of provisions
for the Oaks rush (N.Q.) got bogged in the black-
soil-and-pudding-stone flat t’other side of Quartz Hill.
The teamsters hired, borrowed, or temporarily stole
all the prads within a five-mile radius, in an
endeavor to get out of the mess. They and the 10
or a dozen others who helped them, wearing nothing
but their shirts and stockwhips, stood knee-deep in
the ooze cursing and flaying in simultaneous effort.
But the harder the nags strove the deeper the waggon
sunk. At length, recognising- the hopelessness of
the job, the crowd made camp and lingered for five
weeks until the muck stiffened.
* * » *
“Verdad”: The South American method
of yoking "bullocks can give some points to the
Australian style. Over there the bullocks are lim-
bered up fairly wide apart, by a yoke of wood shaped
to fit across the base of the horns. (No Polled Angus
need apply.) The yoke is firmly strapped to the
horns so that it is held rigid. It appears cruel, but
I have heard no complaints from the steers, and they
don't look any more worried than their brothers in
Australia. The advantages are various. ’ Two-wheeled
carts are easily controlled, and nearly all the
carts there are two-wheeled. The Chileans
do some neat work with bullocks. When heavy tim-
ber is being loaded on to a vehicle the team lower
their heads, and a chain is hitched round the logs
and on to their yoke. Then the animals raise their
heads, and lift and push the timber aboard. They
are controlled chiefly by voice, aided by a goad.
“Tasman”: How many ways are there
of hanging gates in the bush ? I’ve them
swinging on factory-made hinges (rare) ; on iron
loops made of old bullock bows, the bow being
narrowed and the shafts driven through auger-holes
(fairly common) ; on an iron loop at top, with the
bottom revolving in a hole in a piece of wood
sunken until the top was three or four inches above
tire ground; on loops made of hoop-iron and of
leather (both used for light gates only). Another
gate is made with the upright prolonged both ways.
The top is rounded and fits into an auger-hole in a
piece of slab nailed across the top of the post,
while the bottom is likewise rounded and turns in
a socket made either of wood, as described above, or
the bottom of a beer bottle. These are the main
divisions, but there are minor varieties. With the
factory-made hinges, f’rinstance, Wirewhiskers can
use either the one-piece kind or the sort that goes
in two chunks, the head of the shaft fitting on a_
spike driven in the post. With the latter variety
there’s nothing-ho stop the first rag-time bull that
comes along from lifting the gate off its hinge’s.
The one-piece kind make better hinges, but they
have their drawbacks. The wandering he-cow can
still heave the contraption skyward and prance on to
the harem, leaving ruin in his tracks.
“Nymboi”: Some months ago, on the Bar-
rington River, a tributary of the Manning (N.S.AV.),
a draft of fat bullocks was being yarded to be
trucked to Sydney. One was missed, and search
failed to locate him. Three weeks afterwards two
Wingham men came across an old powder magazine,
which had been used during the construction of the
North Coast railway line. Out of curiosity they
looked inside, and there was his nibs, fast in a trap
of his own making. Except for loss of condition
he was none the worse.
THE AUSTRALIAN SLANGUAGE.
THE NEW T EACHER: “Make a sentence containing the word ‘among.'”
BILLY BLUEGUM : “Mathieson’s dorg is a mong.”
THE NEW T EACHER: “Well, finish the sentence, Billy. Among what?”
BILLY (bright boy): “Among good dorgs, Miss.”
“Pick”: Is there any portion of Aus-
tralia’s crust inaccessible to the bullock puncher?
The deep, valleys of the Shoalhaven (N.S.W.) are
scored with his zigzag trails, and once the Lett
brothers, of Kiandra, crossed with their bullock
waggons even Lobb’s Hole, where the parrots open
their umbrellas before attempting to float down the
hillsides. The Letts were delivering timber from a
Bago country sawmill to the. Yarrangobilly River
bridge. They drew the lumber to the rim of the
tableland, and without unhitching the teams paid
out the unloaded waggons at the ends of wire ropes
with turns round convenient trees. It was a 1500 ft.
drop in three-quarters of a mile. Then they released
the timber, reloaded it at the bottom, and scrambled
on up the cliffs to the other side of the chasm. The
brothers saved 50 miles by making this hazardous
short cut, but the worries and broken gear decided
them that the longest way back was the sweetest way
home.

“T. McM.”: Just two months before the
war a German arrived at Thursday Island, char-
tered a launch and set off on a “special mission”
through the islands of Torres Strait, the which
islands are famous throughout the
world for the variety and uncommon-
ness of their orchids. At Badu, one
of the biggest dots, Dogsausage en-
gaged the whole village, men, women
and children, to search for the plants,
and in a few weeks the island was
denuded of them as if the forests had
been swept by a plague. When the
vandalism was complete, Dogsausage
objected to pay the natives their
rightful wages as required by the
law of Queensland, and he resorted
to all sorts of Hunnish tricks until
the Department came down with a
heavy hand, and he had reluctantly
to square up. BufP on doing so he
remarked: “Well, you won’t find
many orchids on . Vour islands after
I am gone.” He spoke the truth—•
for a change. Orchids are now so
scarce that a search would not bring
in one a week. The others were
taken to Berlin and sold at fancy
prices. >
“Reka”: Maoriland orchardists, farmers,
agricultural experts and acclimatisation enthusiasts
are considering- the opossum, and examining his
references. The orchardist complains that the ’poss
dines gloriously on apple-bloom and young fruit,
and the farmer declares he has met him under sus-
picious circumstances in his crops, but the acclima-
tisation enthusiast asserts that the value of the little
chap's skin transcends all other considerations. The
decision arrived at so far is that the recruit
shall be given an exemption provided he signs an
agreement to confine himself wholly to a bush diet.
Otherwise —Outski! The trouble with all the
immigrant pests in M.L. has been that they never
adhered to the purpose for which they were im-
ported, but allowed themselves to be led astray by
the variety of temptations awaiting them. Stoats,
ferrets and weasles, invited in to exterminate the
rabbits, early discovered that the native birds and
their eggs were easier victims, so that while bunny
frisked unmolested the bush birds rapidly neared
extinction.
“A.E.R.” : This is the communique as my
Tanna (New Hebrides) boy heard of it and the
report of our interview about it:—•
“Master, altogeder manbush he speak all asame.
English more Garman he fight two fella he make a
line. Captain belong English he tell all man belong
him he shoot. Now Garman he shoot altogeder. Now
some more English soldier he come. Captain belong
him he tell him he shoot Garman he shoot uni
altogeder finish. Now Garman he go go, he go along
house belong King America, he go all about. King
America he hear um he talk. He say, ‘All Garman
he come, might he want speak along me fella.’
Now he lift um up had belong him. Two fella
Garman he shoot. He killum King America dead
finish. Now Garman he go go, be putum King
America along coffin. He want to keep him sikus
lhuns. He leave him. He stop. By-m-by coffin he
break. King America he go up along Heaven. Gar-
man he been shoot um horse belong King America
and he put him along beeg fella case. He leave him.
He stop. By-m-by case he smash up. Horse be-
long King America he go up along Heaven. You
find um dat news along paper belong you master?
He true or he no true master?”
“I don’t think it’s true, 'Tom; he gammon talk
belong manbush.”
“0, tank you, master. Me go tellum Chief belong
me fella.”

WHEN TOMMY CAME MARCHING HOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Devine came back the other day.
We’d planned a great home-cornin’.
No long trombone we had to play,
No fine, heroic drummin’.
With two sticks and a milk-can Borne
Put up a martial clatter,
While Carter blew a bullock-horn.
Says Tom Devine, with healthy scorn:
“Gorstruth! what is the matter ?”
We set three colored petticoats
From Baker’s chimneys blowin’
(’Tis not the bravest flag that floats,
Yet ’twas the finest goin’) ;
We cheered our hero all we knew,
No song of praise neglectin’,
To show our pride as he limped through.
He merely spat and snorted, “Who
“The ’ell are yous expectin’ ?”
They lured him to my shop somehow,
And sued for news of battle.
Says Tom: “Who rides the mail track now ?
Who’s herdin’ Stringer’s cattle?”
A dint the Turk put in his head
He covers with a ringlet.
He’d won a medal, so we read.
“I might ’ave ’ad it pinched,” he said—
“l’ve sewn it in my singlet!”
Says Cole: “But, ’strutli, you must ’ave seen
A fearful swag of scrapjMn’.”
And Tom agrees: “Where men are keen
That’s pretty sure to ’appen.
One night a little bloke from Hay
Who plugged a Pentridge warder
Got such a doin’ that at day, ' 1
Amazed, they ticked him for a stray ‘
Distinguished Service Order.
“Then Sydney Bob was rather yexed
With Green—who’d pinched his braces.
That was ‘continued in our next’
In harf a score of places.
McCubbin threw his grub at Lea
(You know how sticky stew is) ;
They fought till neither man could see.
You talk of fight —Qorstrike me, we
Saw stacks of it at Suez!”

"Xgatik”: Before the whalemeat-eating
controversy is forgotten, let me put in a word for
the sea-elephant. The flesh of this mammal, in
comparison with the whale’s, is as whiting to dugong.
An ox's tongue is not in it with that of my client,
and I never eat his liver without thinking of pork.
And that reminds me. Someone said weka was no
good to cat. He is a land agent. On the Mac-
quaries, where the bird was introduced many years
ago by one JGlder, it invariably forms the main
portion of the lonely sealers' Sunday dinner and is
considered a great delicacy. The oil skimmed from
the cooking pot is a sure cure for rheumatism. Ask
any of Maw-son’s men and see if they don’t praise
both elephant and weka as grub.
* * * *
“Barcoo” I don’t want to reopen the big-
tree argument, but I must put on record a giant red
gum that upended - itself in Gippsland (Vic.). Years
ago, when Cobb and Co. provided the only trans-
port between Gippsland and Melbourne, this big
fellow flourished not far from Bairnsdale. Portion
of the stump had been burnt right out, leaving the
still-alive tree supported by two fangs at opposite
sides. The driver of the mail coach had the ground
portion of the butt chipped down, and it was a
source of pride and pleasure to him to drive his six-
horse vehicle loaded with freight and passengers
straight through the cavity. Dinkum.
“Novus”: Ten or eleven years ago John
Ross delivered a 16-ton boiler at the Dempsey mine
(Vic.). On the journey from the Mansfield railway sta-
tion he had the collaboration of 4.3 horses. Mud, blas-
phemy and despair were always prominent. Abo.-
liars with any knowledge of the country between
Jamieson and Woods Point will understand the size
of the job. Of course, 43 horses were not used
throughout the journey. Once off the main road the
many sharp bends necessitated shortening the line.
For fully 30 miles of the picnic, however, the
procession was led by the full strength of the c-o.
“Splodger” : ll<nv do pa and ma pelican
construct their habitation and where? Some say ma
rends her raiment to build the nest, but probably
that's romance. Pelicans from my experience
patronise a tremendous area. 1 have been charmed
with them on all sides of this continent, except the
under side, as well as on the inland rivers. Once
while camped near Bourke (N.S.W.) awaiting the
liver pilot a commotion set me scurrying to the bank.
It was caused by a great mob of pelicans taking to
the water. The back-pedalling as they alighted
sounded for all the world like a Darling River Noah’s
Ark at full speed.

“Nick Bottom”: As a man who is person-
ally acquainted with the accused, let me tell “X.Y.”
(B. 10/8/’IG) that the kangaroo-rat won’t eat meat.
1 kept one as a pet for over three years and, though
often tempted, the little animal steadfastly refused
anything but a strictly vegetable diet. Again, the
rat doesn’t smell any worse than many other mar-
supials and not half as bad as the bandicoot. If
“X.Y.” had adhered strictly to the truth he would
have said that the coot is the stinker, and although
even the most self-respecting dog aches to kill him
on sight, the most depraved mongrel refuses to eat
him. This is not the case with the ’roo-rat, for I
have frequently given his remains to the dogs as a
tit-bit. Finally, I’ll bet “X.Y.” a brace of rums
that he can’t tell the difference between curried
’roo-rat and spring chicken.

“The Reefer” : The discussion as to what
happens when a gun with a blocked barrel is fired
brings to my memory a Papuan native policeman
who got a cleaning-rag jambed in the barrel of his
.303 carbine. He brought the trouble to his Euro-
pean patrol officer and asked, “Which way you
think I gettim out this feller, taubada?” “What,
you crimson idiot! You no-savvy cleanem gun?”
snapped the boss. “Gimme one feller cartridge,
quick feller.” Click snapped the breech-lock. Bang
went the bomb. Wh-rrrr! Biff! ! It took me a
long while to convince those two that they weren't
dead. In fact, I’m not sure that the nigger is con-,
vinced yet.
“Frank S.”: When is someone going to
investigate the question of the drying-up of the rich
Western District of Victoria? This desiccation has
been going on now for half a century. In 1844
John Thompson, of Kielarnbete, camped on the bank
of Lake Terang. It was then a noble sheet of
water of a tout 700 acres, and
■ was actually running - over at one
corner. Thompson was the only-
white man to see it overflow.
Since then it has been drying-up
steadily, until last year it was as
parched as a wowser’s sideboard.
Lake Kielarnbete is also going-
dry ; but its extra deptli is
enabling it to last better. When l
was a boy, about 30 years ago,
Kielarnbete was much bigger. It
is now some 40ft. below the level
of those days. What is the explana-
tion? Surely a phenomenon of this importance is
worth some scientific study ?

“Oueyn”: A sufferer from dengue fever
and lung troubles recently returned to the Victorian
Mallee for a spell. Living among the farmers he
says he finds great benefit from inhaling the smoke
of the pine. Canada advertises its pines deafeningly,
but there is no necessity for Australians to buy
steamer tickets. Any doubter may have the name
of the party benefiting.
“Aneb”: A lot of rabbits having en-
trenched themselves on a hillside, I was advised to
get to work with gas. So I invested in a drum of
carbon bisulphide, and now find all hands in dis-
agreement as to how it should be used. Some say
that it's only effective when the ground is wet, i.e.,
in winter, and others declare this to be bunkum.
Some “experts” maintain that the stuff ought to be
“fired” before the burrows are sealed, and they are
flatly contradicted by other argumentative persons.
Would someone who knows definitely state whether
the most effective method of use is as liquid fire or
gas, and if operations can be carried on irrespective
of the season ?
"Xgntik”: “Pick” (B. 10/S/’Hi) inquires
about the marine monster that preys upon the
penguin. During a jaunt to the Auckland Islands
1 observed a sea-leopard, commonly known as the
grey seal, dart upon an unsuspecting Victoria penguin
and swallow it. I have killed several of these
seals, and in each case found penguins' feathers
decorating the bingy. By the way, does anyone know
the breeding haunts of the sea-leopard? The beast's
alias at the Royal Society is Hvdrurga leptonyx,
and when it visits the Australian coasts it generally
mb Ices the voyage in pairs.
“The Hun”: If “Corundum” (B.
5/10/T6) will allow a house, to be a waggon within
the meaning of the Act this is my big team. When
Orme’s store was erected at Green’s Gunyah, before
the present town of Lockhart (N.S.W.) appeared on
the map, it was built on pine logs, so that it could
be pulled on to the site in the new town when the
surveyor had got through with his job. Corney
Bros., teamsters, hitched 48 horses on to the em-
porium when moving time came. The distance was
only about six furlongs, but it took two days to
do it.
“Barooo” : The kangaroo mouse, the comi-
cal little marsupial that is two or three times the
size of the ordinary mouse, makes a nest of very
fine grasses in secluded places well away from obser-
vation. Recently one was caught in its residence at
Billinbah, Narandera (N.S.W.). There was a cluster
of nine young ones adhering to the small mother
in the best “kangaroo-on-the-teat” manner. The
little lady’s pouch was too small to accommodate
the whole darned family, and some of them had
to be content with a head inside and the rest of
their bodies out in the cold.
‘Tiny”: When Fat Dick died the whole
camp went into mourning for a day and a night in
a tasteful garb of white kaolin. They were a - weird-
looking crowd, their hair plastered with as much of
the clay-as it would hold adding particularly to the
frightfulness. The boss ordered the body to be
buried a good distance from the homestead, and I
was told to bullock-dray Dick to his last home. He
was rolled in an old skin rug and then wound round
and round from head to feet with a thin rope
woven of hair from the mourners’ heads. The chief
Binghi walked ahead of the sad and variegated pro-
cession, prodding the ground occasionally with a yam
stick until he found a soft spot in which to dig the
bone-pit. This was made just deep enough to hold
the remains, which were then covered with earth. A
stick was placed upright at each corner of the grave
and brushwood was stacked on top to a height of
four feet. With that the ceremony ended. After
tile burial the name of the departed is never men-
tioned, Binghi believing that to do such a thing
would be very unlucky.
* * * *
“Jans Tas." : Anybody ever heard of the
medicinal properties of the bracken fern? As
youngsters whenever we were stung b\- jackjumper
or bulldog ants we used to grub up a fern, get its
root, break it and apply it to the affected part. If
the juice was rubbed in well the pain would vanish.
“Waikonini” : Ilawkes Bay (M.L.) Philo-
sophical Society has been helping itself to some
recollections of the good old days. Amongst them
was the record of one F. .7. Tiffen, who in 1849
isolated himself with a flock of sheep in a corner of
Godforgotten. For three years he lived there prac-
tically alone. A while after he had the monotony
broken for him by being called to Wellington to
give evidence in a murder trial. Carrying his swag
he sauntered 340 miles. He said it was a welcome
eh a nge.

“The Ratcatcher’s Daughter” : I thought
there were some big eels in the lagoon at Napier
(M.L.), and once 1 fancied I saw the taniwlia.
which is the father of all cels and wears ears. But
Prospect Reservoir, which holds up Sydney’s water
supply, had a surprise for me. ’The other night, a
flood night, great numbers of eels passed over the
by-wash of the dam. We got some on the flats that
measured 4ft. 6in. from rudder to whiskers and were
as thick as a J. C'. Williamson chorus matron's leg.
A WATERSCAPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlio “crick” was “up” at Dungaree.
The dreary yellow waters spread
As far as human ‘eye could see,
Encumbered with the drowned and dead ;
And robbed of every horn and hoof,
Cobb perched upon his stable roof.
Came Tonkin drifting on a door,
A fragment of the local church.
Lugubrious and damp and sore
lie joined his neighbor on the perch,
lie said: “You haven't seen to-day
My missus pass along this way?”
Cobb dumbly shook his dripping head.
And Tonkin breathed an oath. “I hoped
They’d foundered somewhere here,” he said,
“For she and Ryley have eloped.
I wouldn't care a single darn,
But, ’struth, the beggars took the barn !"

MOUSINGS AT THE FRONT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Silence of battle, and a stormy night!
God snores contented in His cosy skies,
While fraught with fever of a world of lies
The west wind coughs, and blows her nose with
might.
Come up, O Cis! Behold this mortal sight,
Poor little animal- with glassy eyes 1
Look slippy, for the kids want fresh supplies.
What dead men leave is ours by common right.
Lift up your tail, Matilda, for you tread
Where nice, clean angels would not dare to wait.
What’s that? Ah ha! Looks like a piece of cheese.
Allow me, dear, to taste. These Huns I dread.
Ah! Hum !... .Help ! Lord, I've struck a poisoned
bait. . . .
Hie!.... Good-night, nurse! Hie!....Kiss the
patient, please.

EQUIPMENT TYRES.
This conveys little to the ordinary Car buyer, but to the
man who knows it means a lot. It means the difference
between low priced light-weight tyres-—and heavy-weight
covers requisite for satisfactory tyre service on our roads.
To keep the selling price of Cars down the light cover is
invariably fitted, and the unenlightened Car buyer-
knowing no better—accepts the light equipment tyres.
Here’s an instance—one Car sold in this Country comes
out equipped with 815 x 105 m/m tyres. The Agent wrote
the maker asking if he could have Cars minus tyres, so
that he could use Australian goods “Yes”—was the reply
—“we will allow a rebate of 53 Dollars (£ll 1/-) per set.”
Similar tyres are sold out here for £44 6/- per set. The
“Dunlop” price for heavier and better tyres is £3B 11/-
A comparison of the weight of these imported tyres with
DUNLOP TYRES
(Australian-made) is instructive and points a moral:—
Dunlop—4 Covers 82 lbs., 4 Tubes 16 lbs. —98 lbs.
Imported—4 Covers 671b5., 4 Tubes 10 lbs. — 77lbs.
A difference of 21 lbs. weight in material, to say nothing
of the higher grade rubber we use. This extra weight of
the best quality rubber and fabric means thousands of
miles more service than you can expect or get from the
light-weight tyre equipment on most imported Cars. Do
you want this class of tyre—and the troubles incidental
to light covers and tubes ? You probably don’t. You can
avoid light tyres by ordering “Dunlops”—heavy high-
grade tyres suitable for Australian road conditions.

“Bad Australian”,: I have just come off a
500-kilo, ride through Paraguay, and during
that barbaric excursion I saw more oranges
than I supposed this world to contain—
sweet, sour and bitter oranges,'cultivated in
gardens and paddocks, and train-loads hurry-
ing south to Buenos Aires for distribution
throughout the Argentine; and all of them
as nothing compared with those which grow
wild. The vast Paraguayan rnontes, very
similar to N. S. Wales North Coast or
Queensland scrubs, are profuse with the
trees. Between Tacuati and San Pedro, on
the River Ypane, the track runs through a
monte which for five leagues is practically a
wild-orange grove. Hundreds of thousands
of tons of the produce rot annually in the
Paraguayan mantes, and although sugar
grows practically without cultivation, no-
body seems to be making any attempt to
establish what might be the biggest mar-
malade and fruit-extract factory on the
planet. Men are scarce and excessively
tired in Paraguay, and the women (who
are in a great majority) attend to the
banana and mandioca patch, and the roach-
backed hog, and the never-empty cradle,
while the boss lounges in the shade, _ smoking
his native cigar and imbibing cana, while
he strums an old guitar or pumps a wheezy
accordion.
* * * *
“Gliolmondley Fitzmaggot” writes from
Salisbury Plain (in the Gold Country) :
A digression from war, to talk for a minute on
margarine. One seldom sees butter here; it has
become too expensive as an article of common diet.
So the much-maligned margarine, or butter sub-
stitute, takes its place in the messes of both officeis
and men. Its enforced use is steadily undermining
the rather unreasonable prejudice that has for long
existed in the heads of many Australians against
this synthetic cow-grease. It is wholesome and
palatable, and many a good cow-cocky soldier has
eaten pounds of it before he realised the fact that
it was not the true product of child-slavery. There
is, and rightly too, a healthy commercial repug-
nance t 6 the practice of retailing margarine as
butter, and it should continue to exist; but mar-
garine, as margarine, is another proposition. One
of the minor results of the war will be the breaking
down of popular prejudice against butter substitute.
The dairy-farmers of Bacchus Marsh, the Northern
Rivers and the Darling Downs will mumble curses
into their nicotine-smeared beards, but, on the other
hand, it may bring the kid of the cow country
back into some of his own. Genuine butter, as an
article of diet for the soldier, has ceased to be; it
is as dead as the voice of Reason at a Union meeting
discussing conscription.

“Dead Finish” : Any bad Australian who
wants to balance the Chow when he comes
around the farm buying “fat” has my per-
mission to use this recipe. Mix a couple of
packets of cornflour in about half a kero-
sent tin of water. Then render down half
a kerosene tin of fat and infuse one into
the other. When this sets I’ll wager the
Dingbat won’t be able to detect it from
clean fat. It is miles ahead of the better-
known gidya ashes recipe. I’ve tried both.
“Cam”: A Melbourne firm is showing a
hitherto incredulous Australia that even if
the foreigner was wiped off the list we
would still be able to get a clock. Woods
used in casing the locally manufactured
article include fiddleback, blackwood, Queens-
land maple, silky oak, rosewood, walnut and
Tasmanian hardwood. The last, I think,
looks the best of the lot, the grain showing
up splendidly when polished.

“F.H.”: It’s against the law in Victoria
to take money for telling a fellow-citizen
what the future holds for him, unless the
telling is done by a doctor or a clergyman.
But one fortune-teller’s loss is another’s
gain, for the Ass in Victoria doesn t prevent
the Maoriland prophet from seeking in Vic-
toria the spoil which the local soothsayer
is forbidden to touch. He does it by ads.
in the newspapers, and Webster,
obligingly forwards the money and no ques-
tions asked.
* * *
“F.N.T.”: An interesting item at the
A.N.A. exhibition, to be held in Melbourne
next January, will be an aeroplane-body
made entirely of Australian timbers. Avia-
tor Basil Watson, lately back in Victoria
from England, is to show it. It will hardly
be the first all-Australian-timber machine,
however; and many tractor-blades, which
are built in several pieces and are the most
important piece of timber in an aeroplane’s
works, have been made from Australian
stuff. The Defence Department’s machines
are thus furnished —those made in Austra-
lia, that is ; and some of these blades look
handsome enough to hang over the fireplace
in the library.
* * * *
“R.A.Y.”: It’s a pity for all concerned
that the farm machinery salesman in Aus-
tralia doesn’t know his job better. Some
manufacturers are year by year turning out
improved implements to meet local condi-
tions, but their enterprise fails to return the
best results because of the incompetents who
trade for them. These agents as a rule
know nothing either of the mechanism of
the implement they sell or of the conditions
in which it is expected to work. Their main
qualifications for their job is a mendacious
loquacity, with the result that costly new
implements are to be seen cast aside on
many farms, while the neighbors, alarmed
by these failures, fight shy of the risk and
plod along with old and inefficient types of
farming aids. Manufacturers ought to know
their business best, so I presume that any
increase in it would give them a headache.

“Fax”: A Melbourne pub. whose title
bears evidence of old age is the Sarah Sands;
and not many people seem to know how the
old Sydney-road landmark got its name. The
Sarah Sands was a troopship which caught
fire on a voyage to India in the autumn of
1858. While the vessel was burning the
women and children, wives and families of
the soldiers, were lowered into the boats,
the men staying aboard to fight the flames
and work the pumps. Eventually the fire
was got under and the leak, which had let
in 15ft. of water, was stopped. Women and
children then returned to the devastated
Sarah Sands, and 10 days later she landed
the whole of her human freight at Mauritius.
The story of this remarkable achievement of
seamanship and discipline was reprinted in
one of Thackeray’s “Roundabout Papers” in
the first volume of the Cornhill Magazine
(1860).
* * * *
“Exile”: The cafeteria eating-house is to
be found everywhere in Kingomalleyland.
It is usually a spacious, well-lighted cafe,
very clean and very plain. The visitor goes
along a railed passage, at the end of which
are benches holding hundreds of plates and
dishes containing butter, bread, fruit, stewed
fruit, pickles, preserves, etc. Alongside is a
pile of trays, also a pile of serviettes, each
rolled round two knives, two spoons and a
fork. You grab a tray and serviette and
help yourself from the row of dishes. If
you want any of six kinds of mush a waitress
serves you, or she will carve for you cold
or hot meat or help you to a portion of fish,
or stew, or pudding. You take small pots
of milk or cream and the usual glass of iced
“watter,” without which no self-respecting
Yankee ever eats a meal. Next you reach a
bench where you can get tea, coffee or cocoa
made freshly for you. If you want a boiled
or scrambled or fried egg, or fancy a buck-
wheat cake or a griddle cake or a waffle, you
order it from the waitress and can stand
and see it cooked if you like. You stagger
with your tray to a cash desk; there the
waiter glances over your load of food, adds
the cost mentally and hands you a cash-
register ticket for the amount. You then
go through the brass railing to any table
you choose in the room, and there you find
condiments and sugar. You can take all
the time you please to finish your meal, and
can leave the cafe only by passing a cashier.
The meals are remarkably cheap, well
cooked and good, and you simply wait on
yourself. I’ve had a full breakfast for
and a good luncheon for Is. lAd.
ADVERTISEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said a certain man to me
(Round about us golden weather,
Azure sky and shining sea)
As we drank good wine together,
Tended by a smiling girl:
“Once I had a priceless pearl.
“Having then a plaguey dearth
Of loud talk and boastful phrases,
Though I knew its wondrous worth,
Little did I sing its praises,
Thinking that its lustres choice
Spoke more loud than any voice.
“Up and down the town I went
Waiting (’tis a madcap story)
Till it should make evident
Its rare value and its glory ;
But, by all the stars that throng
In the heavens, I waited long!
“Girl, another round of wine!
As about the world I drifted
(Talk of pearls, and talk of swine!)
Scarce a single snout was lifted
From the trough to greet the light
Of that gem so pure, so white!
“Then I lost it. How or when
Little matters. Waking, sleeping,
Mistress Fortune’s gifts to men
Take a wondrous lot of keeping;
Though we hold them close and fast,
We may lose them at the last.
“Now this paltry thing I wear,
Praising it with ceaseless crying;
And the people throng and stare,
Thinking it a pearl outvying
Sheba’s gems or those that shone
On the breast of Solomon.’’
This the tale he told to me
As we sat at wine together;
At our feet the azure sea
Shining in the golden weather,
And a laughing girl anigh—•
Red of cheek and blue of eye.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
BREAKING IT GENTLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I say, dear boy,” he whispered me,
“You couldn’t let me have a quid?”
I'm “weaker” far than picnic tea ;
I really couldn’t, but I did.
He came on later. “Say, old man,
You couldn’t lend me thirty bob?”
I couldn't, and I never can,
And yet submitted like a yob,
And let the wretch my family rob.
On Wednesday said he: “I suppose
You haven’t got three quid to spare?”
By Friday his exactions rose—
He wanted five. In grim despair
I parted up. What good is thrift
When all one’s pinching, ’twould appear,
But spurs the prodigals to sift
Cash from our clothes, and to adhere
With greedy teeth to either ear?
When Monday came he said: “Old man,
I’m badly off for fifteen pounds.
1 know you’ll lend it if you can.”
I yelled with rage. ’Twas past all bounds.
“One, three, five, fifteen quid. Avaunt!”
He answered with angelic cheek:
“It is the fifteen that I want.
I meant the smaller sums last week
To break it gently, so to speak !”
