EDDYSON.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
“Bad Australian”: Engineer Edwar
Bradley and Captain Angel M. Zuioaga crossed t
most elevated part of the Andes m the balloon
Eduardo Newberry on Saturday, June 24, 191 °’
this Republic of Argentina is howling itself hoarse
over the event. Thc-press acclaimed the voyagers
‘Argentina’s noble sons,” comparing their exploit
with the deeds of Hannibal, Napoleon and the
South American Liberator San Martin, ail of whom
have some reputation for big deeds in lofty Pj ac ® s -
There is no doubt that the first crossing of the
Andes even in a balloon is a very respectable
achievement, and it was only after several
that the feat was accomplished. The Eduardo New-
berry left Santiago de Chile at 8.30 a.m. on Ju e
24, and reached Uspallata, on the Transandine rai
way, Argentine side, 31 hours later. The journey
and landing were -accomplished under ideal condi-
tions. The greatest height reached was 8100 meters
(26,570 ft. approx.) and the lowest temperature
recored 32deg. below zero. The Camara de Deputados
patted the aeronauts on the back and voted each
a gold medal at the expense of the nation. Owing
to repeated failures, and what looks like jealousy
on the part of one or more responsible members or
the Aero Club Argentino, Bradley and Zuioaga were
instructed by the club to abandon their projected
voyage. Either these instructions did not rea.cn
them in time or they were wilfully disobeyed. The
result has been a somewhat discreditable row, end-
ing in the resignation of Bradley and Zuioaga, but
the matter will probably end in a crawl-down on
the part of the club. Bradley is the star air-spoit.
After the death of Edward Newberry through a fail
in Mendoza and the miraculous escape of Macias
when attempting the Transandine flight from Uspal-
lata, he came to the conclusion that the aeroplane
flight was impracticable and got busy on the balloon
idea, with the aid of Zuioaga. The result justified
their faith and labor. But there is a vast differ-
ence between drifting in a balloon and piloting a
’plane across the Andes with temperatures at 3-deg.
below. #
“Adelaide” : Perhaps the oddest thing about
Jack Booth’s new motor-cycle world’s record is the
place where it was made. In the cycling era, and
up to the early days of motor-cars, it was a common-
place of advice to keep off the Adelaide-to-Gawler
road. It is level enough ; but it was vilely kept and
in (say) 1900 to have come over from Vic. to put
up a speed record on it would have been simply
humorous. And now it holds a world’s record. Syd-
ney people needn’t despair of their Parramatta-road,
after all.
* * * *
Back in the long ago, when country clubs
built their own bats, Bungonia and Windellama, the
oldest settlements in Argyle (N.S.W.), had a match.
The Bungonias undertook to provide the bats, but
after travelling 12 miles to the playing ground
remembered they had left the tools at home. So the
match was started with pick-handles, Carter and
Morgan, of the Windellamas, taking strike, while a
horse decorated with a man raced back to Bungonia
for the willow. When the animal returned after
its 24 miles’ flutter Carter and Morgan were still
belting the cover off the ball with the remaining
splinters of their pick-handles, and the enemy was
carrying water in relays to the bowlers.
“X.Y.”: While the burning of motor-
cars can, in many instances, be traced to the care-
less use of naked lights, in many other cases the
origin of the flare-up remains a mystery. Once a
fire has started there is no doubt that its almost
instant spread over the whole of the car is assisted
by the dangerous character of the dressing applied to
the leather, or leather-cloth, of the trimmings. This,
generally, is nothing less than guncotton dissolved in
acetone.' In U.S.A., where cars are most numerous,
the mysterious outbreaks have led to careful investi-
gation, and the bulk of the evidence suggests that
they are caused by electric sparks emanating, not
from the electrical outfit of the car, but from the
person who fills, or the appliance used in filling, the
petrol-tank. One widely-accepted theory is that the
chamois skin placed in the funnel in order to strain
the petrol becomes highly charged with electricity
owing to the friction of the pet-rol passing through it.
Recently, a number of scientists investigated the
matter, and the electric origin of the fires was pretty
clearly established; but the experts exonerated the
chamois leather except in so far as it shares the
electrical state of the person filling the tank, in
dry, frosty weather (when most of these fires occur)
it is very easy for a man to become highly electri-
fied. Skating'about on the floor of the garage, which
is usually of insulating material, or an active pro-
cess of dusting the car, especially with chamois skin,
or a silk, or feather-duster, may electrify the car,
which is usually insulated from the earth by its
rubber tyres. Under favorable atmospheric condi-
tions it has been proved that electric sparks of
sufficient power to fire petrol vapor may easily be
produced. This matter is not of great moment to the
owners of cars in the coastal districts of Australia,
but it deserves the serious attention of those who
use them in the Western country. In dry weather
the electrical activity observable in Western Queens
land startles new-comers. Most people sleep
summer, using a stretcher which is placed inside
the house in the day, and often, when an attemp
is made to move the bed into the open, the operator
receives a shock which surprises him. If he attempts
to strip the bed he will see at the removal of each
blanket or rug a display of sparks and hear a loud
crackling. During the recent dry spell in Queens-
land two motorists were making towards Charleville
when they were overtaken by a “Thargommdah
shower” or duststorm. One of the men got out o
the car and placed his hand on the hood As ne
did so he received a shock which almost knoeke
him down. On attempting to crank the car both
men received shocks, but managed to start tne
?ng"ne By this time they had become .cautious
Some thick'rubber sheeting was used as an insulation
from the ground while the travellers stepped aboard.
Bv the time they arrived at Charleville the condi-
tion of the car was # #

unknown, but who is supposed to have come to

POLITICAL
POINTS
FOR THE SUPREME EMERGENCY.
O FFICER: “ You’re always obtaining leave.
Deaths in the family appear very fre-
quent. Are there any of you left?”
PRIVATE: “Only a sister, and I’m keeping
HER for the Cup.”
Party politics—the survival of the slickest.
The plain fact is that conscription didn't
get a fair deal. It was represented as a
thing detestable in principle and only to be
put up with because of a grave emergency.
From the first day of the war The Bulletin
called for it. Ministers turned it down—
there was no need for it yet. Pearce, like
most of the rest of them, threw discredit
upon it, failing or wilfully refusing to see
the justice of it. The Censorship was most
improperly used to suppress arguments in
favor of it. And when the people were asked
to approve of it, it was put to them as an
undesirable thing which they were asked to
stomach for the duration of the war only.
If, from the outset, conscription—or univer-
sal service, if you like —had been represented
as the just thing and the only orderly thing,
it would not have encountered one-tenth of
the opposition.
Oh, yes, we took this Referendum vote
seriously enough. At Prahran (Vic.) a man
made anxious inquiries at the electoral office
a week before the 28th. He wanted to know
hew an elector who expected to be away
from home on the 28th could record his vote.
The conditions were explained to him.
“Leaving the State?” he was asked. “Oh.
no,” he said, “I’m not goin’ out of Victoria.”
“Going to a provincial town—Ballarat or
Bendigo?” “No. The fact of the matter is,
boss, I’m expectin’ to be in gaol on Satur-
day.”
When the conscriptionist considers the
result of the Referendum in some divisions
of N. S. Wales and S. Australia in parti-
cular, it may do him good to remember that
Pontius Pilate also appealed to the people
and they said “Give us Barabbas!”
On the majority judgment of the High
Court in the Bananaland stock case, upend-
ing the last hope that inter-State trade might
be free from the calamitous State inter-
ferences of the past:—
Justice Barton: The decision of the majority of
this Court will have a grievous effect upon the
future of the Commonwealth, as it will tend to
keep up the separation of its people upon State lines
by imputing to the Constitution a meaning which, I
venture to say, was never dreamed of by its framers,
and a meaning which will probably result in the
very dangers and dislocations which its provisions
were intended, and, in my judgment, were aptly
framed, to prevent. I end my judgment by express-
ing heavy sorrow that their Honors’ decision is as
it is.
Justice Isaacs: Though Australians hitherto thought
their Constitution had placed inter-State trade on a
basis broader than in Canada, and more inviolable
than in America, a belief to some extent, at all
events, shared by the High Court in prior cases, and
even by the Privy Council itself, it now appears
that not only was this a wide-spread error, but that
inter-State trade can exist only as far as the several
States will allow it. I cannot add the traditional
judicial regret at inability to concur in this
decision.
Both these Judges, who were leaders of the
Federal Convention and knew what the
framers of the Constitution intended, virtu-
ally say that this judgment makes the Fede-
ration a jest. Whatever their law may be,
their conclusion on that point is unclialleng-
able. The Constitution could not be kept
out of the melting pot now by a herd of
elephants.
The Gov. of Vic. and his Ex. of South Aus-
tralia and the Cocked Hat of Tasmania were
taken down the Murray the
other day to see for themselves
how Australia lets its water run
to waste. All three were attended
by genthelps, who stood back
respectfully. But when there
wasn’t a Governor immediately
in front they also could see how
the water was running to waste.
The captain of the steamer had
seen it running for years, and
the bloke who peeled the pota-
toes was sick of the sight of it.
The hovering reporters tele-
graphed back to their papers
that the potentates were “deeply
impressed.” The genthelps were
less deeply impressed—-not
through callousness, but simply
because it isn’t etiquette for a
genthelp to be as deeply im-
pressed as his Excellency. And
the water is still running to
waste, and the bloke on the river
steamer is still peeling potatoes
and keeping up his attitude of
splendid indifference. If his
surname isn’t Australia it ought
to be.
A silver lining to every cloud !
Even the chairman of Melbourne
Stock Exchange, addressing his
gloomy co-brokers on the ques-
tion of double taxation and the
need for national economy, finds
something to be thankful for —
that “there isn’t a third Govern-
ment to take what the other two
have left.”
Anyhow, the German has no right to
chortle over the result of our Referendum
until he has shown that he is game to have
a Referendum too. If there were no Neins
in his neighborhood he wouldn’t have to
chain his men to their machine-guns.
Just before he threw up his job ex-
Treasurer Higgs suggested that a “study” of
his last statements of accounts blew out the
charges of extravagance. Well, have a look
at these expenditure figures and see how
economy is being practised by one Govern-
ment in seven: —-

was £23,160,733; this year it is £32,585,791.
Apparently the reason why “peace” expendi-
ture was boomed is that war expenditure in
1913-14 was od., while the 1916-17 estimate
(indefinite) is £85,299,500, and that the in-
terest bill has increased from £99,910 to
£8,162.000. These figures show how absurd
it is to even mention the need of preventing-
waste.

One of the great advantages of buying
from the foreigner, whom you can’t send
your Board of v Health to inspect, is that you
often get more than you pay for. This, for
instance, is from the annual report of the
Local Government Board for Scotland, 1915 :
In August we were informed by the medical
officer of the English Local Government Board that
two cases of anthrax occurring in civilians had re-
cently been traced to infection from shaving-brushes.
At a later date one of these brushes was found to be
still infected with anthrax spores. The other brush
was derived from the same source. Some of the
material from which the brushes were manufactured
had also been examined and anthrax spores detected
in it. It was ascertained that the mixed hair (horse-
hair plus goat hair, etc.) used in the manufacture
of these brushes was derived from China.
The inherent tendency of
certain persons to do what
they know they shouldn’t is
the reason they have to pay
many bills they rather
wouldn’t. Thus Premier

There are also two “super-
visors,” who inspect these in-
spectors. That is all; but in
the interests of economy and
national efficiency someone
should certainly be appointed to oversee
those two supervisors.

Federal Food Prices Commissioner Acker-
man got some interesting evidence in Sydney
lately. A miller’s representative said that a
certain locally-made rolled oats was admit-
tedly superior to an imported American
brand, yet the price, of the superior Aus-
tralian article was fixed below what the
inferior foreign goods sold for in Queens-
land. That, the witness argued, was detri-
mental to the local product. And perhaps
he was right. The Stinking Fish party has
dinned into the ear of Australia that the
foreigner can always do the thing better;
so now, when a Board fixes the price of an
Australian article at less than a foreign
competitor sells his goods for, the unpatri-
otic Australian is liable to assume that it is
simply because the foreign article is better.
It is another hole that price-fixing falls
into. Price-fixing makes a hobby of falling
into holes.
The good example set by Australia in pro-
tecting from misuse the word Anzac is being
followed by Great Britain. A Bill was passed
into the House of Commons last week
which will prevent the managers of mushy
tea-shops, or any other business fraud, thing
or place trading on the name.

but I’ll he sorry when all the German
submarines are cleared off. They’re the
only things that ever Kept my family
from going Glome ’ every year.”
will be secured at Parliament House, and ex-

Recently, according to George Moir, a well-
known Melbourne lawyer, a firm in Western
America offered .to supply Australia with
100,000 ft. of hardwood grown from Austra-
lian seeds. (Laughter.) The joke is a good
stable-companion for that now elderly item
relating to South African wattle-bark. We
import it in large quantities for tanning; and
it, too, is taken from trees grown there from
Australian seeds. (Renewed mirth.) Still,
amusing though they may be, these tales
would be much funnier if they weren’t true.
Albert Mullett, Government printer at
Melbourne, a strong barracker for the local
manufacture of paper, doesn’t confine his
research enthusiasm to the one line. There’s
Australian clay, too, “the finest clay in the
world.” It seems quite likely that Mullett is
right about the quality of the clay; but the
outlook for the clay-baker has a mottled
appearance, nevertheless. Some time ago a
Melbourne citizen bought one million teacups
from Japan, in one order, and now he is
realising a mighty profit on the deal, after
paying all charges. So long as this sort of
thing can go on, with apparent satisfaction
to all parties, it looks as though the best
clay in the world is going to stay in the
bank—a fixed deposit drawing no interest.
Every little local squabble and dispute in
wartime intensifies the national danger and
should be penalised accordingly. A young
Bananalander who passed through Sydney
the other day on his way south to volunteer
said lie didn’t realise until he got into Bris-
bane how serious the war situation was, all
the Outback papers he had seen being far
more interested in local shearers’ strikes and
nursery rows of that sort.
King O'Malley lately told a deputation,
Avhich buttonholed him on the subject, that
he would consult the general-manager in
reference to the Commonwealth Bank
financing landholders in the Federal Capi-
tal area, and if that wasn’t enough lie
would mention the matter to the Cabinet-
Now, what does Kink mean? Is he trying
to intrude his Ministerial whiskers into the
bank’s business and use a political pull to
secure advances? If so, one of tw T o things
is going to happen. Either the King will
be flattened out or the bank will soon start
to rock- on its foundations.
A PLEA FOR SILENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tradition holds with an unholy grip.
Through weary phrases politicians waddle.
They lift the sluice and let the large floods rip
Of their incessant and insistent twaddle.
Never a new-cut word of force or strength
Lights the dull course of what they call debating.
The old thin thoughts to their elastic length
Are duly stretched while work is still kept waiting.
It does no good, this mouthing idle things—-
No sort of good is aimed at or pretended.
As vain as that slight song the blowfly sings
Is the long buzz of M’s.L.A. upended.
It never makes impression on a vote ;
No rhetoric can carry a conviction ;
Yet every statesman like a William goat
Bleats hoarsely on in praise or contradiction.
Speech had its place what time with lips of fire
Old Freedom’s champions set keen councils rock-
ing;
But now the Caucus simply pulls the wire,
For all the points of State are interlocking.
Let us have silence. If, like Turks and Kurds,
Our politicians must have room for fighting,
Set a cold limit of a hundred words
And make the user put them down in writing.
This would save pain and paper and much print,
And ease the jaws and elevate the nation.
Hansard would wane; but there would be no stint
Of time for silent, solid legislation.
N.s.w.

By The Bulletin man who watches
them in Sydney :
Not many horses are deemed to have a chance tor
the Melbourne Cup because Ike has .put Shepherd King',
the Caulfield .Cup winner, on such a mark that the
public is afraid to invest. This imported nag must
have a great say in the race, but there is another
from the same stable, Aides, also from the Cold
Country, that is touted as something out of the
ordinary. Charles Wheeler trains the pair, and has
form to guide him, for he turned out last year’s
victor, Patrobas. Aides led into the straight in
the Caulfield Cup, and was going so well that some
people took their boots off in a rush to get 6 to 4
about the stranger winning. Aides will be a better
horse on Tuesday. Maoriland has a couple in the
event with claims, and the writer has heard one
hard-head declare that Kilboy could fall 'down and
then win. Sasanof has been going great guns on the
track. The Sydney-owned Lingle, favorite for so
long time until lately, has done nothing to make
the people behind him feel sorry for themselves.
Lingle has gone through a great preparation, and
will start backed for a ton by his big-punter pro-
prietor. Shepherd King, Lingle and Aides or Kilboy
look like being well in the picture 100 yards from
the finish. Which is the best 'of the quartette this
writer will be able to explain after the dispute is
over. . '«

From this paper’s old-established Mel-
bourne prophet:— '
Only once in the past 13 years has the
winner of the Melbourne Cup carried more than Bst.,
although the runner-up in six instances has humped
anything from Bst. 51b. to lOstj This year there
appear to be only five possible winners in the under-
-Bst. lot, viz., Lingle, Kilboy, Stag-eland, Sasanof and
Eastcourt. The question between Kilboy, Stageland
and Eastcourt will not necessarily be settled by their
V.R.C. Derby running on Saturday, but the winner
of the classic race is pretty certain to start first
favorite for the Cup, on the strength of what
Patrobas did last year: Should Kilboy win easily a
lot of people will think the Cup all over bar the
shouting; nevertheless the writer will continue to
think that all the three-year-olds are a moderate lot
until their excellence is proved. If anything beats
Kilboy in the Derby it will possibly be Stageland, who
is said to be a good stayer, although his form in
public has given no hint of his alleged stamina.
The son of Comedy King won a nine-furlongs race at
Randwitk after being decisively beaten in the A..T.C.
Derby. But he may have been short of condition
in those days.
Last year the first two horses in the Caulfield Cup
were warm favorites for the longer race at Fleming-
ton, and neither of them had the least bit of a say
in the finish. All the same, it seems safe to count
on the probability of Shepherd King, Arnata and
Sasanof —the first three horses at Caulfield —being
prominent figures in the final stages of Melbourne
Cup struggle. Shepherd King is one of the finest
imported nags we have seen, and he looks good for
two miles. Nobody knows but what Amata may
be as fond of a distance as the other offspring of
Wallace that have won the Melbourne Cup. There is
no visible reason whv the insignificant-looking mare
who beat Sasanof in the fastest Caulfield Cup on
record shouldn’t do it again next Tuesday. There
is more reason for thinking that Shepherd King’s
penalty will not stop him from beating them both.
He put up a great performance at Caulfield. The
doubt about him this time turns on the question of
jocke.vship. Lewis may not ride him, and a less
powerful horseman may fail to get the nervous animal
into a good position ere the critical point is arrived
at.
To the writer’s way of thinking, a dozen horses
are Melbourne Cup possibles. Even St. Spaza has a
chance on his Adelaide Cup performance, and Cyklon
is a good one occasionally, whereas the handsome
Lingle has never yet been very good in public.
Prince Bardolph is a sticker that commands respect,
and Carlita’s record of rich victories gives her the
appearance of being worth backing with 9st. 51b. up.
But if Carlita is worth considering, Bursar must be
a second-to-none chance. He and his stable com-
panion Quinologist, both from England, proved them-
selves genuine stayers on the last day of the A..T.C.
meeting. Bursar had bedn backed for Melbourne
Cup before Quinologist was thought of as a real good
one. His own peonle had under-rated the winner of
the Met. because Bursar was reckoned so much better
over a distance. Judged bv his brilliant run past
Carlita in the Rand wick Plate l'ace. Bursar is at the
top of the first class. He looks a champion, and is
greatly fancied by many of the Sydney shrewd-heads.
The writer prefers Bursar to Sheoherd King, Amata
-nd the best of the three-vear-olds, whichever that
jay be. .

Of tile four imported horses that
Wheeler’s stable contributed to the Caulfield Cup the
winner and Aides were the handsomest, Green Gap
was the neatest, and Sunbury the least fit m condi-
tion as well as the youngest of the party. Green
Cap and Shepherd King are of the'same age, and
have the same sire—Martagnon. Aides is a year
older, and he sometimes looks as though he would
rather be at home with the ladies than go trying to
win races. * i # *
Shepherd King’s capture of the Caulfield
Cup was apparently a deep-laid scheme, with
“Bobby” Lewis at the root, of it. -The' horse was
imported by F. H. Lewis, who is generally supposed
to be more or less connected with brother Bobby in
these matters. Ite was sold to the owner of Patrobas,
for whom Lewis rides, with the proviso of a iui thei
payment if he won a Cup, which was practically a
proviso that Lewis would have the mount in the
Cups. Bobby took great pains before the race to
assure himself that Shepherd King could gallop faster
than any of the others. He went out of his way to
ride various candidates at exercise on the track.
Having left himself no room for doubt as to the gee s
superiority at the weights, he made no secret of his
belief that he would bring. off the coup. The fact
that he allowed the nervous animal to drop into last
place before bringing him along on the outside ot
the field was proof of Bobby’s confidence in the good
thin «-
Probably the wetness of the Spring had
something to do with the all-round success of English
horses at Randwick and Caulfield. Soft going comes
natural to them, whereas a lot of Australian nags
gallop best on an ironclad course. Besides, the limbs
of the imported ones are not very tough, as a rule,
and if they don’t actually break down in the course
of training when the ground is hard they are very
likely to go lame, more or less.
* * * *
The four-year-old Rael Locin made his
first appearance on any sort of racecourse at Leon-
gatha (Vic.) in February last, when he ran nowhere
in the local Cup. Later, he entered a training
stable, and faced the starting-barrier at Aspendale
and Moonee Yalley'-hr July. When he ran second to
High Rock at the -V.R.C. -October meeting he was
still so green that the roar of the crowd bewildered
him, and he turned his head to see where the noise
was coming from when he should have been sticking
to business. But for this Rael Locin would have
been the actual winfier on that occasion. A week
later the novice was sufficiently seasoned to win the
Toorak Handicap in a very resolute style, although
he was suffering from an abscess in the mouth.
This was lanced the same night, after which he
regained his normal appetite- for meals. •
*"* * *
The little Maoriland mare Bee had bad luck
to rrtiss the Cup at Moonee Valley (Vic.) last week. She
started favorite at 7 to .2, but on the journey the
saddle slipped and when she looked like a winner
at the distance she got jambed close into the rail.
This gave Andelosia the chance to collar the cash
with a few inches to spare from _ Lucky Escape and
the imported mare. As Andelosia was a 20 to 1
chance, MacShortodds felt that the war was nearly
over. This was Andelosia’s second win since the
autumn, but the stable was not at all sweet on his
chance owing to the poor showing he made at the
V.A.T.C. meeting. He cost 900 guineas as a yearling,
and at three years had a good innings, canturiiTg
among other events the Caulfield Guineas, Linlith-
gow Stakes, St. Kilda Cm) and the Club Handicap
at Rosehill. The weight-fixer for the Caulfield Cup
reckoned him a stone better horse than Shepherd
King. But the owners had a different opinion, and
pulled him out after the Coongy Handicap. The
time at Moonee Valley (2min. s|sec.) is a record for
the race.
* * * *
Zuland, the Maoriland sprinter, is a
profitable moke. Since he came across the Tasman
a few months back he has won six races, the - latest
being Moonee Valley (Melb.) Welter Handicap on
Saturday. The secret of Zuland’s success is the
astute manner in which he is placed. People behind
him are content with small prize-money events, and
have been fortunate in always getting good prices.
His figure on Saturday was 14 to 1 against. Zuland
is a nice-looking rag, and can carry a bit, so his
future position in the weight-list may not altogether
put an end to his usefulness.
* * * *
The Sydney sprinter Lord Malt had been
backed in several six-furlong dashes lately, but with-
out hitting the front at the right moment until
Saturday. Starting at 7 to 4in the Flying at Canter-
bury Park that afternoon his Lordship did what the
punters wanted. There was some ease, too. about the
effort. He didn’t have am thing of much class against
him; but it looked as if he might be able to do the
same thing on the smaller courses when asked.
* * * *
When an animal comes from the country
with a boom on it the public does not inquire into
the why and wherefore too closely. It simply takes
things for granted, and proceeds to separate Mo from
his cash. Thus word went round that Davy Buchanan
was the best of good things for the Park Stakes at
Canterbury (Sydney) on Saturday. Result—s to 4
David, 8 to 1 bar one. One of the unbarred ones,
Lutana, which a week earlier had appropriated a
maiden event at Warwick Farm, led to within a
yard of the post before Davy realised the importance
of his mission. Then he poked his head in front.
About a year ago Davy was produced in an event
at Randwick and backed for a hatful. He didn’t
materialise, and was taken back to the woods to
browse on the small stakes hung- up by the hayseed
clubs.

Trainer Frank McGrath’s Plenty threatens
to live up to his name. After delaying for inearly
a year Plenty has taken two Sydney suburban handi-

double. This was in ’B2 when he had Navigatoi in

Desert Gold, Maoriland’s crack gee-gee,
had her first race as a four-year-old in the Cham-
pion Plate (1-1-mile) at Wellington the other day
and showed the other nags that they were not going
to win many w.f.a. races this year. On the extreme
outside at the start she was two furlongs in front
when the rails were reached, and she got to the post
in front of an abandoned field in 2mm. 7sec. lhis
was the mare’s 15th consecutive win. Punters put
up a pound to win *3s. # #
“The Jonah”: Hard mozzle? Well, some
years ago 1 drew a horse in each of five sweeps on
the Caulfield Cup, and the whole five nags were
mixed up in the great smash of that year. The
jockey of one (Robin Hood) was killed. In the Mel-
bourne Cup the same year I had tickets in eight
sweeps. In each sweep there was a sport, and
that meant one blank. Now a what do you think.
I drew every blank. Luck, not satisfied about its
treatment of me, came at me again, there was a
10s. sweep on the Steeple and two blanks. When
one horse and a blank were left in the hat a mate
bet me a pound to-a shilling that I would not draw
the blank. I'didn’t, but I drew a prad that failed
to start. Can anyone beat me as a Jonah?
* i * * *
“Hydros”: How’s this for keeping the
honors in the family? The Grafton Cup, run-last
July, was won by Norbury, with Nojah and Noviro
in the places. The three are from the same mare.
Nirvana, and if that, as a record, can be touched, 1
want to surrender. As a boy the writer rode an old
mare to victory in a “bridle” race.. She was the
mother of the other starters. They ran second and
third. ’ „
* * * *
Art Magirl, ex-wrestler and gymnast,
handed over the slumber punch for the second time
to Dave Smith. He did it on Saturday at Mel-
bourne. In the sixth collision Smith intercepted a
glove with his chin, got up to see what was the
matter and shaped a right which ended the argument,
Dave’s showing was very moderate. He has been
getting more hidings than are good for him.
■*■ * * *
Ned Fitzgerald was presented with some-
thing heavy by Wave Geike in the second round of a
disturbance at Brisbane Stadium on Saturday. He
took three, helpings, and before he wanted another
Geike was half-way home. How these coming cham-
pions do fizzle out! # #
Les Darcy threatened last week to do two
things—go to Melbourne and biff George Chip on
Monday and then enlist. Well, he may do both all
right, but last Saturday there was any amount of
betting to be got that he wouldn’t be in Melbourne,
and up till Tuesday the people who knew his
previous address didn’t know where he had been
for several days. And amid the prevailing uncer-
tainty another opponent for Mr. Chip was being
eagerly searched for.
* * * *
If Darcy strikes U.S.A. before he strikes
the slide and can get away with Mike Gibbons, there
would be no question at all about the middleweight
championship. It would he his in the eyes of every
promoter and barracker in the world. A 1 McCoy’s claim
to the thing is a joke. If Jeff Smith is seriously in
the picture, a conflict between Darcy and him would
be worth about two cents to see. But.the great battle
to be pulled off by Darcy, if he ever gets a chance, is
with Jack Dillon, now at the top of the tree m
Yankeeland. That would be a _ rousing fight for
Americans, and a big scrap to win. Heaven knows
how long it may be before the disturbance happens!
This page hasn’t a book on the subject; but as Darcy
explained a few days ago that he had no intention of
going to America while the war lasted, and as Mr.
Dillon has not arrived in Australia, the future looks
as doubtful as the interior of a deep, dark well.
Meanwhile someone whispers into this paper’s ear
that the press agents are putting in some fine work.
* * * *
Yankee sports and imitations thereof are
wondering whether Willard will ever fight again. If
it is true, as alleged, that Willard is now a mass of
21st. 21b. of meat and has accumulated a good-sized
wad there is not much risk in wagering that the tor-
ture of shedding Jcwt. of tissue is not one of the
things he wants to endure. He is just on 30, too.

Fred Kay is the most awkward proposi-
tion in pugs a brother practitioner can be summoned
to subdue. Kay boxes the reverse end up, so to speak,
and does the wrong thing with great success when he
ought to be doing something else and getting into
trouble. To a man shaping in the natural way with
left foot and glove in the lead, the spectacle of Kay
with his right boot and hand in advance, crouched
down alongside an easily swinging left, is an appari-
tion weird enough to make the average pug burst into
tears; For he is clean out of reach, and a boxer who
cannot be touched and is mounted on a pair of freak
legs and can duck and squirm away from assault takes
a lot of mastering. Jimmy Clabby tried his fist at the
job on Saturday at Sydney Stadium, and the lanky
Westralian fooled and outboxed him all the way.
Everv time James registered a hit he scored three
and bis long-suit, of hanging on didn’t help
him get a point. Kay put up a very clever exhibition

Miss Buret, who the other day again
became champion of her side of the Manly (Sydney)
Foil’ Club is a diminutive but effective smiter of the
perverse pill She is the daughter of the club s secre-
tary is armed with a sunny smile and an easy swing,
and when Tn form can be relied on to play steadier
o'olf than most skirt-wearers. She has been ktate
champion,, and that takes some doing. Miss Dmet
has no great command of length, nor does she can>
anv sDecial supplv of fireworks in hei kit, but she
keens the line well, putts with more than common
accuracy and doesn’t lose her block, so to speak.
This time at Manly she had to face a final that made
her roil up her sleeves. Mrs. Alan Moore was th ■
other one, and she had her ironmongery in front foi
a good part of the journey. It was the toughest final
the champion has had, and she hove a sigh of relief
when the final putt went down. During recent yea
Mrs. Moore has taken small part m competition go .
but the little Moores are bigger now, and she will
probably be heard of* #
At Katoomba (N.S.W.) the other day a
dozen ladv rifle-shots challenged the pick of the local
club—one of the finest on the Blue Mountains—on the
miniature range, and the men got out of it witn 434
points to 433. Throughout N.S.W. country districts
rifle-shooting by people who are not men is flistnow
a' popular vice. It’s better than tea-guzzlmg, too.
The Sydney match between Gray and Fred
Lindrum ended: Gray, 16,000 ; Lindrum, 15,110. At
half the distance the pegging was about level, but an
the way through it looked as if Gray was well
within striking distance. In the first week 200 was
Grav’s best effort, and Lindrum did 202. There was
nothing to rave about in that. In the second week
the losing-hazard artist came to light with a lattle
of 480 and an accumulation of 360 off the red. Then
he helped himself to £2OO and the game.
* * *
An old-timer who keeps his identity under
cover is George Fearn, of Gladesville (N.S.W.). Away
back in 1879, Fearn, Captain Webb (who crossed the
English Channel and was afterwards Hleo, at
Niagara Falls) and a couple of others started in a
six-day swimming event (14 hours per day) at Lam-
beth (Eng.) baths. Webb won. Webb and learn
met in 1881 in a six-days’ match (12 hours per day).
Webb again won. What pluggers they were! Alto-
gether, Fearn captured about 00 trophies, great
massive silver medals in particular ; but an enterpris-
ing Bill Sikes of the period got the majority alter
they .had been shifted to Australia. In those bygone
days George started (with son Harold and Peter Sulli-
van) to build racing boats, and the shells they turned
off easily held, a monopoly in- the sculling world.
Rheumatism now reminds the old-timer of the swims
of long ago. * * »
Sydney summer baseball started off with
the customary baiting of umpires in all its awful-
ness. This time it was the players who ran not ; and
unless the practice gets the axe supporters can hardli
be condemned for their Dervish-like howl. But what
sort of game is this that stirs everybody on the
premises to rage and fury ? * #

FLEMINGTON, ON TUESDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They reach the straight. The noise and cheering dies.
Kilboy and Shepherd King cling close together.
Now Sasanof his splendid metal tries ;
There’s not the width between them of a feather.
The fancied bay has done his final dash.
The Maorilander hustles like a winner.
Lord ! Here’s the Sydney horse that bears my cash—
It’s Lingle creeping up, as I’m a sinner!
Kilboy in front! No ! Sasanof takes charge.
Out of that bunch it’s hard a horse to single.
They’re neck and neck. The shout grows hoarse and
large.
The push is yelling “Lingle, Lingle, Lingle!”

“WITH HEALTH
Everything is a Source of Pleasures
; —without it, nothing - else, whatever it may be, is enjoyalale.
Even the other personal blessings—a great mind, a happy
temperament—are degraded and dwarfed for want of it.
Everything should be made secondary to health.”
There’s no reason why you should not be healthy—Nature
intended you to be so.
The various organs of your body have certain functions to
perform. It is your duty to see that these organs are not
overtaxed or run down, which will prevent them from pro-
perly fulfilling their respective functions. For instance,
constipation, indigestion and other disorders of the system
are the direct result of your internal muscles being much
below normal —failing in their duty.
My exercises pay. particular attention to the muscles that
, govern the action of the stomach, liver and bowels, and
quickly bring them back to a healthy state again.
Surely you want to enjoy the best of health ? Then why
not write to-day for full particulars of my
POSTAL COURSE OF HEALTH AND STRENGTH
CULTURE ?

TO POLLY KERRISON' and ANNIE
KERRISON (or Annie Newton), who
left Christchurch, New Zealand, upwards of
20 years ago,’both children of the late Wil-
liam Kerrison and Anna Maria Iverrison,
and to all persons claiming by, through or
under them or. either of them, including their
children.
PURSUANT to the directions of the Hon-
orable Sir Robert Stout, K.C.M.G., Chief
Justice of New Zealand, under section 25 of
The Public Trust Office Amendment Act
1913, you are hereby called upon to send in
to the undersigned-; on or before the first day
of February, 1917, your claim to part of the
estates of ARTHUR GEORGE KERRI-
SON, late of Christchurch, laborer, who died
on the 20 th June. 1914, intestate, and
ALBEIRT KERRISON, late of Islington,
Christchurch, laborer, who died on the 30th
October, 1914, intestate, failing which such
estates will be distributed among the per-
sons entitled thereto, disregarding your
claims.
Dated at Wellington, this 13th day of
July, 1916.
J. W. MACDONALD. Solicitor to the
Public Trust Office, Wellington, New Zealand.

How I Darkened My
Grey Hair.
Lady Gives Simple Home Recipe That
She Used to Darken Her Grey Hair.

to its natural color with the prepared dyes
and stains, but none of them gave satisfac-
tion and they were all expensive. I finally
came across a simple recipe which I mixed
at home that gives wonderful results. I
gave the recipe, which is as follows, to a
number of ihy friends, and they are all
delighted with it. To 7oz. of water add a
small box of Orlex Compound and loz. of
bay rum. These ingredients can be bought
at any chemist’s at very little cost. Use
every other day until the hair becomes the
required shade. It will not only darken
the grey hair but removes dandruff and acts
as a tonic to the hair. It is not sticky or

REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF
TheNation&l Bank of Australasia
LIMITED.
The Balance-sheet of-the Bank as on 30th September last, and the Profit and Loss Account for the
half-year which then terminated, are now submitted to the shareholders.
The net profit, after deduction of all expenses, including £12,563 3s. lOd. for Note, Income and

The increased amount of expenses for the half-year, as shown by the Profit and Loss Account com-
pared with previous statements, has been caused almost wholly by increased pay to the staff and other
employees during- the term.
During the half-year the branches at Broome Hill and Quirading and the agency at Kukerin, in
Western Australia, were closed, and the Wongan Hills branch, in the same State, converted into an
agency, and an agency was opened at West Leederville, also in Western Australia.
The agency at Whorouly, in Victoria, was closed.
The total number of the Bank's staff who have enlisted with the Australian Expeditionary Forces is
now 289, oi' whom 60 have joined since our last report.
In all, 15 of the Bank’s officers have been killed in action or died of wounds, including five during
the past half-year, viz.: —
Lieutenant O. V. Richardson, of Adelaide Office.
Sergeant E. M. Peart, of Horsham Branch, Vic.
Sergeant G. D. A. Gallagher, of Mildura Branch, Vic.
Corporal B. Humphris, of Moonta Branch, S.A.
Lance-Corporal J. B. Brooks, of Strathalbyn Branch, S.A.
Donations to the various War Relief Funds during the half-year amounted to £2lOO.
The Ordinary General Meeting will be held at the head office*of the Bank, Collins Street, Melbourne,
on Wednesday, Ist November, at uoon.
By order of the Board of Directors,
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
E. H. WREFORD, Chief Manager.

The customary Auditors’ Report and the Statement of the Directors and Chief Manager to comply with
the Companies’ Acts (Victoria) appear on the Official Report.
E. H. WRF.FORD, Chief Manager.
That bright young man, Kenneth Cross,
A.A.M.C., has been rewarded with a job in
London as assistant-director of medical ser-
vices. His majority came in tiie same prize-
packet. Major Cross is a son of "Ada Cam-
bridge,” which is to say Mrs. George Cross,
sometime yicaress of Holy Trinity, Williams-
town, who used to write novels in the vicar-
age breakfast-room while the Rev. George
did his sermons in the library and little Ken-
neth chased beetles in the back garden. The
new assistant D.M.S. married Trixie Wal-
pole, a daughter of that Robert Walpole who
carved his name on public notice as secre-
tary and chief platformist of the Employers’
Federation for a number of years.
There is a fascinating French officer
about Melbourne just now on leave. I don’t
know his name, but his beautiful clothes
call for notice. The khaki blouse has bell
sleeves and is fastened at the left shoulder
with three buttons, while a belt bunches in
the blouse-tunic to make a frilly ballet skirt.
The Forrest dame, who has no daughter
of her own, is very devoted to niece Muriel
North, and gives her a lovely time, always
bringing her on her trips to Melbourne. Auntie
Forrest says that her niece narrowly escaped
being Lady Muriel, as her father missed the
chance of being heir to the Earldom of Guil-
ford by a fluke. The Earl of Guilford, was in-
considerate enough to marry a second wife
late in life, and a son was born to inherit
the title and estates. Muriel has a brother,
Charlie North, who 4eft Westralia early in
the war, and lie is now flying for his coun-
try. He has a pretty talent for song com-
position and owns a powerful baritone voice.
Bob Jeffries (son of the alderman), who
is in France, took a baby native bear as a.
mascot. The little Australian died of the
cold before he reached England, but was
alive and hearty when his crowd were going
through France. The Grand Republic loved
him, for he was the first of his kind to arrive
with an army. Bob has sent back a portrait
of the late lamented and a sorrowiul
account of his 12 hours’ illness. His last
words were, “Australia, sun and gumleaves!”
It is no use protesting—we’ve got to
wear our hair on top! The fashionable
high-boned collars demand it. The very
newest idea in hairdressing is the coil flat
on the top in the manner that was called
“Russian” some 15 years ago. Also, we are
assured that “more hair is to be worn,”
which means that the hairdressers are busy
compiling switches, pads, transformations
and other frauds.
The Girls who Stayed at Home have been
resting for a few weeks and gloating over
the £2OOO made by their amateur song-and-
dance. They begin again at The Playhouse
on November 24.
There are to be no frisky young things in
the female police force of Victoria—not if
the National Council of Women has its way.
Recently Chief Secretary McLeod asked the
N.C.W. to nominate a couple of Jane Bob-
bies, but the Council rejected the suggestion,
while volunteering the opinion that Jane’s
age should not be less than 40. Probably the
Chief Sec. will be obliged, in the end, to put
an advt. in the papers, calling for not-tco-
tenders for the job.
Dancing for grown-ups being considered
over the odds, one or two matrons are airing
their best ballrooms with flapper frivols —
not showy, expensive affairs, you know, but
just quiet gatherings for schoolboys and
girleens. Mrs. Donald Mackinnon hostessed
one on a recent Saturday, just a week after
the Toorak Parburys had obliged.
Irene, one of the lengthy Kiddle sister-
hood, has gone to England to see if she can
give a hand with Red Cross work. She will
drop in at Ceylon to take tea with the
Algers, who grow it. Mrs. Alger (once
Lassie Forbes) has just fled back to her
husband and the dear, faithful coolies, after
a few weeks’ holiday in Melbourne.
The wife of manager McCrae, of Dalgety’s,
has returned to N. S. Wales after tripping
to Melbourne-to see her mother, Mrs. Dill,
who had injured her hip. These accidents
are no joke when one is 90 years of age.
Another of the old pioneer breed to survive
a bad mishap is the Turnbull widow, who
was nearly asphyxiated by fumes from a
bath-heater. Her daughter and son-in-law
Bob Simson have crossed from their N. S.
Wales squattage to cheer the invalid on to
convalescence.
Lieut. Hugh Anthony, younger son of one
of our local timber men, whilst helping to
shell Huns, was wounded and taken prisoner.
After days of worry, a cable from the Rhine
side fluttered across to his anxious parents
saying young Hugh was on the mend and
quite happy. Of course. If he was dis-
contented or hungry a cable saying so
wouldn’t be allowed to travel far.
Promoters of the Patriotic dinner which
had to be turned into a hospital charity spree
are demanding to know the reason why the
War Council, after refusing a permit for
their proposed patriotic gorge, countenanced
the one at the Oriental last Friday, organ-
ised by Mrs. Harry Emmerton on the Red
Cross ticket. The War Council allows only
a certain small percentage (5 p.c. at most)
to be sunk in expenses. And you can't ruin
a proper gorge on the lines of a shilling’s-
worth of banquet to every 19s. worth of
patriotism, even though wines do go in as an
extra.
Hats are worn on the nose. The wide,
semi-transparent brim sends a flicker of
light and shade on the buried eyes of the
face beneath. And that’s all you see of the
spring-time maiden’s glances.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

IN
BANANA LAND
T. W. McCawley, Bananaland’s youthful
Crown Solicitor, has had a very successful
run lately. His recent trip to England saw
the State on top in the steamship Eastern
case; and the High Court verdict in the
just-decided stock action was another win.
These two wins alone saved the State any-
thing up to £85,000 in damages, not to men-
tion costs. When not in court McCawley
fills his leisure moments as Under-Secre-
tary, for Justice, Public Curator, Registrar
of Titles and Commissioner for Stamps.
Over 40 years ago the labor schooner Lady
Belmore, with 130 blackbirds aboard, met
a cyclone outside Alackay and went aground
on East Point beach, just opposite the town.
No lives were lost, and as it was impossible
to refloat the ship she was dismantled. The
Harbor Board opened a quarry in the
vicinity some time ago and the old wreck
attracted the attention of a newly-arrived
ten-bob-a-day quarryman. He spent one
recent Sunday digging the soft sand from the
wreck and then dynamiting the Aluntz-metal
spikes and keel plates. llis day’s work
netted him £B4.
Up at Townsville they are not so pre-
occupied with the horrors of war that they
can’t spare a tear for local atrocities. -For
instance, an alderman made an epic speech
the other day about the terrible state of
tJie roads and the agonies of simply going*
home:—■ &
Recently a male resident returning home on his
bicycle lost a whole set of teeth through the jolting,
whilst m the same vicinity a man driving- along had
the wheel of his buggy turned inside out. People
had frequently to take off their boots and stockings
to reach then- homes, and even had to proceed to
gii eater lengths when the tides were high. One most
piteous instance was that of a lady who was wheel-
ing a baby in a perambulator, when she struck a
hob user the perambulator, spilt the babv, and in
hr- struggles to save herself and the infant broke her
gaiters and both stockings came down.
What does bleeding Belgium amount to now?
Ryan had the last laugh in his argument
with Hughes about copper. The little man,
who is obsessed with the painful idpa that
he has cleaned up the metal problem and
that nobody else has done a thing, was
grieved when Ryan announced that he had
made a contract with the British Alunitions
Department, the latter agreeing to take the
State’s metal at £lOO per ton for two years.
Hughes couldn’t understand how Air. Ryan
could say such a thing—why, he (Hughes)
had had the matter in hand long before Ryan
ever went to England, and the business had
only just this moment been completed. Then
Ryan said he couldn’t understand how
Hughes came to fail in like that, because the
copper Hughes had sold wasn’t the metal
he (Ryan) had sold at all. The facts seem
* to be that, hoping to get some of the old
copper mines started, Ryan persuaded the
Alunitions Department to take up to 48,009
tons in the next two years at £lOO per ton
c.i.f. London, whereas Hughes’s sale was of
the output of copper from the big producing
mines (Alorgan, Lyell, Wallaroo, etc.),
i .tailing, it is expected, about 50,000 tons,
at £l2O per ton. On the present figures
Hughes seems to have made the better bar-
gain. But there is this to be said, of course
Hughes had something to sell; Ryan
merely wanted to get a firm offer which
might induce abandoned mines to get busy.
A FAIR OFFER.
RECRUITING SERGEANT: “Hello, Jacky, why don’t you enlist?”
JACK : “What, me enlist—what about Misser Jones, mine boss, enlistin g?”
R.S.: “Oh, Mr. Jones can’t enlist — he’s got too much land and cuttle to lcok after.”
JACKY: “Well, if Misser Government give me a thousand acres, I go and fight for him
every day the week.”
“Thirsty I”: In view of the proposed issue
of ss. notes, the whole question of design
should be reconsidered. The half-sovereign
note is responsible for many cases of fraud
because its shape and color are so much like
those of the pound note. The addition of
the words “half sovereign” is not such a
safeguard as the Treasury officials imagine.
With a little manipulation, which the writer
is not going to advertise here, the words
lend themselves to the working of a beauti-
ful sivindle. Up here in Torres Strait,
where there are crowds of simple Australian
aborigines, unscrupulous Asiatics and others
practise it 24 hours a day. I know of a
banker in these parts who refuses to circu-
late the ten-bob mistakes because he con-
siders that he would be aiding and abetting
fraud by handing them out. The notes
ought to correspond somewhat in size
with the values they represent, with the said
values clearly marked in commercial signs
without embellishment. Let us have some-
thing like the old Bananaland issues.
Kiosk in Wonderland.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I built a gay kiosk in Wonderland
And garnisht it. with many a painted dream;
1 built it ou a shining golden strand
With gentle outlook on a silver stream;
And daily did I gather comrades there
With dancing eyes of youth and gleaming hair,
And daily did I hearken to their songs
And fresh my heart at little fountains fair.
Within that gay kiosk of Wonderland
We kept Aladdin and his magic ring,
And one grave Pasha, bearded, tall and bland,
AVho came from Khiva’s Khan or Herat’s King.
And one day when we played a lovely play
There came a knock; and when we said “Come
in!”
The door was opened by a Bottle bold
And from the Bottle slowly rose a Djinn.
That gay kiosk of mine in Wonderland
Held all the Fairies like a book of gold;
Titania decked her Ass before the band
And Puck performed the tricks he knew of old ;
Brown dwarfs attended when you clapped your hands,
And weirdest songs were chanted by a shee,
And blue-eyed pages served you all with wines,
And smiled upon you from their bended knee.
Of course that gay kiosk in AVonderland
Had gruesome Caves a-winding underground
Where lurked at times old Ali’s robber band
AVho’d cut your head off if they heard a sound.
(You see a gay kiosk would not be gay
Unless it kept somewhere a kind of thrill
And thrills are only found in caverns deep,
Where someone lurks who knows the way to kill.)
Also, that gay kiosk in Wonderland
Had latticed easements opening on the sea;
At one a Princess sweet with lily hand
Sat weeping tears as salt as salt could be.
(AYe’d found her in an ancient fairy tale
And brought her in to soothe her bitter woe:
It seemed so kind to help a dear Princess
Whose griefs occurred a thousand years ago.)
To-day I’ve got a palace on a hill,
I bought it all with hardly-gathered gold;
Its gardens shine by many a rippling rill,
And lovely flowers its recent walls enfold;
And yet....l’d gladly change it any day
For one I built upon a golden strand
When Youth was lord of every laughing hour
And that kiosk was mine in Wonderland.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 28, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindaii, —
Her Ex. was the instrument that opened
the Arts and Crafts sale of work the other
day in aid of the Red Cross Society. As a
business agent she wins, because the stalls
were cleared almost as soon as she approved
of them. On the tables were some very
beautiful samples of work, particularly in
pottery, glazed and unglazed, all the products
of the Central Technical College. The founda-
tion clay was local—a lesson for politicians
and other anti-Australians. Purchasers
almost came to blows over the bargains. The
potters included Airs. Devereux, Alisses
Payne and Vera Affleck.
And we’ve found a genius. Her name is
Agatha Fitzgerald, and book-illustrating is
her gift. Australia’s beautiful fairy-tales
will owe a lot to her. You just wait and see.
Jean AleWhirter. daughter of James
AlcWhirter, the well-known Brisbane mer-
chant, has accepted a finger-band from Capt.
Charles Drew, of the British Army Medical
Corps. The knot-tying happened at Logie,
near Stirling.
The Queen Carnival resulted in an easy
win for the soldiers’ nominee, Mrs. Lee. As
a result of the competition about £3OOO was
diverted to the funds of the R.S. Residential
Club. Airs. Blume, representing the sports
bodies, was second in the race. Her hus-
band handed over 100 guineas as a gift
towards furnishing the Club.
Woman has invaded another of the sacred
precincts of the male worker in the back
country. At Carrar and Ballindallocli shear-
ing-sheds women are cooking in the shearers’
huts in place of the male “poisoner,” who has
enlisted. Ballindallocli had a procession of
three of them in one week, so the new regime
promises little improvement on that of the
male dough-puneher.
Claude, the straight-eyed son of chief
traffic-manager Lloyd, of Brisbane, who left
Australia with the rank of corporal, has
gained the D.C.M. and a commission. Ac-
cording to the young man’s own letter there
is but one brief explanation of the distinction
sheer luck. ’ But don’t believe it —wait
the official details.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“B.B.”: Bowen lias just scored, another
triumph over its hated rival Townsville. A
vessel, which the Censor says does not exist,
before leaving for Zep-land, recently took in
a supply of 160,000 gallons of Don River
water, the scarce Townsville commodity being
passed as of too-poor quality.
On the disadvantages of being a sheep:—
The lot of the Queensland jumbuck is rot all that
it’s cracked up to be. Flocks had just got through a
couple of years of drought to find that in many
places they were up against the dingo, and now every-
where the blowfly is their special curse. When the
monsoonal muggy weather arrives the scourge pro-
mises to be worse th<in on &ny previous occasion.
The fly is of the bottle-green variety. It “strikes”
sheep on any damp portion of the wool, and the
maggot hatches in a few days and proceeds to eat its
way in channels up over the sheep’s back underneath
the wool. In a fortnight to three weeks these mag-
gots drop off the unfortunate animal, if it is still
alive, in thousands, burrow into the first bit of soft
soil, and in a week or two again arrive on the scene
a rejuvenated, full-blown pest. Immediately a jum-
buck is struck it starts to lose condition, and young
ewes and weaners seem to give up all interest in
life. They lie down and die very quickly. In big
flocks where the pest is bad 15 to 20 per cent, is a
common loss. Some benefit is derived by “crutch-
ing’’—shearing the wool from between the hind-
quarters and over the tail —but it is doubtful if die
benefit is commensurate with the cost.
A tale of providence and thrjft:—
They were very careful at the Old Pioneer, and
with excellent reason, for at that date the Pioneer
was getting as much as 3oz. to the truck, all coarse
stuff, and the mine was manned by a crowd of old
Geordie and Cousin Jack diggers who knew every
trick in the gold-stealing game. Of Tregaskis
Brother Spence declared: “Aye, he’s a dagger!
Bob Tregaskis would lift an ounce nugget under the
eye of the Recording Angel and leave with two
good marks.” This meant much, as Tregaskis was
a K.G. (K for cousin, G for Jack) and Spence
was a Geordie, and Geordies are notoriously con-
temptuous towards all Cornishmen. Besides, Spence
was himself an expert, with a reputation for lifting-
gold in quaint ways. When the puddlers were being-
cleaned up, the legal manager, the mining- manager
and two athletic directors of the Old Pioneer care-
fully overlooked the proceedings, each with his right
hand ostentatiously plunged in his coat pocket, where,
as the men knew perfectly well, four forefingers toyed
with the triggers of as many natty little revolvers.
You wouldn’t wonder at it: the sight of the false
bottoms of an Old Pioneer sluice-box was one to
-tempt an archangel. The management kept a
special watchman on the job too, and in addition
an expert searcher, and it was confidently believed
in the office that the diggers’ natural reluctance to
part with nuggets had at length been overcome. Then
Ernie Tregaskis went to the manager one day in the
most casual way in the world, and jerking his thumb
towards the mass of old grease and black waste by
the engine-house door said: “I s’pose a man might
take that along home you. I’d be glad of en. That’s
fine manure you, and it’s hard keepin’ a bit gardin
goin’ these days.” The manager considered the
matter. The pile of oily filth was becoming a
nuisance. “Take it,” he said. It was Ernie’s long-
shift off, and in the course of the Saturday afternoon
he arrived with a dray. There were two loads, and
Tregaskis made so neat a job of the removal that
the surface boss congratulated him on the way he’d
“cleaned her up you.” Ernie made a show of
manuring his garden with a heap of dirty waste, but
the stuff he got from the Old Pioneer went into bags,
and was carefully buried in Ernie’s stable. Not till
two years later did the Cousin Jack venture to burn
this material. It cleaned up just about what he
thought it would, 3500 z., the reward of long fru-
gality. For 19 months on coming up from below
Ernie had swirled his crib billy and absently thrown
the remaining- tea and leaves on that heap of waste.
He called this “puttin’ by a bit for a man’s old age
you.”

“Nick Bottom” : These frank medical ads.
give me courage to write this story. A cane-
cutter friend of mine used to delight the
camp with a pathetic ballad entitled “Eng-
land’s Great Increase in Crime,” in which,
according to the singer, the London police
swelled the crime statistics by “preventing
a poor costive monger from earning his
bread on the street.”
“The Old Soldier”
At the back of the King of Greece’s mind is a
profound admiration for the crowned murderer of
Berlin, who has for more than 30 years been the
model upon which all the minor royalties of
Europe have tried to form themselves. While the
French Government seems to have determined that
’Tino must restrain this admiration and act on the
level as a neutral, the spineless Asquith and the
British romanticists, gushing of Marathon and
Thermopylae, plead for “consideration” for the
descendants of the heroes of the Persian War, who
are supposed to have saved Europe from Asiatic
domination in the sth century b.c. Whether what
happened nearly 2500 years ago ought to make any
difference to a modern European Government in
its agony is a question that ought not to he left to
academics. In any case, it is quite certain that
King ’Tino is not a Greek, and it is very doubtful
whether many of his people are descended from
those who fought at Marathon.
More economy. Presumably in order to
let the youngster know the size of the debt
that is being unloaded on him, O’Malley has
ordered a copy of Knibbs’s three-and-six-
penny Year Book' to be sent to every one
of the 8000 public schools in Australia.
“S.L.J.” : A word with “Good Australian”
who recently wrote to suggest that the staffs
at the base hospitals in Egypt were suffering
from cold feet. I have no hesitation in say-
ing that 90 per cent, of the staff of the 3rd
A.G.H., for instance, would transfer to a
combatant unit, if possible, at an hour’s
notice. This view is supported by the num-
ber of applications for transfers received by
the 0.C., and refused by the A.D.M.S.,
owing to the dearth of experienced hospital
men. Again, I deny that the hospital
orderly’s lot is a rosy 'one. I admit that
there is not the same danger as in the firing-
line, but there are other dangers when up
against enteric, cholera, meningitis, diph-
theria and similar malignant maladies. The
toil is from morning till night, very often
for the benefit of a lot of malingering-
patients wdiose chief question is.- “When is
the next boat for Australia, mate?” or “Do
you know if they are going to ‘board’ me for
Australia?” I have known patients literally
snatched from the grave to emphatically
declare that hospital men are “cold footers,”
who ought to be in the firing-line, little
thinking how they would have fared without
the attention of the despised orderlies. We
realise that the nursing sisters are mainly
responsible for the recovery of most patients,
but every sister needs assistance. Further,
75 per cent, of those on hospital staffs have
absolutely no choice as to the unit in which
they are to serve. A large number of men are
sent away from Australia as reinforcements
to Field Ambulance units, and on arriving in
Egypt are placed in A.M.C. Details and
drafted wherever they are needed. After
lf» months’ service in the A.M.C. the
conclusion I have come to is this: that it
would be desirable to employ more “B” class
men in the hospitals. Hundreds of men are
being sent back to Australia, suffering from
minor chronic trouble or physical defect,
who could be employed in the hospitals; but
such a matter is entirely out of the control of
the hospital staffs. I trust the Commonwealth
Government will take the subject in hand,
and alter the present state of affairs so as
to permit men who are practically perfect
physically to realise their desire to be of
greater service.
Adelaide shopkeepers, on being consulted
by the S.A. Chamber of Manufactures, have
decided not to have the usual “Manufac-
Week.” They might at least stick
up a sign in their windows: “This week we
ought to be showing what Australia can do.
But we’re really too busy this year to be
bothered with Australia.” Too busy, of
course, selling foreign wares—and attending
patriotic meetings.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”:—
A recent fire in Sydney did much damage to an
ancient Baptist Church which some years ago was
turned to commercial uses. This little Bethel is in
Castlereagh-street, a couple of doors south of Liver-
pool-street. Nearly 50 years back the pastor was
Daniel Allen, who used to cause commotions and
riots in Hyde Park on Sunday afternoons. In those
days Hyde Park —then still known amongst old citi-
zens as The Racecourse —was the debating-ground of
many sects. A prominent discuseionist was John
Rushton, a second-hand bookseller of York-street. He
had a large following, and Pastor Allen used to
chip in with abuse of the R.C.’s. These “pleasant
Sundays” were kept up until the temper of the R.C.’s
gave out, and open riots resulted. At this time
Alfred Stephen was chairman of the Hyde Park
trustees, and lived in Dynevor Terrace, College-.sti;eet.
I rom his drawing-room he could see the “fun,” and
decided at last to put an end to it, which he did
by getting the trustees to pass a by-law prohibiting
more than three persons “assembling together” in
the park. The discussions were then shifted to the
Domain, where they have ever since been carried on ;
and peace reigned on “the Racecourse.”
THE UNMARRIED HUSBAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The edict came that single men
Must join the ranks. Bill Jenkins raced
And begged Rose Green to be his bride;
When gentle Rose consent had sighed,
The wedding day arrived in haste.
Repentance, though, was quick to come,
And Jenkins waxed exceeding sore;
Although he’s paid the parson, Bill
In military eyes was still
An eligible bachelor.
He could not an exemption gain ;
Bill reckons it “a blanky cow” ;
But one faint hope he nurses yet:
“I’ll have to see if I can get
Exempted from my missus now!”
VICTORIA.

HEARTBURN-Most people know what this means
as few are exempt from frequent “ spells.”
The appetite becomes impaired, while
a severe gnawing or burning sensation
is experienced in the upper part of the
abdomen.
Heartburn is one of the many ways in
which the digestive organs express
disapproval of ill-treatment —warn you
to be careful.
Chamberlain’s Tablets for the stomach
and liver give instant relief in all cases
of heartburn, and by toning and
strengthening the stomach and bowels,
prevent a return of the trouble.
Chamberlain's Tablets are an excep-
tional remedy for all troubles arising

UNIQUE PROCESS FOR
RETAINING AND
REGAINING BEAUTY.
By. Mlle. Charlotte Sirier.
Does your skin chap or roughen easily, or become
unduly red or blotchy ? Let me tell you a quick and
easy way to overcome the trouble and keep your com-
plexion beautifully white, smooth and soft. Just get
some ordinary mercolised wax at the chemist’s, and
use a little before retiring as you would use cold
cream. The wax, through some peculiar action, flecks
off the rough, discolored or blemished skin. The
worn-out cuticle comes off just like dandruff on a
diseased scalp, only in almost invisible particles. Mer-
colised wax simply hastens Nature’s work, which is
the rational and proper way to attain a perfect com-
plexion, so much sought after but very seldom seen.
The process is perfectly simple and quite harmless.
** * *
It is astounding the number of women who suffer
from unsightly growths of hair on the face, and it
will come as a piece of good news to know that
there is a simple substance known as powdereil
pheminol which will remove it immediately and per-
manently. Mix a small quantity into a thin paste
with a little water, and apply to the objectionable
growths. In two minutes all trace of the hair will
have entirely vanished, and your skin will be as soft
and smooth as a child’s.
The hair should be allowed to breathe, and the
greasy film around each strand must be removed with
a mild non-alkaline shampoo. Soaps should be
tabooed. The very best solution for the purpose can
be made by dissolving a teaspoonful of stallax
granules in a cup of hot water. It stimulates the
scalp to healthy action, and at the same time leaves
the hair in that soft, fluffy condition so much
admired. Any chemist can supply you with an
original packet, of stallax, sufficient to make 25 or
30 shampoos.
One need, not resort to the very questionable ex-
pedient of hair-dye in order not to have grey hair.
The grey hair can easily be changed back to a
natural color in a few days’ time merely by the
application of a simple, old-fashioned and perfectly
harmless home-made lotion. Procure from jour
chemist two ounces of tammalite concentrate, and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply this to
the hair a few times with a small sponge and you
will soon have the pleasure of seeing your grey hair
gradually darkening to the desired shade. The lotion
Is pleasant, not sticky or greasy, and does not injure
the hair in any way. [2
The Inky Way.
At the recent N. S. Wales country press-
men’s conference the delegates compared
their contributions to the war. The Coota-
mundra Pinkstone family had a good record,
four of the sons being at the Front; but
J, N. Crowley, of Wyalong, held about the
best. He has three sons in the field and is
going into camp himself; add he has such
a crowd of relations in khaki that he has
lost count of them. All he knows is that
one brother and five nephews have been
killed.
“Coot” : I pin the long-service scissors on
It. T. Easterby, of Sale (Vic.). He joined
the Beechworth Constitution in ’5B, and
since then has been connected with papers
at Echuca, Bendigo, Horsham and Gipps-
land, and for a brief spell with one of the
Melbourne dailies. But city journalism was
not for him. For 34 years now he has
been editor of Gippsland Mercury, and the
chair isn’t w r orn out yet.
“Copy Boy”: Captain Frank Hudson,
author of much good Bulletin prose and
verse, was in Melbourne the other day, on
the w r ay from France to Maoriland, with a
badly damaged left wrist. As a youngster
Hudson was a lootenant in India, but left
the army to ramble the world, having a
dash at journalism here and in Maoriland,
where he was on the Otago Wit-
ness. Leaving journalism to re-enter the
army, he was a staff-officer in Maoriland,
and when W. Ilohenzollern started sending
round ultimatums he made quickly for
where the bullets were thickest. He is a
son of Rear-Admiral Hudson, but leaves
you to find this out for yourself.
■**■**
Maoriland Anzacs are t,o have their
paper, too. The tiny sheet, mostly of color-
less cables, issued to the Australians is the
Bulletin ; the Maorilanders’ little lot is to
be the much clumsier “Chronicles of the
N.Z.E.F.” The War Contingent Assoc,
will finance it and publish it once a fort-
night in London; and Clutlia MacKenzie,
the son of High Commissioner Tom, blinded
in action in Gallipoli, will be the editor.
* * * *
The Australian forces in the field were
delighted when the Commonwealth decided
to issue an official A.I.F. news sheet; but
their delight didn’t last long. The issue of
August 13, poured into Tiie Bulletin by
disgusted Bilijims, contains 36 lines (not in-
cluding the headings, of which there are 10),
and of these 36, an account of “a sporting
contest” between Governor-General Fergu-
son and Page, M.H.R., swallow 21. Getting
to the kernel of the par.:—
The Governor-General challenged the politician
to lift himself six times on the parallel-bars. The
Governor-General succeeded easily, and Mr. Page
succeeded also, though he nearly collapsed after-
wards. The Parliamentarian then challenged Sir
Ronald to a rifle-shooting match. Out of a possible
60 the Governor-General scored 42, his opponent
replying with 48.
The other items of interest are headed
“Unshipped Australian Wheat,” “Russian
Flag Day in Melbourne,” “Tasmanian Rail-
way Appointment.” Naturally, after this
dose of dullness, one expected to read:
“Cables forwarded by the Minister for
Defence.”
“Jwosp”: Mention of lady reporters re-
minds me of the one that the Lyttelton
Times (M.L.) made local history with. She
was sent to a Council meeting when the
night errant’s contract w y as down for discus-
sion. Of course the item was adjourned. Un-
fortunately the young person was present
next week, and again the matter had to be
held over. But when the third appearance
of Miss stopped discussion the evening gentle-
man threatened action, and an apologetic
deputation from the Council, headed by the
Mayor, begged the editor of the Times to
send Rosebud to the Mothers’ Union and let
them get on with their business. The joy
of the story was that the newspaper staff
was standing by with its handkerchief in
its mouth watching to see how long the
chivalrous Council would continue to ad-
journ. We had a sweep on the number of
weeks, and the chief reporter scooped it.
“Glen H.”: J. A. Raws, formerly of the
Argus, who was lately reported “Killed in
action” in France, was found lying dead
with several others, and not one of them
showed a sign of a wound. It was shell
shock that made an end of a capital press-
man and a good fellow.
* * * #
That energetic pressman, Thos. J.
McMahon, packed his fountain-pen and
steamed across the Gulf from Thursday
Island to Darwin the other day. He has
spent several months visiting the Torres
Strait islands with a view to letting the
light on to these little-known parts of the
Commonwealth. Last year he invaded what
used to be German New Guinea, tramped
over a lot of British Papua, and doggedly
tried to keep pace with the long-limbed Judge
Murray, who is Papua's champion walker, in
a journey into the precipitous inland
country. McMahon says that racing up
5000 ft. mountains is not the game it’s
cracked up to be, The Territory and the
unholy show that the Federal Government 3s
making of things there are now occupying
his attention.
TRIPE AND ROSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A dish of tripe and a bowl of roses
Set on the board. Two eager noses
That know that the hour of joy is ripe
Are bending down —to the dish of tripe.
Roses can wait, but tripe is fleeting,
And he who’d write must now be eating.
The poet will later light his pipe
And twine the roses around the tripe.
It’s tripe and roses the livelong year,
With onions added to draw a tear.
On onion beds the Muse reposes—
Roses and tripe and tripe and roses!
N.S.W.
