The Witch,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WITH witchcraft working out her spell
She wove a snare for me
And in the baleful trap I fell
Beside a summer sea.
Brown seaweed tossing on the swell
Laughed in the sunlight free.
Old was I in the game of life,
A sinner, hardened, hoar,
Keen as the blade of some long knife
A bearded reiver wore —
I had been wedded to one wife
And loved at least a score.
How should I fall in gin or snare?
How should she trick me so?
Scents of strange flowers were on the air,
The tide was creeping slow.
An old fool’s case is past repair
As all the wise men know.
Oh. hut her voice was soft and sweet,
Her hair a rain of gold,
Ten dimples on her naked feet
Were pink and round and bold ;
My heart by its erratic beat
Had too much blood to hold.
She lured me to a lonely place
Afar from friends and kin;
The wonder of her perfect face
Reproved my soul of sin ;
For it were sin and deep disgrace
Such beauty not to win.
I with the weight of forty years,
She young as sun or moon—
The hair that rippled round her ears
Was music of no tune —
Ye gods that rule a lover’s fears,
Why was I born too soon?
I heard the spell, I saw the snare,
I laughed and straightway fell
Into the .net of smiles and hair
That she had spread so well;
I took the chance and did not care
For Heaven if this were Hell.
Straight to my doom she drew me on;
I went with laughter gay.
The sun full-rayed in glory shone
And warmed the happy day.
What recked I where the world had gone—
We too were bent on play!
With bucket of green painted tin
And one small wooden spade
I toiled as navvies toil to win
A smile from that sweet maid —-
Oh, but the dimple on her chin
For all my labor paid !
N.s.w.
PAT O’MAORI.
Lieberwurst Goes to Leeward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rpHE little Papuan steamer the Lokohu,
400 tons, and. as her captain styled
hex 1 , much to the disgust of the long,
Yankee-faced, yellow-bearded engineer, one
goat power, loped at a painfully slow gait
westward along the New Guinea coast from
Moresby to Yule Island and Darn, with a
call at Nagheer; then to Thursday Island,
and thence where God or freight might send
her. She had picked up all along the coast
from Samarai—Cloudy Bay, Dedale,
Moresby, Red Scar Bay and Galley Reach —
a mixed cargo of copra, trochus shell, a few
hundred bags of copper-ore mined in the
Astrolabe countx-y ; ivory nuts, old copper
sheathing from an alleged wreck somewhere,
a few bales of sisal hemp as a trial shipment,
and a bulky and light collection of native
junk—arrows and stone axes and model
canoes. So she sat w r ell enough in the water
for the job that the South had sent her; and
as she made for the entrance to Hall Sound
she saw drifting north-westerly .a big barque
in trouble, her discolored iron hull lifting
high on the deep slow swell, so that the water
ran off her in cascades, the long weeds on
her bottom trailing, as she lifted, like gi’een
hair and spreading out in flat tresses as she
fell back in the sea again.
To the little, grizzle-beai’ded, red-faced cap-
tain, Tom Leggo, master of the Lokohu, she
became a derelict, and he reckoned the
probable profit of salvage in one glance at
her foul hull and her masts broken off
10 feet above the deck. To the engineer,
who had left the engine-room at the first
excited cry of the Papuan deckhands, she
told the same-tgle.
“Been drifting for weeks,” said the en-
gineer, stroking his yellow goatee, as if it
helped him to words.
“Aye,” replied Captain Leggo—“an’ by the
cut of her and the fat John Stofel over the
starb’d rail she’s a Dutchman. Port y’r
helium a bit.”
The fuzzy-haired Papuan threw the wheel
to port; the Lokohu turned from the en-
trance under the high sombre-wooded shore
and went westerly, under the counter of the
derelict. As the steamer passed around the
stern of the barque, a following sea lifted her
high out of the water; the long, drowmed
weeds trailed again, and they read her name
* n th e faded, sea-eaten letters on her stern.
‘Mulhausen—Bremen,” said Captain
Leggo. “She’s a German; round as an apple,
a carry-all.; and buoyant as a kiln o’ bricks
when she’s loaded. Man, in a sea she’d be
like Niagara Falls for’ard !”
“They must have lightened her after she
got dismasted, skipper.”
“Aye—or they’d been in Hell or Berlin by
now. Down to y’r engines, George—she’s
signallin’.”
George Farquhar padded down to his
s yning engine-i'oom and waited for the orders
o. the bridge-telegraph. Captain Leggo did
not need to look at the dismasted barque’s
signal to be sure of her distress; he rang his
e *4fines to half-speed as he came amidships
of the German; rang again for a few kicks
astern; manoeuvred the Lokohu to within a
cable s length of the Mulhausen; then
stopped his. engines, and drifted with the
barque, noting that the set was towards
breakers and a beach beyond them, and but
hve miles away.
“Lord knows how far she’s drifted,” said
Laptain Leggo to himself. “She’s had no
steerage way; so I suppose old Getsoontight
just kept her nose to it, and called himself
lucky when she didn’t turn round and
round.”
The German hailed him, asking for a tow.
■'•Where to?” shouted Leggo, back across
the heavy swells.
The big, heavy man with the Bismarck
moustache who stood by the port rail of the
barque yelled his reply, with the wind help-
ing it to the steamer.
•‘Anyvere ve can angkor in safedy.”
“How much will you pay?”
“Take me in somevere vere you can gome
aboard, und ve fix it.”
“Righto! I’ve got a hundred fathom of
wire hawser —what you got?”
"Zwei undert undt forty fadom andt forty
fadom of chain.”
“I’ll send my boat, and we’ll give you a
line till I can get you in smooth water.”
In the lee of the barque the steamer’s boat
was pulled alongside, the steamer helping
the weight of the hawser paid out by the
mate over the stern, steaming slowly ahead in
dread of a fouling of the propeller and ap-
proaching the barque nearer than the strict
rules of safety authorised. But it was well
and truly done —the line aboard the barque
fastened to the bollards forward, and the
steamer end of it to the bitts aft; the boat
picked up, and hauled to the davits. The
Lokohu, steaming a little north of east,
slowly brought the head of the Mulhausen
away from its dangerous course to the break-
ers and the beach. It all looked supremely
impudent—the little 400-ton steamer snatch-
ing from wreck that great, lumbering, sea-
beaten barque that was five or six times the
size of the rescuer, and the impotent iron
walls following as humbly as an elephant led
by a child.
Aboard the German they were glad as men
rescued from wreck on an unknown shore.
Her captain's gladness was, however, tinc-
tured, for he saw in safety the shadow pre-
ceding payment to the salvors; but the men
of the Lokohu were frankly jubilant. Far-
quhar, the engineer, figured out his share
with the second engineer; Captain Leggo
called the mate, and told him of a few im-
possible things he intended to do with the
money. Even the Papuan deck-hands were
glad, though experience might have told
them that abundantly little would be their
sbai’e. As the steamer towed the salve out
of danger the amount of the salvage in-
creased in the mind of Captain Leggo, who
. began to think himself unbusinesslike in be-
ginning the tow without a fixed reward; and.
as the barque moved to safety, the master of
her began to mentally discount the value of
the service of Captain Leggo and the Lokohu.
But, before he could fix any plan for the
depreciation of the amount of salvage. Cap-
tain Leggo had made his own way of fixing
towage at a certainty.
The Lokohu had made close into the east-
ern shore, towing the Mulhausen into the
channel that led into the bottle neck of the
Narrows and opened out again to the spa-
ciousness of the Sound. The German cap-
tain did not like his position, and every
second made him like it still less, lie looked
ahead at the little steamer, whose engines
had been described by her own engineer as
one goat power, and at the sag of the
cable towing a ship five times the weight of
the steamer, and then he saw the bottle-neck
closing in, and the white curves of broken
water. He ran to the forecastle head, or if he
didn’t run he moved at a very swift walk ; and
from the head of her he hailed Captain
Leggo. That master mariner did not seem
to hear; but he winked for his own amuse-
ment, and ordered the helmsman: “Starb’d
little-feller one.”
The German shrieked at Captain Leggo’s
back that he did not like his position at all;
he yelled even more loudly that he preferred
to be towed direct to Brisbane, or Hell.
Then Captain Leggo deigned to hear him;
and, making a megaphone of his hands, re-
plied with a statement of the impossibility of
turning in the Narrows, and his fixed belief
as a sailor that he must go on.
In further explanation the German used
all the German ixrofanity he could remember,
and eked it out with the curses of Leith and
the Tyne; he insisted on the Lokohu turning
her nose to sea; and. as she remained obsti-
nately headed for the Sound, and the broken
water opened out narrower ahead, he at last
ordered the Lokoliu’s line to be cast off, and
let the port anchor go. Then Captain Leggo
grinned to himself, and hummed his one
song—a dolorous thing belonging to the dead
past; sang it while the Mulliausen’s anchor
chain crashed down noisily to the depths:
“Please g-ive me a penny. Sir,
My mother dear is dead
Port y’r helium—hard a port, boy:
And, oh, I am so hungry, Sir,
A penny, please, for bread ;
We were not always paupers
I’ll make that squarehead buy me cake and
starb’d, my lad—port a little —keep her head
like that.”
The Lokohu came.round, and sidled up to
the portside of the Mulhausen, like a little
dog making friends with a big one. Captain
Leggo stuck a cheroot in his portside jaw,
and looked up quizzically at the master of the
German.
“What’s the row?” asked Captain Leggo,
pleasantly.
“Der row is that my ship is as nearly
wreck-ed.”
“Wait a minute ! I’ll come aboard ! For-
’ard there —throw a line aboard of her.
Chuck a line aft, and fenders out fore and
aft.”
The little steamer and the big ship came
together, rising and falling on the light sea
against the fenders. The German dropped
his pilot ladder over-side, and Captain Leggo
climbed it to the deck of the Mulhausen, and
shook hands with her captain.
In the cabin of the Mulhausen the German
captain told Leggo that his name was
Wesclie. and that the Mulhausen had been
dismasted in a hurricane off New Caledonia.
She had drifted eleven hundred and fifty
miles in ninety-seven days; and Captain
Leggo, taking in this information with a
proper appearance of sympathy, reappraised
the salvage. By now the Mulhausen must be
short of supplies and water. He came out of
his mental arithmetic to hear Captain
. Wesche still on the subject of the drift.
“Past der Louisiades ve drift up to der
New Kinny goast sdill vestwards; und here

Premen.... now so far nort’, and a fair vind

For how much vill you tow us?”
It was a long argument with whisky in it
whisky brought from the Lokohu, the beer-
drinking Mulhausen being long out of beer.
Finally, the Australian and the German
agreed that the Lokohu should proceed to
Hall Sound, and do her business, the Mul-
hausen remaining anchored until her return;
the Lokohu to receive £l5OO for salvage and
£2OOO for towage payable at Singapore. Cap-
tain Leggo, as more than half-owner of the
Lokohu, regarded almost all the money as
already in his pocket. He went back gaily
to his ship, cast off from the Mulhausen, rang
his engines to duty, and ran away through
the broken water of the Narrows, singing
more cheerfully than before :
Please give me a penny, Sir,
My mother dear is dead.
He returned within 48 hours, and won
Captain Wesche’s heart and the goodwill of
the crew of the Mulhausen with gifts of
island truck —green cocoanuts, pawpaws and
bananas, taro and yams, wallaby and game.
And then they set to breaking out the Mul-
hausen’s anchor by the power of a man-
worked capstan. It took them nearly a day
to get it apeak, the long chain walking home
only a link to the minute.
And so, with a fair wind and fresh seas,
fhe Lokohu got the Mulhausen up to Singa-
pore ; and Captain Leggo, his mouth water-
ing for the dollars, went aboard to the
anchored German and found him not alone.
With him was a round-faced young German,
with Kaiser moustaches, and very alert eyes.
He was speaking earnestly to Captain
Wesche; and, to Captain Leggo,' he seemed
to be laying down the law. Their conversa-
tion was so important that they asked Cap-
tain Leggo to remain without the cabin for a
few minutes; and Captain Leggo, under-
standing not a word of German, still under-
stood from the tones that Captain Wesche
was first incredulous of the advice, and then
unwilling to act on it, and that the insist-
ence and authority of the alert young man
increased with the minutes. Finally, Cap-
tain Wesche sighed deeply, said “Aeh 1” many
times as if in deep disgust, and sighed
assent. The young man then came from the
cabin, bowed slightly to Captain Leggo, and
mounted the companion with an excited alac-
rity that seemed not to belong to a merely
mercantile mariner.
Then Captain Leggo entered Wesche’s
cabin, and was received almost affection-
ately; for the German —a good seaman him-
self—had recognised qualities in the Aus-
tralian which he could not help admiring,
while rueful of the stinging charges for sal-
vage and towage. Captain Leggo had a
whisky and then listened to the surprise of
his life.
“Gaptin Leckko,” said Captain Wesche,
“I owe you drei tousand funf undert boundts
for salvink undt towink.”
“That’s so, Captain.”
“I gannot bay that.”
“What! you make a contract, and tell me
at the end of it, when the work’s done, and
the coal’s burned, and I had to may 455. a
ton for it at Thursday and sixty bob a ton
at Port Darwin —and you’ve got the gall
to tell me you can’t pay!”
“Gaptin ! Gaptin ! Lizden to me.”
“No fear. . . .I’ll seize the ship.”
“It is dot I vant you to do, Gaptin.”
“Want me to seize her? . I don’t under-
stand !”
“Not siece—but dake. I haf the right to
zell the ship—l zell her to you.”
“But why? What for! She’s a fine, big
iron barque, and you’ll sell her for a paltry
three thousand five hundred?”
“I zell her. . . .for four sousand five undert
boundts —to pay off my grew and vill dake
me avay.”
“But why?”
“Vat is vy, if I show you I gan zell?”
“That’s 50... . show me that; and I can
get the money ashore, and tow her back to
Sydney... .But why do it? Giving me a
pot-full of money?”
“Gaptin Lecko, take my vordt I gan do
no more mit der Mulhausen ; and if there is
a man I should like to see make der money,
if I gannot make it meinself, it is you,
Gaptin Leckko. You charge me like a Yew
of Frankfurt, but I like you. Give me ein
sousand boundts, and dake der ship—is it
so?”
“Lead me to it.” said Captain Leggo, and
they shook hands.
The German sold and discharged her
cargo, and then the Lokohu took her over
as she was.
At the Bank of Australia, India and China
—a bank which never saw Australia, by the
way—Captain Wesche made good his right
and title to sell the Mulhausen. The Aus-
tralian captain gave his receipt for £3500,
and his order on Australia for one thou-
sand pounds, and the exchange thereon;
and the Mulhausen was turned over to the
mate of the Lokohu. The Lokohu lent its
Australian flap- to the Botany Bay, late the
Mulhausen, of Bremen; the port authorities
recognised the sale and gave Captain Leggo
his clearance. They put eight hundred tons
of ballast into her; and then Captain Leggo
invited his mate and engineer, and Captain
Wesche, and the lately-discharged mate of
the lately-renamed Mulhausen; and they
went forth for the evening.
It was a joyful evening—a dinner to
make sailormen forget the sea; many whisky
pegs while swapping yarns stretched luxu-
riously in the long chairs with the leg-rests
known as “Bombay flirters” ; rickshaws in
the dappled moonlight under the trees of
the quiet avenues; yet more pegs as the night
waned to dawn; hand clasps, and friendli-
ness, and farewells, as of men who under-
stand one another, from the Germans on
the beach to the Australians in the boat
bound for the anchored Lokohu.
At noon next day, as they prepared for
sea, Captain Leggo was hailed from an
outward-bound steamer. Answering the
hail, the Australian saw Captain Wesche
on the deck of the outward bound waving
his hat, and crying: “Gut bye und gut luck !”
Standing by him were his late mate and the
alert-eyed, round-faced young man with the
Kaiser moustaches.
“The Manila boat,” said Captain Leggo.
“Well! good luck to him —lie’s done me a
good turn, God knows why.”
It was a nightmare of towage. From the
Flores Sea to the Arafura, and back to the
shelter of Goode Island, they towed that big
solid block of 3000 tons capacity, built for
cargo, and too short, deep and broad for
her length. Her SOO tons of ballast was
only a mouthful for her in the seas running;
the Lokohu towed, for ever and ever, it
seemed, in the teeth of the sou’-easter. For
two days they towed at three knots an hour
in the fear of snapping the tow line; and
Captain Leggo never once sang ‘‘Please give
me a penny, Sir,” although twice, as if in
palliation of his silence, he went aft in the
Lokohu, and addressed the late Mulhausen,
once of Bremen, as “a big, ugly cow.”
“The big ugly cow” shipped seas all over
her, and rose again shaking the water off
her like a retriever regaining the land.
They had out two hundred and forty
fathoms of four-and-a-quarter-inch wire
hawser, and the late Mulhausen’s own thirty
fathoms of chain. Captain Leggo, driven to
exaggeration, described the sag as sometimes
fourteen hundred and forty feet, and, when
the Lokohu ran down one sea and the
barque lifted on another, it seemed certain
that the barque intended to come aboard the
steamer and be sociable.
Captain Leggo comforted himself with his
own tremendous optimism.
“It’s all right,” he said to Farquhar, the
billygoat-bearded engineer; “after the
straits we’re good and easy inside the reef
down to Broadmount; and then there’s the
three-knot current going south to help us
into dock.”
So at long last by trying always, which is
the way the Greeks came to Troy, the
Lokohu towed the barque through Torres
Strait, into the quiet water behind the
Barrier against the current, and down to
Broadmount, where they found the other
half of that current from the Pacific ready
to bear them south—even, if they wanted,
to Gabo, where the current leaves the coast
again and goes eastward home to the Pacific
depths where it was born.
At Sydney they learned the quick, deci-
sive methods of Captain Wesche and the
alert-eyed young man of the Kaiser mous-
taches. The papers that came aboard with
the pilot told them all —Germany at war
with the world! The alert young man.
with the Kaiser moustache had told ail that.
“He knew refitting would take a month,
and that the barque would be locked in.
but he did me a good turn, anyhow,” said
Captain Leggo. “I hope he got through.
.... She’s a good ship.”
“Please give me a penny, Sir, My mother
dear is dead,” sang Captain Leggo, as the
late Mulhausen’s anchor chain crashed to
the sea floor off Balmain.
RANDOLPH BEFORD.
The Corroboree.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE moon looks like a boomerang, that
might be circling among the birds
whose souls have wandered forth to
the stars; pale as old silver its light floats
down, touching the leaves, and making more
weird the fantastic shapes that steal through
the forest. A curlew cries, there is the hoot
of an owl, and a flying-fox passes with a
flapping of wings ; but added to these, the
natural sounds that awake in the night, is a
strange tum-tumming. Around camp-fires
that glow like beacons, where deep forest-
aisles open on to a clearing, women beat with,
their hands the stretched skins of opossums.
It is the call to the tribe to come to corrob-
oree; while nearer, nearer, between the great
trunks of giant cedars and the twisted ropes
of the lawyer-vines, draw those in whom all
the interest centres —the players of the play ;
the stars of the crude aboriginal stage;
the tribe’s warriors.
Now a chorus is added. “Mo-la-ali wingiii
clah,” the voices commence, and words that
tell how the hunters set out when the dawn
was red and the dew was on the grass, are
wedded to sounds that the singers have
learned from Nature’s own music. They
describe at length the glories of the hunt, in
which they themselves have taken no part;
for it is more seemly that woman, whose
state is low in the scale of things, should sit
at the camp-fire, and wait, wait.
This is the signal. The men burst forth,
six-score of them, their bodies covered with
grotesque markings, yellow and white and
the favored blood-fed, the clay of the creeks
having furnished the coloring. Round each
middle is the fur of an opossum, or a girdle
of human hair. The bone through the nose,
adorned at the ends with the yellow of the
white cockatoo’s crest-feathers, renders more
weird the ebon faces, touched here and there
with a phosphorescent fungus. They are
hunters, but they might be devils !
Each man carries a trophy of the hunt—
live or six opossums, a porcupine; or, slung
across his shoulders, the sinewy length of a
carpet snake. In addition to these, liis wog-
gera (a tomahawk), newry (a vine), dilly
bag, boomerangs and paddy-melon sticks.
Now the central fire is lighted to receive
the feast.
An iguana is scented. It is a stuffed
iguana, tied tp a pole that stands in
the midst of the circling fires. The
actors see it, but, having the success of
the play at heart, before tracking it they
search for its trail. Now they are hot, now
cold, like children who play at search-for-
the-thimble. At length they come upon it.
and there rises the yell, “Yi-yee!” awakening
the birds, scaring the wild creatures and roll-
ing away till it dies in the dark forest.
The trail is followed up to the pole, and
the dances begin, every movement having its
significance. It is a play without words, a
story-book written in the language of
motion.
Grey dawn looks on the scattered remains
of a feast, and among all those that lie
huddled in sleep is one who has tasted
glorious success. It is Woyjen, known to
the whites as “Gamira Paddy.”
What more can man desire? He has
given sons and daughters to the race. Now
he is the author of a corroboree! North
and south and east and west his work will
go; and when at length he wanders forth
to the stars, he will sit in the place reserved
for the Immortals!
